<HI>BHAGAVAD GI1TA1.
<NI>1) {%General.%} The Bhagavad Gi1ta1 is a poem consisting of
<>650 verses divided into eighteen chapters. The Gi1ta1
<>covers chapters 25-45 in the Bhi1s2ma Parva of the Maha1-
<>bha1rata, and it is in the form of a talk or discussion
<>between Arjuna and Lord Kr2s2n2a. The mighty armies
<>of the Pa1n2d2avas and the Kauravas were arrayed on
<>opposite sides for mortal combat on the field of Kuru-
<>ks2etra when Arjuna, overcome by grief at the prospect
<>of fathers, brothers, preceptors and other Kinsmen
<>fighting and killing one another expressed to his
<>charioteer, S4ri1 Kr2s2n2a his aversion to fighting. But, the
<>Lord pointed out to the unwilling Arjuna, by unique
<>and various arguments, his imperative duty, under the
<>circumstance, to fight and fight in heroic earnestness with
<>the result that Arjuna shed his disinclination to fight and
<>entered the fray, which ended in the ultimate victory
<>of the Pa1n2d2avas. And the dialogue between Arjuna
<>and Kr2s2n2a, and especially the great teaching of Kr2s2n2a
<>on the field form the theme of the Gi1ta1. The Gi1ta1
<>contains three spheres or fields of teaching; the karma
<>yoga (philosophy of action), jn5a1na yoga (philosophy of
<>knowledge) and Bhaktiyoga (philosophy of devotion).
<>The three yogas are treated each in six chapters.
<NI>The theme of the Gi1ta1 is philosophy, and it is revered
<>as one of the most sublime philosophical texts of the
<>Hindu religion. Many a great thinker and philosopher
<>like A1ca1rya S4an3kara, Ra1ma1nuja1ca1rya and Madhva1-
<>ca1rya have annotated, and written commentaries on
<>the Gi1ta1. There is a school of thought which believes
<>that the Gi1ta1 was taught to Arjuna by Kr2s2n2a himself
<>on the battle-field in something like tabloid form and
<>that Vya1sa eleborated the mighty teaching in its present
<>form. The poet, Ba1n2a, who flourished in the 7th
<>century A.D., and the great S4an3kara, who lived in the
<>8th century believed that the Gi1ta1 was sung by Kr2s2n2a
<>himself. But there are some modern thinkers, who
<>argue that the Gi1ta1 was composed some time between
<>the third and fourth centuries B.C. and it was inter-
<>polated into the Maha1bha1rata in the second century
<>A.D.
<NI>The Gi1ta1 has translations in all the languages of the
<>world. The whole world has acclaimed it as a very [Page113-a+ 65]
<>weighty and valuable contribution in the sphere of
<>philosophy.
<NI>2) {%Theme of the Gi1ta1.%} It has been mentioned above that
<>the Gi1ta1 forms chapters 25-45 in the Bhi1s2ma Parva of
<>the Maha1bha1rata. The subject-matter of each chapter
<>of the Gi1ta1 is given below.
<NI>{%Chapter%} 25. {%Arjunavis2a1da yoga :%}--Both the armies take
<>positions in Kuruks2etra. At the sound of the conches
<>Arjuna becomes dejected and sorrowful at the prospect
<>of killing relations, preceptors and Kinsmen.
<NI>{%Chapter%} 26. {%Sa1m3khya yoga :%}--The greatness and majesty
<>of the Sa1m3khya and the Karma yogas.
<NI>{%Chapter%} 27. {%Karma yoga :%}--The need for action according
<>to the Jn5a1na and the Karma yogas.
<NI>{%Chapter%} 28. {%Jn5a1nakarma Vibha1ga yoga :%}--Power of Sagun2a
<>Brahma (Brahma with attributes), Nis2ka1ma-karma
<>yoga (Action without an eye on the result) spiritual
<>greatness of various yajn5as.
<NI>{%Chapter%} 29. {%Sannya1sa yoga :%}--Sa1m3khya yoga, Nis2ka1ma-
<>karma yoga, Jn5a1na yoga, Jn5a1na yoga with Bhakti.
<NI>{%Chapter%} 30. {%A1tmasam3yama yoga :%}--Nis2ka1makarma yoga,
<>A1tmodha1ran2a, Jn5a1nayoga.
<NI>{%Chapter%} 31. {%Jn5a1na yoga :%}--Jn5a1navijn5a1nas, origin of the
<>world, the Daiva and A1sura aspects of I1s4vara, worship
<>of other Devas.
<NI>{%Chapter%} 32. {%Ta1rakabrahma yoga :%}--Seven questions of
<>Arjuna about Brahma, A1tmatatva and Karma. S4ri1
<>Kr2s2n2a's answers thereto. Bhakti yoga, the S4ukla and
<>the Kr2s2n2a ma1rgas.
<NI>{%Chapter%} 33. {%Ra1jara1ja guhya yoga :%}--Jn5a1na and Vijn5a1na,
<>origin of the world, I1s4varasvaru1pa, Saka1manis2ka1mo-
<>pa1sana, Bhagavadbhakti.
<NI>{%Chapter%} 34. {%Vibhu1ti yoga :%}--Bhagava1n's Vibhu1ti (Divine
<>attributes), Bhakti yoga.
<NI>{%Chapter%} 35. {%Vis4varu1padars4ana yoga :%}--Arjuna's prayer
<>to be shown Vis4varu1pa (cosmic form), description of
<>vis4varu1pa by S4ri1 Kr2s2n2a and San5jaya, S4ri1 Kr2s2n2a reveals
<>vis4varu1pa to Arjuna; fear-stricken at the sight, Arjuna
<>sings the praise of Kr2s2n2a.
<NI>{%Chapter%} 36. {%Bhaktiyoga :%}--The great benefits of wor-
<>shipping God with form and without form.
<NI>{%Chapter%} 37. {%Ks2etraks2etrajn5a Vibha1ga yoga :%}--Description
<>of Ks2etraks2etrajn5a with Jn5a1na, and of Prakr2ti and
<>Purus2a.
<NI>{%Chapter%} 38. {%Gun2atrayavibha1ga yoga :%}--The greatness of
<>Jn5a1na; world's origin from Prakr2ti and Purus2a, the
<>three gun2as, sattva, rajas and tamas; means to attain
<>god; marks of Yuga1ti1tapurus2a.
<NI>{%Chapter%} 39. {%Purus2ottama yoga :%}--Essential principles of
<>world and life, attainment of God, relationship bet-
<>ween Ji1va1tma1 and Parama1tma1, principle and theory of
<>ks2ara purus2a and aks2ara purus2a.
<NI>{%Chapter%} 40. {%Daiva1sura sampadvibha1ga yoga :%}--Good ac-
<>tions, evil deeds, scientific actions and unscientific
<>actions.
<NI>{%Chapter%} 41. {%S4raddha1trayavibha1ga yoga :%}--Descriptions of
<>Scientific tapas, diet, yajn5atapas and da1na. Interpre-
<>tation of Om.
<NI>{%Chapter%} 42. {%Moks2asannya1sa yoga :%}--Tya1gam, Sa1m3khya
<>theory, Varn2adharmas, jn5a1nanis2t2ha1, Nis2ka1makarmayoga
<>with bhakti and the greatness of the Gi1ta1.
<NI>(For another story about the greatness of the Gi1ta1
<>see Dus4s4a1sana II).
<HI>BHAGAVATI1. The words Bhagava1n and Bhagavati1
<>mean Parama1tma1 (universal self) and Prakr2ti (Nature [Page113-b+ 65]
<>and its modifications) respectively. Prakr2ti is also
<>called by the name S4akti. The following elucidation
<>once given by Maha1vis2n2u about Bhagava1n and Bhaga-
<>vati1 is greatly illuminating.
<NI>Time, space, atmosphere and the universe (Brah-
<>ma1n2d2am) are, just like Parama1tma1, eternal. This is the
<>truth and reality. Below this eternal Gokula exists
<>Vaikun2t2haloka, which also is, like the former, eternal.
<>Just like this, Prakr2ti, which is a sport to Brahma1 and
<>is also without beginning or end (Sana1tani1) too is
<>eternal. In the same manner as flame exists in fire in-
<>separable from it, moonlight in moon, beauty in the
<>lotus flower and brightness in the sun, so does nature
<>exist in soul inseparable therefrom. In the same way
<>as the goldsmith cannot make gold ornaments without
<>gold and the potter cannot make pots without clay, the
<>Parama1tma1 will not in the least be able to function
<>unaided by Prakr2ti. Prakr2ti (Nature, Devi1) is all power-
<>ful. ‘Para’ becomes powerful enough to do everything
<>when he joins the Devi1.
<NI>The sound ‘S4a’ means welfare and good fortune, and
<>the sound ‘kti’ means prowess. Hence “S4akti” means
<>the embodiment of welfare and prowess or she, who is
<>the giver of welfare and prowess. Bhagavati1 combines
<>in herself knowledge, affluence, riches, fame and strength.
<>As the Parama1tma1 is always with and inseparable from
<>such Bhagavati1 he is called Bhagava1n also. When
<>Prakr2ti and Parama1tma1 remain combined it is called
<>Parabrahma, which possesses neither form nor attri-
<>butes. And, when Prakr2ti and Purus2a separate, of their
<>own accord, they assume forms and attributes.
<NI>The above is S4aiva doctrine in a nut-shell. The
<>Vais2n2avas do not accept this position. They ask, “How
<>is it possible to have brightness or effulgence without
<>there being an effulgent one?” Therefore, the Vais2n2avas
<>believe in the existence, at the centre of an effulgent
<>sphere, of a thing possessing the utmost effulgence and
<>brightness equal to that of Brahma1. This ‘thing’--
<>Deva--is very efficient and effective to remedy sorrows
<>due to birth, death, disease etc. and to him the life-
<>time of Brahma is just one minute only. This Deva is
<>called Parama1tma1, Parabrahma and Kr2s2n2a by the
<>Vais2n2avas. ‘Kr2s2’ means maximum devotion (love)
<>towards Parama1tma1, and ‘n2a’ means he who becomes
<>slave to such devotion. Hence Kr2s2n2a means he who
<>becomes a slave to the love of his devotees. There is
<>another meaning also for the word Kr2s2n2a. Kr2s2 means
<>all and ‘n2a’ means seed or root; and thus Kr2s2n2a means
<>he, who is the root of everything. In the very begin-
<>ning there was only this Kr2s2n2a; and this Lord, sub-
<>ject only to his own will and pleasure, divided him-
<>self into two, the left side becoming woman and the
<>right side man. (Devi1 Bha1gavata, Navama Skandha).
<HI>BHAGINI. A word used in addressing women. Verse
<>129 in chapter two of the Manusmr2ti lays down that
<>the wife of another person and women who are not
<>one's relatives should be addressed either as Bhavati,
<>Subhage or Bhagini.
<HI>BHAGI1RATHA.
<NI>1) {%Genealogy.%} Descended from Vis2n2u thus: Brahma1-
<>Mari1ci-Kas4yapa-Vivasva1n-Vaivasvata manu-Iks2va1ku-
<>Vikuks2i-S4as4a1da-Kakutstha-Anenas-Pr2thula1s4va-Prasena-
<>jit-Yuvana1s4va-Ma1ndha1ta1-Purukutsa-Trasadasyu-
<>Anaran2ya-Haryas4va-Vasumanas-Sudhanva1-Traiyya1-
<>run2a-Satyavrata or Tris4an3ku-Haris4candra-Rohita1s4va- [Page114-a+ 64]
<>Harita-Cun5cu-Sudeva-Bharuka-Ba1huka-Sagara-
<>Asaman5jas-Am3s4uma1n
<F>*) According to verse 12, Chapter 25, of Vana Parva, Bhagi1ratha is the son of Dili1pa. But most of the Pura1n2as refer to him a
<>he son of Am3s4uma1n.</F>-Bhagi1ratha.
<NI>2) {%Recluse.%} Many years had not lapsed after his coro-
<>nation as King when, in the heart of Bhagi1ratha spiri-
<>tual thoughts began to crop up. A great sage called
<>Tritula was his guru. Once Bhagi1ratha asked the guru
<>what was the means to get rid of worldiy sorrows, and
<>the guru replied as follows:--
<NI>The ego will be annihilated when the tendencies and
<>cravings towards objects of the senses are absolutely
<>suppressed and the whole and perfect truth is realised
<>through knowledge, the practice of concentration etc.
<>The ego will not disappear as long as one does not
<>become one's real self by completely overcoming the
<>sense of pride, shame etc. which form the outer case
<>of worldly life. The egoless state is the ultimate
<>achievement and most supreme state. Oh! Bhagi1ratha!
<>if you would get rid of sentiments like pride etc., give
<>up all attachment to material objects, become fearless
<>and gift away, with thoughts concentrated on the inner
<>self, all your wealth to enemies, and then move among
<>those enemies, without any sense of ego and pride
<>about this material body, and take alms from them
<>(enemies) and also give up me, who am your preceptor
<>in the matter of knowledge; then you will become the
<>most sublime Brahma”. This advice of the guru
<>affected him so much that Bhagi1ratha, holding his
<>duties firmly in mind, engaged himself in spiritual
<>practices, and after spending some time thus he per-
<>formed, according to rules, the Agnis2t2oma yajn5a aimed
<>at gifting away everything. All wealth like cows, land,
<>horses, gold etc. were distributed in gifts to noble
<>brahmins and the poor folk according to their eligibi-
<>lity for the same. Within three days he had gifted away
<>everything except the clothes he was wearing. And,
<>then he invited his neighbouring enemy king and gave
<>to him, without the least hesitation, the kingdom which
<>had been bereft of all wealth. The ministers and other
<>citizens felt very sorry about the whole thing. But, Bha-
<>gi1ratha left the country at once for other places having
<>with him only the clothes he was wearing. He spent his
<>days in various places and forests where he was not
<>known even by name. Ere long, Bhagi1ratha attained
<>ultimate spiritual solace. And, then he accidentally
<>came to his former kingdom, which was then being
<>ruled by the enemy King. The ministers and other
<>citizens to whose houses he went begging for alms
<>recognised him, and with sorrow unbearable they
<>appealed to him to accept the throne again and rule
<>the country. Bhagi1ratha rejected their request, and,
<>after staying there for some time, he started for other
<>places. During this wandering of his he met his old
<>preceptor, Tritula, and both of them, in company,
<>toured for some time cities and forests. They felt it very
<>painful to keep on to their body like that. They
<>thought like this: “Why should the body be kept like
<>this. What if this material object continues to exist
<>or perishes? But let it (the body) continue as long as
<>it exists without in any way being against the order
<>of things and ethical practices”. And, in this frame of
<>mind they traversed the forests. Now, the minister of
<>a distant kingdom who was on the look out for a [Page114-b+ 61]
<>successor to the King who had died heirless, persuaded
<>Bhagi1ratha to accept the Kingship of that country. The
<>ministers of Bhagi1ratha's former Kingdom also now
<>requested him to resume his old kingship, especially
<>since its new ruler had already expired. Bhagi1ratha
<>obliged them, and became once again King of his own
<>country. (Jn5a1nava1sis2t2ham).
<NI>3) {%Bhagi1rathaprayatnam. (Hima1layan or Herculean effort).%}
<>Sagara, an old predecessor of Bhagi1ratha had two
<>wives called Kes4ini1 and Sumati. Kes4ini1 had one son
<>named Asaman5jas and Sumati 60,000 sons. Sagara
<>once conducted an As4vamedha yajn5a in the Indo-
<>Gangetic plane, when Indra stole away the sacrificial
<>horse and kept it quite near to sage Kapila who was
<>doing tapas in Pa1ta1la. The 60,000 sons of Sagara set
<>out in search of the horse and found it out in Pa1ta1la.
<>At the sight of the horse they shouted themselves
<>hoarse. Enraged at this sage Kapila reduced those sons
<>of Sagara to ashes in the fire which emanated from his
<>eyes.
<NI>After entrusting Asaman5jas with the duty of perform-
<>ing the funeral rites of his 60,000 sons Sagara expired.
<>Asaman5jas transferred that duty on to Am3s4uma1n, and
<>he to Bhagi1ratha. Bhagi1ratha did penance on the sea-
<>shore concentrating his mind on Gan3ga1devi1. The Devi1
<>appeared before Bhagi1ratha and asked him to choose
<>what boon he would, and he requested the Devi1 to per-
<>form the funeral rites of 60,000 sons of Sagara remain-
<>ing in the form of ashes in Pa1ta1la. To this Gan3ga1devi1
<>replied that the earth will not be able to withstand
<>the impact of her powerful flow, but she shall, if S4iva
<>permits, flow into his matted hair. And, Gan3ga1devi1
<>asked Bhagi1ratha to first get that permission. This did
<>not dishearten Bhagi1ratha, who went to mount Kaila1sa
<>to do penance so that S4iva might grant him his prayer.
<>He thus did penance for 1000 years. S4iva appeared to
<>him and agreed to receive the rushing flow of Gan3ga1
<>water on his matted head. And, accordingly S4iva stood
<>in position to receive the rushing waters of Gan3ga1, and
<>Gan3ga1 flowed on to his head. Even the most powerful
<>flow of Gan3ga1 water on his head did not cause S4iva to
<>move from his position even by a hair's breadth. This
<>awakened the conceit in Gan3ga1devi1, understanding
<>which S4iva contained her on his head. Without finding
<>any outlet the waters of river Gan3ga1 flowed along the
<>matted hairs of S4iva for thousand years. So, Bhagi-
<>ratha had once again to please S4iva. Thus pleased
<>again S4iva shook his matted head and one drop of
<>water fell on the ground, and that is the river Ganges
<>in North India. The Gan3ga1 flowed along plane ground
<>to Pa1ta1la and performed the funeral rites of Sagara's
<>sons. (M.B. Vana Parva, Chapter 108; Va1lmi1ki
<>Ra1ma1yan2a, Ba1la Ka1n2d2a, Canto 42; Brahma1n2d2a
<>pura1n2a, Chapter 97; Bha1gavata, Navama Skandha,
<>Kampa Ra1ma1yan2a (Tamil), Yuddha Ka1n2d2a; Padma
<>Pura1n2a, Part 4, Chapter 21).
<NI>4) {%Other Information.%}
<NI>(1) Bhagi1ratha is a member of Yama's assembly and
<>serves him. (M.B., Sabha1 Parva, Chapter 8, Verse 11).
<NI>(2) S4iva bestowed boons on him. (Vana Parva, Chapter
<>180, Verse 1). [Page115-a+ 65]
<NI>(3) Bhagi1ratha had faith in the great efficacy of making
<>a gift of cows. (Anus4a1sana Parva, Chapter 76, Verse
<>25).
<NI>(4) He married his daughter to Kautsa. (Anus4a1sana
<>Parva, Chapter 131, Verse 25).
<NI>(5) Once Bhagi1ratha made a gift of one lakh of cows
<>with calves to Mahars2i Kohala, and attained Uttama-
<>loka. (Anus4a1sana Parva, Chapter 137, Verse 27).

<HI>BHAIRAVA I. A na1ga belonging to the Dhr2tara1s2t2ra
<>Vam3s4a. There is a reference to this na1ga in Maha1-

<HI>BHAIRAVA II. A Pa1rs2ada of S4iva.
<NI>1) {%General.%} Immediately after his birth, this terrible
<>monster subdued all the Devas without getting the
<>permission of S4iva. Enraged at it, S4iva turned him into
<>a tree by a curse. But S4iva was soon pacified and lifted
<>the curse by declaring that those who offered pu1ja1 to
<>the gods would get the full reward for their worship
<>only if they offered pu1ja1 to Bhairava also.
<NI>Bhairava became a tree as soon as he was cursed. He
<>became a tree because he subdued the gods. Therefore
<>that tree came to be called “DAMANAKA” tree
<>(Damana = Subdue). It has another name. “TA1TIRI”
<>tree. Even now, pious people offer pu1ja1 to this tree.
<>(Agni Pura1n2a, Chapters 52-80).
<NI>2) {%Birth.%} Once Brahma1 and Vis2n2u became swollen
<>with pride. In his haughty way, Brahma1 insulted S4iva.
<>Under the insult, S4iva was infuriated and out of the
<>fire of his fury was born Bhairava with his black form.
<>(Lin3ga Pura1n2a 1.90).
<NI>3) {%Brahmahatya1.%} As soon as he was born, Bhairava
<>rushed towards Brahma1 and pinched off his fifth head
<>which had insulted S4iva. By this, he incurred the sin
<>of Brahmahatya1. For the expiation of this sin, as
<>ordered by S4iva, Bhairava started on a journey to go
<>abegging, with Brahma1's severed head in his hand.
<>S4iva then created a woman named “Brahmahatya1”
<>and asked her to follow Bhairava.
<NI>Although Bhairava visited many holy places, he was
<>not able to liberate himself from the sin of Brahma-
<>hatya1. At last, at the suggestion of S4iva, he went to
<>Va1ra1n2asi1 and washed off his sin. Brahma1's head which
<>he carried, was also deposited there and the place be-
<>came famous under the name of “Kapa1lamocana
<>ti1rtha”. (S4iva Pura1n2a, S4atarudra Sam3hita1).
<NI>4) {%Family.%} In Ka1lika1 Pura1n2a there is an elaborate
<>account of the family of Bhairava. According to this
<>Pura1n2a, Vijaya, the reputed King of Va1ra1n2asi1 was born
<>in the family of Bhairava. Ka1lika1 Pura1n2a also says
<>that Vijaya destroyed the Kha1n2d2avi1 city and set up
<>the Kha1n2d2ava forest in its place.
<NI>According to Ka1lika1 Pura1n2a, Bhairava and Veta1la
<>were, in their previous births, two S4ivapa1rs2adas named
<>Maha1ka1la and Bhr2n3gi. It was by the curse of Pa1rvati1. [Page115-b+ 64]
<>that they were born as mortals in their next birth.
<>(Ka1lika1 Pura1n2a 53).
<NI>The Pura1n2as mention eight Bhairavas called Asita1m3ga,
<>Ruru, Can2d2a, Krodha, Unmatta, Kapa1li, Bhi1s2an2a and
<>Sam3ha1ra.
<NI>5) {%Shape.%} Bhairava has a terrible shape. He wears the
<>crescent moon and plaited hair on his head. His wea-
<>pons are, sword, arrow, dagger, bow, trident, rope
<>etc. At times he is dressed in elephant-skin. He has
<>five faces. Serpents are his ornaments. (Agni Pura1n2a,
<>Chapters 52-80).
<HI>BHAIRAVI1. One of the eight Ambas. They are:

<HI>BHAJAMA1NA. A Ya1dava King, whose father was Sat-

<HI>BHAJYA. An a1ca1rya in the line of the R2k disciples of

<HI>BHAKS2AKA. A S4u1dra sinner. In Padma Pura1n2a the
<>following story is related about this S4u1dra.
<NI>Bhaks2aka who was once going aimlessly somewhere
<>felt extremely thirsty, and quenched his thirst by
<>drinking water which was found near the small plat-
<>form raised for the holy Tulasi1 shrub. The sanctity of
<>the water thus drunk washed away all the sin attached
<>to him. Some time later he was killed by a forest-
<>dweller, and he attained svargaloka (heaven). Bhaks2aka,
<>in his previous birth, was a king who led a voluptuous
<>life, and he, once, robbed a very beautiful woman of
<>her chastity, and it was as a punishment for the sin
<>that the King had to be born, in the next birth, in the
<>womb of a S4u1dra woman. (Padma Pura1n2a, Brahma
<>Ka1n2d2a, Chapter 22).
<HI>BHAKTI. A Devi1 born in Dra1vid2a des4a (Southern
<>India). Once Devi1, along with her two sons, Jn5a1na
<>and Vaira1gya, started on a walking tour to Gokula and
<>Vr2nda1vana via Karn2a1t2aka, Maha1ra1s2t2ra and Gurjara
<>(Gujarat). During the long tour the mother and her
<>sons became aged. But, as soon as they set foot on
<>Gokula and Vr2nda1vana old age quitted Bhakti and
<>she became young again. But, her sons remained old.
<>So the mother requested Na1rada to turn them young
<>again. Na1rada read out to them the Vedas, the
<>Veda1nta (Upanis2ads) and the Bhagavad Gi1ta1, all to no
<>purpose. Bhakti's sons still remained old. Then Sanaka,
<>Sananda and Sanatkuma1ra asked Na1rada to read out
<>the Bha1gavata to them. Na1rada did so, and the sons of
<>Bhakti1 Devi1 became young again. (Padma Pura1n2a).
<HI>BHALANDANA. A king. Though by birth he was a
<>brahmin he became a Vais4ya because of doing things
<>belonging to the Vais4ya community like trade and

<HI>BHARADVA1JA I. Another name of Di1rghatamas.
<F>**) Di1rghatamas is also called Bharadva1ja. But the Bharadva1ja of pura1n2ic fame is not Di1rghatamas. Di1rghatamas is the son whom
<>Br2haspati illegitimately got of Mamata1, his brother's wife. There was then another legitimate child in the womb of Mamata1. Knowing this
<>the devas told her ‘Bharadva1ja’ meaning ‘bear the brunt of two’ and so the son of Br2haspati got the name of Bharadva1ja also. The real
<>name of this son was Di1rghatamas or Vitatha. Di1rghatamas is not the Bharadva1ja who was the father of Dron2a. The famous Bharadva1ja
<>was the son of Atri. Di1rghatamas or Vitatha was the adopted son of Bharata, son of Dus2yanta. (Bha1gavata and Kampara1ma1yan2a. For
<>details see under Bharata I and Di1rghatamas.</F> [Page117-a+ 65]
<HI>BHARADVA1JA II. The sage Bharadva1ja of Pura1n2ic
<>fame.
<NI>1) {%General information.%} Ayodhya1 Ka1n2d2a of Kampa
<>Ra1ma1yan2a states that this sage was the son of Atri
<>Mahars2i. He lived for many thousands of years. He is
<>connected with Va1lmi1ki and the story of S4ri1 Ra1ma.
<>Bharadva1ja was for many years a disciple of Va1lmi1ki.
<>He was present with Va1lmi1ki when the hunter killed
<>one of the couple of Kraun5ca. When Va1lmi1ki and
<>Bharadva1ja reached the shores of the river, Tamasa1,
<>that day Va1lmi1ki told Bharadva1ja thus: “Look, Bhara-
<>dva1ja, what a clean ghat this is. The water is pure and
<>clear. Place your water-jug here and give me my
<>valkala. We will get down here in this sacred water”.
<>Then Va1lmi1ki taking the valkala from the disciple
<>walked along the shore admiring the beauty of the
<>forest trees and found on his way the historic Kraun5ca
<>couple. (Sarga 2, Ba1la Ka1n2d2a, Va1lmi1ki Ra1ma1yan2a).
<NI>2) {%Bharadva1ja and the study of Vedas.%} Bharadva1ja gave
<>himself untiringly to the study of the Vedas. He obtain-
<>ed from Indra a boon to extend his term of life on
<>earth to many thousands of years by different stages,
<>each stage covering a span of a thousand years of life.
<>All these years he devoted to an incessant study of the
<>Vedas. Finding the term not sufficient for completing
<>the study of the Vedas he appealed to Indra again for
<>extension and Indra appearing in person took him before
<>three mountains and giving him three handfuls of
<>sand told him thus, “What you have studied about
<>Vedas till this time is equivalent to the amount of
<>sand I have now given and what is yet to be studied
<>about the Vedas is as big as the three mountains before
<>you”. Any other mortal being would have been dis-
<>heartened by this revelation made by Indra, but not
<>Bharadva1ja. Undaunted he continued his studies. (Bha1ga-
<>vata).
<NI>3) {%Bharadva1ja in the role of a magician.%} The a1s4rama of
<>Bharadva1ja was in Citraku1t2a and S4ri1 Ra1ma and
<>Laks2man2a in the beginning of their exile went to his
<>a1s4rama accepting his blessings. Bharata on his return
<>from Kekaya knew about the exile of his brothers and
<>hoping to bring them back to Ayodhya1 went in search
<>of them with a big retinue of soldiers and men. Keeping
<>the retinue outside, Bharata went to the a1s4rama of
<>Bharadva1ja. The latter decided to give Bharata and his
<>people a grand reception and calling Vis4vakarma1 to his
<>side asked him to arrange a royal banquet that night.
<>Devas, Gandharvas, Apsarases, As2t2adikpa1las and all
<>such people were invited for the night. Renowned dancers
<>from devaloka like Ghr2ta1ci1, Hema1, Vis4va1ci1 Mis4rakes4i1 and
<>Alambus2a1 appeared for entertainment. Even Vanara1ji1
<>took part in the dance. Dishes of food came to the
<>guests of their own accord. The night came to an end
<>wonderfully and at daybreak everything vanished and
<>all were amazed at the magic of the sage. (Sarga 91,
<>Ayodhya1 Ka1n2d2a, Va1lmi1ki Ra1ma1yan2a).
<NI>4) {%Dron2a's origin.%} Dron2a was the son born to Bhara-
<>dva1ja of the celestial woman, Ghr2ta1ci1. (see under
<>Dron2a).
<NI>5) {%How Bharadva1ja died once but was born again.%} See under
<>Arva1vasu.
<NI>6) {%The name Bharadva1ja.%} This is how the connotation of
<>the word is explained.
<Poem>Bhare'suta1n bhare s4is2ya1n
<>Bhare deva1n bhare dvija1n [Page117-b+ 65]

<HI>BHARATA I. Son of Dus2yanta born of S4akuntala1.
<NI>1) {%Genealogy.%} Descending in order from Vis2n2u - Brahma1-
<>Atri-Candra-Budha-Puru1ravas-A1yus-Nahus2a-Yaya1ti-

<NI>2) {%Birth.%} Dus2yanta was once hunting in the forests when
<>he hit a fawn with his arrow. The fawn fled to the
<>A1s4rama of Kan2va Muni and the king followed it. On
<>reaching the A1s4rama grounds he saw S4akuntala1 water-
<>ing the plants helped by her companions Anasu1ya and
<>Priyam3vada1. Dus2yanta and S4akuntala1 fell in love with
<>each other at first sight. Kan2va was absent from the
<>A1s4rama and they married according to the Ga1ndharva
<>rites and S4akuntala1 became pregnant soon. The king
<>gave her his signet ring as a sign of faith and left for
<>his palace. When Dus2yanta left her S4akuntala1 fell into
<>a deep reverie and she never knew about the arrival of
<>the arrogant sage, Durva1sas to the a1s4rama. Durva1sas
<>mistook her as disrespectful and cursed her saying that
<>she would be forgotten by the man of whom she was
<>thinking then. S4akuntala1 never knew about the curse
<>also. [Page118-b+ 65]
<NI>Kan2va Muni when he returned to the A1s4rama and knew
<>everything, sent S4akuntala1 to the palace of Dus2yanta.
<>But King Dus2yanta never recognised her and when
<>S4akuntala1 was returning deeply grieved Menaka1 her
<>mother, took her and left her in the a1s4rama of Kas4yapa.
<>There S4akuntala1 delivered a son. The boy grew brave
<>and fearless and he could subdue even the wildest of
<>animals around there. Kas4yapa, therefore, named him
<>Sarvadamana.
<NI>Once when Dus2yanta was returning home after visiting
<>Indra he saw S4akuntala1, recognised her, and took her

<NI>3) {%Marriage and reign.%} Bharata was a partial incarna-
<>tion of Maha1vis2n2u. Even while he was young he became
<>a ruler and conquering the world destroyed the wicked.
<>Bharata had three wives. All the sons born to them
<>were bad and so the mothers killed them all. Aggrieved
<>over the loss of his sons he worshipped the devas to
<>get a son for him. At that time the great preceptor
<>Br2haspati forcibly married Mamata1 the wife of his
<>brother. Mamata1 was pregnant then and when she
<>conceived from Br2haspati also she bore two children.
<>On delivery she threw the child of Br2haspati in the
<>forests and went away with the other son.
<NI>The Devas took care of the discarded child and named
<>him Bharadva1ja and gave the child to Bharata. Bharata
<>gave the boy the name Vitatha (Di1rghatamas). Bharata
<>ruled over his land for twentyseven thousand years and
<>the land was, therefore, called Bha1rata. (S4loka 96,
<>Chapter 2, A1di Parva, M.B.).
<NI>After ruling the land ideally he left for the forests
<>entrusting the land to his son, Vitatha. (Navama
<>Skandha, Bha1gavata).
<NI>Vitatha also was called Bharata and he had five sons:
<>Suhotra, Suhota, Gaya, Garbha and Suketu. (Chapter
<>278, Agni Pura1n2a).

<>pudding and it was after taking that pudding that the
<>wives of Das4aratha became pregnant. (Sarga 15,
<>Ba1laka1n2d2a, Va1lmi1ki Ra1ma1yan2a). [Page119-a+ 65]
<NI>3) {%Till the end of S4ri1 Ra1ma's forest life.%} The sons of
<>Das4aratha married the daughters of Janaka, King of
<>Mithila1. S4ri1 Ra1ma married Si1ta1, Bharata, Ma1n2d2avi1,
<>Laks2man2a, U1rmila1 and S4atrughna, S4rutaki1rti1.
<NI>Das4aratha made arrangements to crown Ra1ma as King
<>and then leave for the forests and lead an ascetic life.
<>At that time Bharata and S4atrughna were in the
<>country of Kekaya with their uncle, Yudha1jit. When
<>the day of coronation was drawing near Kaikeyi1
<>demanded of Das4aratha the execution of two boons
<>which were once promised by Das4aratha to her long
<>ago during a battle between the devas and asuras. One
<>of them was to crown her son, Bharata, as king and the
<>other to send Ra1ma to the forests for a period of
<>fourteen years. Das4aratha was shocked to hear that.
<>But, without any hesitation, Ra1ma and Laks2man2a
<>accompanied by Si1ta1 went to the forests and Das4aratha
<>overcome with immense grief caused by this unplea-
<>sant turn of events fell down dead. Messengers were
<>sent then to Kekaya to bring Bharata and after travel-
<>ling for three days Bharata and S4atrughn2a reached
<>Ayodhya1. Though they were not informed of the death
<>of their father they were worried all the way because
<>of the several bad omens which they saw. On entering
<>Ayodhya1 they were shocked to find all the roads deso-
<>late and arriving at the palace they found it silent and
<>gloomy. Kaikeyi1 then told him all that had happened
<>and when Bharata knew that his mother was at the
<>root of all this calamity his rage knew no bounds.
<>Forgetting himself he drew from the sheath the glitter-
<>ing sword and stood before his mother with the drawn
<>sword wavering to strike or not to strike and mused to
<>himself “No, Not a woman and not one's own mother,
<>No, it should not be done”. Immediately after this was
<>decided, he swung the sword straight to his throat.
<>But adroitly S4atrughna intervened and swept away the
<>sword before it fell at its aim. This strong move of
<>S4atrughna brought Bharata to his senses and he looked
<>at his mother so fiendishly that at his stare his mother
<>turned pale like a flower brought near a burning flame.
<>Bharata immediately changed into the dress of a
<>Sannya1si1 and started to go to the forests. S4atrughna
<>followed his brother. Vasis2t2ha also started. The news
<>spread like wildfire and people began to crowd at the
<>palace eager to follow the brothers. Very soon a huge
<>procession was seen moving towards the forests. Vasis2t2ha
<>and Arundhati1 in a chariot in the front, Kausalya1 and
<>Sumitra1 in another next to it and Bharata and S4atru-
<>ghna closely following the chariots, walking. People
<>joined the procession from behind. The great crowd
<>of people reached the banks of the river Gan3ga1. Guha
<>coming to know of the great exile of people from
<>Ayodhya1 through spies went and saw Bharata, at first
<>in disguise, and later as himself made his acquaintance.
<>He then took Bharata and S4atrughna across the river
<>to the presence of S4ri Ra1ma at Citraku1t2a.
<NI>When they reached Citraku1t2a only Bharata-S4atrughna,
<>Vasis2t2ha and Arundhati1, Kausalya1 and Sumitra1 entered
<>the a1s4rama of S4ri1 Ra1ma, all the others remaining out-
<>side. When Ra1ma and Laks2man2a were told about the
<>death of their father they were filled with grief. All the
<>sons, then, Vasis2t2ha officiating, performed the obsequies
<>of their father. Ra1ma and Bharata then discussed the
<>future. S4ri1 Ra1ma persisted in his vow and said he
<>would return to his country only after fourteen years [Page119-b+ 65]
<>and insisted that Bharata should rule the country
<>during that period. Bharata accepted the arrangement
<>saying that if his brother did not come back after four-
<>teen years he would give up his life by jumping into
<>the fire. S4ri1 Ra1ma then gave his sandals to Bharata who
<>accepting the same with due respect returned home
<>followed by others. On reaching Ayodhya1 Bharata did
<>not go to the royal palace which he considered as empty
<>because of the absence of his brother, Ra1ma and
<>abhorrent because of the presence of his mother, Kaikeyi1.
<>Instead, he went to a nearby village called Nandi and
<>installing the sandals there lived there and ruled the
<>country.
<NI>4) {%Return of S4ri1 Ra1ma.%} S4ri1 Ra1ma when he came back
<>to Ayodhya1 after fourteen years was crowned King.
<>Bharata got two sons of his wife Ma1n2d2avi1, Suba1hu and
<>S4u1rasena. While they were thus living happily in
<>Ayodhya1, message was sent through an ascetic by
<>Yudha1jit from Kekaya that some gandharvas were
<>creating trouble in that country. It was the state of
<>Sindhu in Kekaya which was subjected to this
<>molestation and on the advice of Ra1ma Bharata went
<>and subdued the trouble, killing the gandharvas. He
<>then created two small states on either side of Sindhu
<>and made his two sons the Kings of those states.
<>When S4ri1 Ra1ma gave up his life in Sarayu1 river and
<>rose to heaven as Vis2n2u Bharata and S4atrughna also
<>gave up their lives and took the forms of the conch
<>and the wheel which adorn the hands of Vis2n2u.
<>(Uttara Ra1ma1yan2a).
<HI>BHARATA III. A son of R2s2abha.
<NI>1) {%Genealogy and birth.%} Descending in order from Vis2n2u-
<>Brahma1-Sva1yambhuvamanu-Priyavrata-Agni1dhra-
<>Na1bhi-R2s2abha-Bharata.
<NI>Emperor Priyavrata partitioned his empire to his eight
<>children. Agni1dhra got Jambudvi1pa. Agni1dhra had
<>nine sons: Na1bhi, Kimpurus2a, Harivars2a, Ila1vr2ta,
<>Ramya, Hiran2van, Kuru Bhadra1s4va and Ketuma1la. On
<>the death of the father Jambudvi1pa was divided into
<>nine states and Na1bhi got the land called Hima. Na1bhi
<>married Merudevi1 and got a son, R2s2abha. R2s2abha had
<>a hundred sons and Bharata was the eldest. (Chapter 1,
<>Am3s4am 2, Vis2n2u Pura1n2a).
<NI>2) {%Marriage, administration and entry into A1s4rama life.%}
<>Bharata took over the administration of the kingdom
<>at the death of his father, R2s2abha. He married Pan5ca-
<>jani1 daughter of Vis4varu1pa. They had five sons, Sumati,
<>Ra1s2trabhr2t, Sudars4ana, A1varan2a and Dhu1mraketu. The
<>Pan5cama Skandha of Bha1gavata contains a statement
<>to the effect that India got the name Bha1rata from
<>this king. (It is worthwhile remembering at this
<>juncture a previous statement that the name Bha1rata
<>was obtained from Bharata, son of Dus2yanta). Bharata
<>like his forefather was very erudite and affectionate
<>and always respected his duties. He always meditated
<>on Brahma1 and in his heart there shone the Parama-
<>purus2a in the figure of Va1sudeva adorned with Sri1vatsa,
<>Kaustubha, Vanama1la1, S4am3kha, Cakra, Gada1 and
<>Padma. He ruled the country for a crore of years and
<>after that dividing the country among his sons went to
<>the a1s4rama of Pulaha Mahars2i to spend the rest of his
<>life there. On the rocks lying in the river flowing in
<>front of the A1s4rama were the marks of Cakra on one
<>side and Na1bhi on the other and the river therefore
<>came to be known as Cakrana1bhi. Bathing in this river [Page120-a+ 59]
<>and doing pu1ja1 Bharata lived there oblivious of the
<>world outside. (Pan5cama Skandha, Bha1gavata).
<NI>3) {%Bharata and the deer.%} Bharata led a purely ascetic
<>life performing everyday the rites laid down by
<>scriptures and muttering the mystic formula of Brahma1-
<>ks2ara. One day a thirsty pregnant deer went to drink
<>water in a nearby pond. As it was drinking it heard
<>the loud roar of a lion nearby. Frightened the poor
<>animal without even waiting to quench its thirst ran
<>into the forest and on its way delivered a child and the
<>deer-babe fell into the river. The deer exhausted and
<>tormented by fear ran into a cave and fell down dead.
<>Bharata happened to see the new-born deer floating
<>on the river and took it to his a1s4rama. From then on-
<>wards Bharata's mind was diverted from the spiritual
<>to the mundane effort of taking care of the young deer.
<>The deer followed him wherever he went and if it did
<>not turn up in time in the evening after grazing
<>Bharata went about in search of it weeping. Years went
<>by and Bharata became old and died with the name of
<>the deer on his lips. (Pan5cama Skandha, Bha1gavata).
<NI>4) {%Rebirths of Bharata.%} Because he died with the
<>thought of the deer in his mind he was reborn as a
<>deer. The deer was aware of his previous birth and
<>regretted that he spent the life of a man for the sake
<>of a deer. The deer, therefore, left the house of his
<>mother in the mountain of Ka1lan5jara and went to the
<>a1s4rama of Pulaha. The pious animal daily bathed in
<>the river and died there on the bank of that river. So
<>in its next life the deer was born as the son of a
<>brahmin in the line of An3giras. That brahmin had two
<>wives and got nine sons of his first wife and one of the
<>second. The son born to the second wife was none other
<>than Bharata. In due course the brahmin died and his
<>second wife jumped into the funeral pyre and ended her
<>life. Thrown an orphan Bharata became a puppet in
<>the hands of his brothers. Bharata was asked to look
<>after the cattle and fields of his brothers for his living.
<>With great forbearance Bharata did all he was told.
<NI>One day Bharata was keeping watch over the fields of
<>his brothers. It was midnight. In the neighbourhood
<>the Can2d2a1las were making merry over the birth of a
<>child to one of the women. Some of them were bringing a
<>man bound by ropes to be given as ‘Narabali’ to the
<>goddess Ka1li1. (Narabali is the offering of a human
<>being with his head cut off to propitiate a deity). On
<>the way the man escaped and the disappointed
<>Can2d2a1las were roaming about in search of a substitute
<>when they came across Bharata keeping watch over
<>the fields. Immediately he was bound by ropes and
<>taken before the idol of Ka1li1. The effulgence of the
<>brahmin astounded Ka1li1 and getting angry for bringing
<>such a pious brahmin for sacrifice she devoured the
<>Can2d2a1las and allowed the brahmin to go free.
<NI>Escaping from there Bharata reached a village walking
<>all the way. That village was being ruled over by a
<>king called Rahu1gan2a and that king was going to see
<>Bhagava1n Kapila Mahars2i along the banks of the river
<>Iks2umati1 in a palanquin. The palanquin had not enough [Page120-b+ 63]
<>bearers and so the brahmin was asked to join the
<>team of bearers. As they were moving the palanquin
<>shook because of the wrong steps kept by Bharata. The
<>king reprimanded Bharata and Bharata then gave the
<>king fitting replies based on the ethics of Veda1nta. The
<>erudition of Bharata greatly impressed the king and he
<>stepped down from the palanquin and bowed to
<>Bharata.
<NI>Bharata went from there to the forests singing devo-
<>tional songs in praise of Vis2n2u and at last attained
<>salvation. (Pan5cama Skandha, Bha1gavata).
<HI>BHARATA IV. A sage and the famous author of Na1t2ya-
<>s4a1stra. He was a critic who lived around the year 400
<>B.C. His book on Na1t2yas4a1stra (Histrionics) is world
<>famous. Ka1lida1sa in the second act of his drama,
<>Vikramorvas4i1ya states that this Bharata used to coach
<>the devas in the art of acting. Na1t2yas4a1stra is a book
<>comprising thirtyseven chapters dealing with the art of
<>dance and music. He has written in detail about the
<>four Alam3ka1ras, Upama1, Di1paka, Ru1paka and Yamaka
<>and also about the ten requisites of a Ka1vya. He has
<>not forgotten to write about the defects and demerits
<>of Ka1vya also. Commentaries on Na1t2yas4a1stra have
<>been written by lions in the profession: Mitragupta,
<>Hars2avardhana, S4an3kuka, Udbhat2a, Bhat2t2ana1yaka and
<>Abhinavagupta. Of these ‘Abhinavabha1rati1’ the com-
<>mentary written by Abhinavagupta is the only one
<>freely available now.
<HI>BHARATA V. The Maha1bha1rata speaks about a few
<>other Bharatas who were sons of Agni. S4amyu is a son
<>of Agni known as Bharata. This Bharata has got
<>another name, U1rjja. (S4loka 6, Chapter 219, Vana
<>Parva, M.B.). There is an Agni of name Bharata with
<>a son named Bha1rata. When this Agni is propitiated
<>one gets healthy and strong and so this Agni is called
<>Pus2t2ima1n also. (S4loka 7, Chapter 219, Vana Parva,
<>M.B.). There is another Bharata son of an Agni
<>called Adbhuta. It is this Agni that burns dead bodies.
<>As this Agni lives permanently in Agnis2t2oma Yajn5as; it
<>gets the name of Niyata also. (S4loka 6, Chapter 222,
<>Vana Parva, M.B.).
<HI>BHA1RATA (MAHA1BHA1RATA). An epic written by
<>Vya1sa.
<NI>1) {%General information.%} The Maha1bha1rata is an epic.
<>Bharata is acclaimed as the first emperor of Bha1rata.
<>The theme of this great epic is the fight between the
<>two lines of princes belonging to the dynasty of Bharata.
<>That is why the book is called Maha1bha1rata.
<>The great size of the volume and the greatness of
<>the wisdom contained therein have contributed much
<>to its getting the name, Maha1bha1rata. Once the
<>devas put the Maha1bha1rata in one pan of a balance
<>and the Vedas in the other pan. Then the devas
<>were convinced that the Maha1bha1rata weighed more
<>than all the Vedas put together. (S4lokas 269-271,
<>Chapter 1, A1di Parva, M.B.).
<F>*) <Poem>Pura1 kila suraih2 sarvaih2 sametya tulaya1 dhr2tam
<>Caturbhyah2 sara{??}asyebhyo Vedebhyo hyadhikam3 yada1
<>Tada1 prabhrti loke'smin maha1bha1ratamucyate
<>Mahatve ca gurutve ca dhriyama1n2am3 yas4o'dhikam
<>Mahatva1d bha1ravatva1c ca Maha1bha1ratamucyate.</Poem></F> This book contains
<>over a lakh of verses. There is no subject on earth
<>which is not dealt with in the Maha1bha1rata. None of [Page121-a+ 65]
<>the world's epics is so big as Maha1bha1rata. In size the
<>Maha1bha1rata is double that of Homer's Iliad and
<>Odyssey put together. The following poem about it is
<>worth mentioning now:
<Poem>Yatha1 samudro bhagavan
<>Yatha1 ca himava1n girih2 /
<>Khya1ta1vubhau ratnanidhi1
<>Tatha1 bha1ratamucyate //</Poem>
<>Maha1bha1rata is not a mere epic. It is a whole literature
<>in itself containing a philosophy which has been an
<>unfailing and perennial source of spiritual strength to
<>the people of India. Above all it has for its core the
<>Gi1ta1 which is perhaps the noblest of scriptures.
<NI>The Maha1bha1rata comprises eighteen books, each book
<>being called a Parva. There are 2109 chapters in it, the
<>chapters differing in size. Besides, there is an annexe
<>called Harivam3s4a which deals with the life and history
<>of S4ri1 Kr2s2n2a.
<NI>2) {%Contents.%} The contents of each Parva are succinctly
<>given below: The Parvas are so named as to give a
<>hint of their central theme.
<NI>(1) {%A1di Parva.%} (A1di = beginning). Pa1n2d2u and Dhr2ta-
<>ra1s2t2ra, king of the Candra dynasty, are brothers. Bhi1s2ma
<>brought them up. Dhr2tara1s2t2ra was born blind and he
<>got a hundred children of his wife Ga1ndha1ri1. They
<>were called the Kauravas. Pa1n2d2u had two wives Kunti1
<>and Ma1dri1 and got of them five sons called the
<>Pa1n2d2avas headed by the eldest Yudhis2t2hira.
<NI>(2) {%Sabha1 Parva%} (Sabha1 = court). The Pa1n2d2avas and
<>Kauravas lived together in the court at Hastina1pura.
<>There, the Kauravas entered into a game of dice with
<>Yudhis2t2hira and through the deceitful tactics engineer-
<>ed by the keen-witted S4akuni, Yudhis2t2hira was defeated
<>and he lost everything he possessed. The Pa1n2d2avas
<>were then compelled to go into exile to the forests for
<>twelve years and spend another year after that incog-
<>nito. If they were found out during that period of
<>incognito they were to go into exile for another term
<>of twelve years. The Pa1n2d2avas accompanied by Pa1n5ca1li1
<>left for the forests.
<NI>(3) {%Vana Parva%} (Vana = forest). This is one of the
<>longest of Parvas and describes the life of the Pa1n2d2avas
<>in the forest Ka1myaka. The well known stories ‘Nala
<>Carita’ and ‘Kira1ta1rjuni1ya’ are told in this Parva’.
<NI>(4) {%Vira1t2a Purva.%} (Vira1t2a = name of a King). The Pa1n2d2av-
<>as after completing their term of exile in the forests spent
<>the year of their life in incognito at the court of King
<>Vira1t2a. Many wonderful events took place during their
<>stay there and this Parva deals in detail about
<>them.
<NI>(5) {%Bhi1s2ma Parva%} (Bhi1s2ma = The first Generalissimo of
<>the Kauravas). The great Maha1bha1rata war was fought
<>in the vast expanse of Kuruks2etra lying to the north-
<>west of Delhi. Bhi1s2ma was the first generalissimo of the
<>Kauravas and Dhr2s2t2adyumna of the Pa1n2d2avas. Vya1sa
<>describes the events of the battle classifying it accord-
<>ing to the generalissimos of the Kaurava army giving
<>the four generalissimos a Parva each. Bhi1s2ma fought
<>for ten days and was struck down on the tenth day by
<>an arrow from Arjuna.
<NI>(6) {%Dron2a Parva%} (Dron2a = The second Generalissimo).
<>When Bhi1s2ma fell Dron2a took charge as the supreme
<>commander. This Parva deals with the fight under his
<>command. Dron2a was killed in the end by Dhr2s2t2a-
<>dyumna. [Page121-b+ 65]
<NI>(7) {%Karn2a Parva.%} (Karn2a = The third generalissimo).
<>This Parva deals with the war under the command of
<>Karn2a. Karn2a fell in the end at the hands of Arjuna.
<NI>(8) {%S4alya Parva.%} (S4alya = The fourth and last of the
<>Generalissimos). When Karn2a fell S4alya took over
<>charge of the Kaurava army. As the war was coming to
<>an end most of the big warriors of the Kaurava army
<>were killed. In the end Bhi1ma and Duryodhana met
<>in a grim fight with maces in which Duryodhana was
<>killed. This Parva deals with the final stage of the
<>battle.
<NI>(10) {%Sauptika Parva.%} (Sauptika = pertaining to sleep).
<>This Parva tells us the tragic story of how Kr2pa1ca1rya,
<>Kr2tavarma1 and As4vattha1ma1 who were the only three
<>left with Duryodhana after the great battle went into
<>the camp of the Pa1n2d2avas at night and killed all the
<>brave warriors of the Pa1n2d2ava army, excepting the
<>Pa1n2d2avas, who were sleeping in their camp unaware of
<>the foul play lying ahead.
<NI>(11) {%Stri1 Parva.%} (Stri1 = woman). This Parva deals with
<>the great lamentation in the battlefield, after the war,
<>of Ga1ndha1ri1 and other women.
<NI>(12) {%S4a1nti Parva.%} Yudhis2t2hira was crowned king and
<>even at that time Bhi1s2ma was lying on his bed of arrows
<>awaiting death. Before taking up his duties of the state
<>Yudhis2t2hira went to where the grandsire lay dying and
<>took his blessing and instruction in dharma. This
<>instruction of Bhi1s2ma1ca1rya to King Yudhis2t2hira of the
<>three dharmas, Ra1jadharma, A1paddharma and Moks2a-
<>dharma is the famous S4anti Parva.
<NI>(13) {%Anus4a1sana Parva.%} (Anus4a1sana = Instruction).
<>Bhi1s2ma still on his death-bed continues his instructions
<>on several topics, like duty, freedom and fasting, giving
<>entertaining stories to illustrate the essence of his teach-
<>ing. This Parva is devoted to this.
<NI>(14) {%A1s4vamedhika Parva.%} (As4vamedha = ya1ga conduct-
<>ed by emperors). This Parva deals with the ya1ga
<>conducted by Yudhis2t2hira when he became the empe-
<>ror of Bha1rata.
<NI>(15) {%A1s4ramava1sika Parva.%} (A1s4ramava1sa = life in a her-
<>mitage). Dhr2tara1s2t2ra, the blind and his wife Ga1ndha1ri1
<>accompanied by Kunti1 left for the forests and lived
<>there for two years in a hermitage. One day the forest
<>caught fire and the three without trying to escape sat
<>on the ground facing eastwards in yoga posture and
<>calmly gave up themselves to the flames. This is dealt
<>with in this Parva.
<NI>(16) {%Mausala Parva.%} (Mausala = Mace). This Parva
<>gives the story of how the Ya1davas fought against each
<>other with maces and were destroyed and how
<>Dva1raka1 sank down into the ocean. It describes the
<>death of S4ri1 Kr2s2n2a and Balara1ma also.
<NI>(17) {%Maha1prastha1nika Parva.%} (Maha1prastha1na = Death).
<>Dharmaputra entrusts his kingdom to his successor and
<>alongwith his four brothers and wife Pa1n5ca1li1 sets out
<>on a long journey to Maha1meru never to return. This
<>journey to death is detailed in this Parva.
<NI>(18) {%Svarga1rohan2a Parva.%} (Svarga = Heaven). This Parva
<>describes the entry of the Pa1n2d2avas and their relatives
<>into Svarga, heaven.
<NI>3) {%Mode of Narration.%} Most of the verses in the
<>Maha1bha1rata are in the Anus2t2up metre. At certain
<>places other metres also have been used. There are
<>prose pieces also in it. There are twenty notable pieces,
<>three in A1di Parva, seven in Vana Parva, seven in S4a1nti [Page122-a+ 51]
<>Parva and three in Anus4a1sana Parva. All the pieces are
<>statements by realised souls. The style of the poetry is
<>simple and the method of presentation lively. At times
<>the imagination of the poet extends up to the horizon.
<>Though there is a flow of spiritualism throughout the
<>matter is purely mundane.
<NI>4) {%Authorship.%} Bhagava1n Vya1sa is believed to be the
<>author of Maha1bha1rata. He was a contemporary of the
<>Pa1n2d2avas and the Kauravas. Many of the events of the
<>Maha1bha1rata were personally witnessed by Vya1sa. He
<>took three years to write this book. The following
<>s4lokas will support the above:
<Poem>Kurura1ja kulastha1na1m3
<>Dr2s2t2am3 vr2ttam3 ca yatsthitam /
<>Tatsarvam3 bhagava1n Vya1so
<>Varn2aya1ma1sa Bha1rate. //
<>(Padma pura1n2a)
<>Tribhirvars2aih2 sadottha1yi
<>Kr2s2n2advaipa1yano munih2 /
<>Maha1bha1ratama1khya1nam3
<>Kr2tava1nidamuttamam. //</Poem>
<>(S4loka 55, Chapter 62, A1di Parva).
<>The Vara1ha Pura1n2a states that Vya1sa composed the
<>Maha1bha1rata at a period when the end of Dva1para
<>yuga merged with the dawn of the Kaliyuga. The
<>Bha1gavata states that S4ri1 Kr2s2n2a passed away on the
<>first day of Kaliyuga. The Bha1gavata is devoted to
<>admire and praise the life of S4ri1 Kr2s2n2a. This book also
<>is written by Vya1sa. This s4loka found in the first Chap-
<>ter of A1di Parva clears the doubt:
<Poem>Va1sudevasya ma1ha1tmyam3
<>Pa1n2d2ava1na1m3 ca satyata1m /
<>Durvr2ttam3 Dha1rttara1s2t2ra1n2a1m3
<>Uktava1n Bhagava1nr2s2ih2. //</Poem>
<>The original name of the Bha1rata which Vya1sa compos-
<>ed was ‘Jaya’. In the first Chapter of A1di Parva is this
<>s4loka to support this statement:
<Poem>Na1ra1yan2am3 namaskr2tya
<>Naram3 caiva narottamam3/
<>Devi1m3 Sarasvati1m3 caiva
<>Tato Jayamudi1rayet//</Poem>
<>After the death of the Pa1n2d2avas and the Kauravas the
<>disciples of Vya1sa, Sumantu, Jaimini, Paila, S4uka and
<>Vais4ampa1yana gave this book great publicity. They
<>wrote books based on this story of Bha1rata of which
<>only the ‘Bha1rata’ of Vais4ampa1yana and the As4va-
<>medha Parva of Jaimini are now available.
<NI>Vya1sa's Bha1rata was first expounded by Vais4ampa1yana
<>at the Sarpa-satra of Janamejaya. At the request of
<>Janamejaya, Vais4ampa1yana added some more facts to
<>the original work known then as ‘Jaya’. Jaya with the [Page122-b+ 69]
<>accretions came to be known as Bha1ratasam3hita1. Su1ta
<>who narrated this story to S4aunaka and others at the
<>forest of Naimis4a added some more matter to the
<>Bha1ratasam3hita1 and made it larger. The present
<>Maha1bha1rata is this expanded edition.
<NI>The following facts prove that the Maha1bha1rata has
<>thus passed through three stages of evolution:
<NI>1) This book is known by three names: Jaya, Bha1rata-
<>sam3hita1 and Maha1bha1rata.
<F>1) <Poem>(a) Jayo na1metiha1so'yam3
<>S4rotavyo vijigi1s2un2a1 (S4loka 22, Chapter 62, A1di Parva).
<>(b) Caturvim3s4ati sa1hasri1m3
<>Cakre bha1ratasam3hita1m
<>Upa1khya1nairvina1 ta1vad
<>Bha1ratam procyate budhaih2
<>(S4loka 78, Chapter 1, A1di Parva).
<>(c) Refer to the s4loka given in para 1.</Poem></F>
<NI>2) There is a reference to the three parts of the book
<>in S4loka 66, Chapter 1 of A1di Parva.
<F>2) <Poem>Manva1di Bha1ratam3 kecid
<>A1stika1di tatha1pare
<>Tathopari cara1danye
<>Vipra1h2 samyagadhi1yire.</Poem></F>
<NI>3) There are three different opinions regarding the
<>total number of s4lokas in the Maha1bha1rata. According
<>to Ugras4ravas there are 8800 verses, according to Vya1sa
<>24000 verses and according to another statement a
<>lakh of verses.
<F>3) <Poem>Idam3 s4atasahasram3 tu loka1na1m pun2yakarman2a1m
<>Upa1khya1naih2 saha jn5eyama1dyam3 bha1ratamuttamam
<>Caturvims4ati sa1hasri1m3 cakre bha1ratasam3hita1m
<>Upa1khya1nairvina1 ta1vad bha1ratam3 procyate budhaih2
<>As2t2au s4lokasahasra1n2i As2t2au s4lokas4ata1ni ca
<>Aham3 vedmi s4uko vetti San5jayo vetti va1 na va1.</Poem>
<>(A1di Parva, Chapter 1).</F>
<NI>4) The narration appears to be of three different kinds,
<>descriptive, philosophical and oratorical, indicative of
<>three different authorships.
<NI>5) Su1ta and Vais4ampa1yana have made accretions to
<>the original work ‘Jaya’ of Vya1sa.
<NI>Viewed with these facts we are led to believe that
<>Vya1sa has composed only the very essence of Maha1-
<>bha1rata comprising 8800 s4lokas and that work was
<>known as ‘Jaya’ as referred to in the first invocatory
<>verse of the epic. Vais4ampa1yana added a few verses of
<>his own and brought the number to 24,000 and gave the
<>book the name of ‘Bha1ratasam3hita1’. It was Su1ta who
<>made many more additions and made the book so big
<>as to fetch it the name of ‘Maha1bha1rata’. The epic
<>which is now current among us contains a lakh of
<>verses and it took perhaps centuries to get this transfor-
<>mation of the book from ‘Jaya’ to ‘Maha1bha1rata’.
<NI>6) {%Period of publication.%} The Kali Vars2a starts from
<>3102 B.C. The great Maha1bha1rata war was fought at
<>a period when the end of Dva1para yuga merged into
<>the dawn of Kali yuga as could be gathered from the
<>following verse from the epic:
<Poem>Antare caiva sampra1pte
<>Kalidva1parayorabhu1t /
<>Samantapan5cake yuddham3
<>Kurupa1n2d2avasenayoh2. //</Poem>
<>(S4loka 13, Chapter 2, A1di Parva).
<>So it can be deduced that the great war was fought
<>round about 3102 B.C. Dhr2tara1s2t2ra lived for eighteen
<>years after the great battle and the Pa1n2d2avas ruled the
<>country for 36 years. (Stri1 Parva, M.B.). It was when
<>S4ri1 Kr2s2n2a died that the Pa1n2d2avas ended their rule
<>and commenced the Maha1prastha1na. So it can be sur-
<>mised that S4ri1 Kr2s2n2a lived for 36 years after the war. [Page123-a+ 62]
<>S4ri1 Kr2s2n2a died on the first day of Kali yuga, 3102 B.C.
<>Therefore it is clear the Maha1bha1rata battle was fought
<>in 3138 B.C. There are indications in other pura1n2as
<>to support this theory. There was that rare phenomenon
<>of a Saptagraha yoga (Combination in astronomical
<>position of seven planets) while King Pari1ks2it, son of
<>Arjuna, was ruling the country.
<F>1. (a) Saptars2ayo magha1yukta1h2 ka1le pa1riks2ite s4atam (Matsya pura1n2a 271. 46).
<>(b) Te tu pa1ri1ks2ite ka1le magha1sva1san dvijottama (Visnu pura1na 4, 24, 106).</F> This happened in a
<>month of Ma1gha (January) and a probe into the dates
<>of such previous occurrences revealed that a similar
<>one had happened in the month of Ma1gha in the years
<>3177 and 477 B.C. (The next one is to happen in 2223
<>A.D.). This phenomenon repeats itself in every hundred
<>years and so it is to be believed that one of the kind
<>happened in 3077 B.C. precisely during the period of
<>Pari1ks2it. It is therefore confirmatory to the statement
<>that the great war occurred in 3138 B.C.
<NI>The Pa1n2d2avas ruled the country for 36 years after the
<>war and Vya1sa wrote this divine epic after the death
<>of the Pa1n2d2avas. Vya1sa took three years to complete
<>his work. Therefore it can be well concluded that
<>Vya1sa wrote the epic in 3100 B.C. The Pa1n2d2avas
<>commenced their Maha1prastha1na after entrusting the
<>administration of the state into the hands of Pari1ks2it.
<>This must have happened in 3102 B.C. Pari1ks2it ruled
<>the country for 60 years and so his son Janamejaya
<>became King in 3042 B.C. It was after two years of
<>his becom3ing King that he conducted the Sarpasatra
<>and it was at this Sarpasatra that Vais4ampa1yana first
<>expounded ‘Jaya’ (Maha1bha1rata) to the sages assembl-
<>ed there.
<NI>This fixing of the date of Maha1bha1rata is based on the
<>conclusions arrived at by the pandits of Bha1rata, but
<>foreign chronologists differ from us. They argue that
<>it was in the 10th century B.C. that the Maha1bha1rata
<>battle took place on the strength of the following:
<NI>The theme of the Maha1bha1rata is a fight between
<>Kurus and Pa1n5ca1las. After the war the two join and
<>there is a statement in the Yajurveda of a people
<>formed by the merger of the two. Therefore the great
<>war should have happened in the 10th century B.C. It
<>was a period of hero-worship and songs on the heroic
<>deeds of the Kurus and Pa1n2d2avas were sung and
<>handed over from one generation to the other and
<>gradually those songs took shape in the form of the
<>Maha1bha1rata.
<NI>There is another school of thought. In the Maha1-
<>bha1rata, Brahma1 is given great prominence. But in
<>Vedic times Brahma1 was not counted at all. During
<>the golden period of the Buddhists Brahma1 had a cons-
<>picuous hold on Hinduism. Therefore it should be
<>deduced that the Maha1bha1rata was written during the
<>6th century B.C. Then there were about 8,000 verses
<>in the Maha1bha1rata.
<NI>Towards the second stage of Maha1bha1rata the epic
<>grew in size bringing into its fold about 24,000 s4lokas.
<>It was at this stage that the Maha1bha1rata got a vais2n2a-
<>vite colour and S4ri1 Kr2s2n2a was adored as an incarnation
<>of Vis2n2u. Megasthenes who visited India in 300 B.C.
<>records that the majority of Hindus were vais2n2avites
<>at that period. There are references in certain parts [Page123-b+ 61]
<>of the Maha1bha1rata to Greeks and Buddhists. There-
<>fore a set of foreign chronologists conclude that those
<>portions of the Maha1bha1rata could have been written
<>only after Buddha and after the attack on Bha1rata by
<>the Greeks i.e. by 300 B.C.
<NI>The third stage of the Maha1bha1rata is its present form
<>and it was at this stage that it included new philosophy
<>in it. The Dharmasam3hita1 of Manusmr2ti became popular
<>in the fifth century A.D. and therefore the third stage
<>of the Maha1bha1rata must have started before that, in
<>the 3rd century A.D.
<HI>BHA1RATA II. (Bha1ratavars2a. India).

<HI>BHA1RAVI. A Sanskrit poet who flourished in the 6th

<>Kira1ta1rjuni1ya. Raghuvam3s4a, Kuma1rasambhava, Kira1t-
<>a1rjuni1ya, S4is4upa1lavadha and Nais2adha are the five [Page124-b+ 66]
<>Maha1ka1vyas in Sanskrit with established reputation.
<>In the Ehole records of A.D. 634 Bha1ravi is held up
<>in praise along with Ka1lida1sa.
<F>1) Yena1yoji na ves4ma sthiram arthavidhau vivekina1 jinaves4ma/ Sa jayata1m3 raviki1rtih2 kavita1s4rita-Ka1lida1sa-Bha1raviki1rtih2.//</F> The theme of Kira1ta1r-
<>juni1ya is S4iva, in the guise of a hunter, attacking Arjuna

<>and sweet. In the use of similes he is second only to

<HI>BHARTR2HARI. A famous Sanskrit poet.
<NI>1) {%Birth.%} He was the son of Vidya1sa1gara, a great brahmin
<>scholar, who lived in Pa1t2ali1putra. There is a legend
<>about the birth of Bhartr2hari in Uttara Bha1rata.
<NI>Vidya1sa1gara while young learnt the Vedas from his
<>guru. But he was not satisfied with that study and
<>wanted to learn more about all s4a1stras and so he started
<>on a tour to find out a guru who would teach him all
<>he wanted. On his way he met several great scholars
<>but none came up to his standard of a guru. One day
<>tired after a long journey he reached a mountain valley.
<>Washing his hands and feet in a nearby river and
<>quenching his thirst he went and sat down under a
<>banyan tree to rest. The cool breeze sent the tired
<>brahmin to sleep and he slept.
<NI>A Brahmara1ks2asa who was living on the top of the ban-
<>yan tree when it became midday descended from
<>the tree to perform the sacred rites of the day and found
<>a man lying asleep. By the sacred thread on his body
<>the Brahmara1ks2asa surmised that the man was a brah-
<>min and woke him up. Waking up Vidya1sa1gara found
<>to his surprise the bearded Brahmara1ks2asa standing be-
<>fore him and after mutual enquiries Vidya1sa1gara told
<>him of his mission. The Brahmara1ks2asa told him thus:
<>“Boy, let your name Vidya1sa1gara (ocean of learning)
<>be true in your life. I am acquainted with many s4a1st-
<>ras and I shall teach you everything I know. But there
<>are certain conditions for the same which you have to
<>observe. You must live with me for six months and
<>should fast during that period behaving yourself as
<>one under a vow. I shall, however, teach you a mantra
<>which would help you to remain unaffected by hunger,
<>thirst or sleep. If you are prepared to accept these terms
<>and become my disciple I shall quench your thirst for
<>knowledge. Vidya1sa1gara willingly agreed to his terms
<>and became his student.
<NI>Both of them then climbed on the banyan tree and the
<>Brahmara1ks2asa sat in his usual place on a branch on
<>the tree and Vidya1sa1gara on a branch just below it. The
<>instructions started and continued for six months.
<>Vidya1sa1gara never felt thirst or hunger during that
<>period. When the teaching was complete the Brahma- [Page125-a+ 64]
<>ra1ks2asa bade farewell to his disciple and departed leaving
<>his body behind.
<NI>Vidya1sa1gara then got down from the tree and performed
<>the obsequies of his guru. Vidya1sa1gara while he was
<>learning used to note down the instructions of the guru
<>on the leaves of the banyan tree. It had come to a huge
<>bundle on completion. Taking the bundle along with
<>him Vidya1sa1gara left for his country. By the time he
<>left the forest area the power of the mantra faded and
<>Vidya1sa1gara began to feel the pangs of hunger and
<>thirst. By dusk he reached Kalin3ga and there he came
<>to the door of a dancing girl named Manda1kini1. The
<>door was locked as the owner was away in a S4iva temple
<>nearby. Exhausted by the journey, hungry and thirsty,
<>Vidya1sa1gara lay down on the doorstep and soon fell
<>asleep with the leaf-bundle as a pillow. Manda1kini1 when
<>she returned from the temple at about ten at night saw
<>a brahmin sleeping on the doorstep, tired and exhaust-
<>ed. She soon asked her servants to get him inside and
<>sent for a doctor. By the clever treatment of the doctor
<>and the expert nursing of Manda1kini1, Vidya1sa1gara sur-
<>vived. When he was fit for travel he started to go but
<>Manda1kini1 objected and requested him to marry her.
<>Vidya1sa1gara did not agree to it and she took him to the
<>King and told him all that had happened. Finding
<>Vidya1sa1gara to be one with great erudition and good
<>manners he decided that Vidya1sa1gara should marry
<>his daughter (a Ks2atriya), daughter of his minister
<>(a brahmin), daughter of Kulapati (a vais4ya), and
<>also Manda1kini1 (a S4u1dra). There was an injunction in
<>those days that a brahmin should marry a S4u1dra only
<>after having married from all the higher castes. The
<>decision of the King surmounted this difficulty for
<>Vidya1sa1gara to marry the s4u1dra girl, Manda1kini1.
<>Marriages were all conducted in order. The King's
<>daughter bore the name Kala1vati1, the minister's Ma1lati1
<>and Kulapati's Suman3gali1. Each delivered a son. The
<>brahmin boy was named Vararuci, the Ks2atriya boy,
<>Vikrama1ditya, the Vais4ya boy, Bhat2t2i and the S4u1dra
<>boy, Bhartr2hari.
<NI>2) {%Kingship.%} When the King of Kalin3ga became old
<>he gave the kingdom to Vidya1sa1gara and when Vidya1-
<>sa1gara died all his other sons made Bhartr2hari the King.
<>Bhartr2hari gave his father on his death-bed a promise
<>that he would see that no sons were born to him.
<F>1) There is a belief that if a son is born to a S4u1dra who was himself born of a brahmin the brahmin father would go to hell.</F>
<>So though Bhartr2hari married three wives he was care-
<>ful enough not to produce any children by them.
<NI>3) {%Bhartr2hari becomes a sannya1si1.%} Bhartr2hari was living
<>happily with his wife when one day a yogi1 of divine
<>disposition came to his house and giving him a ripe
<>mango said: “If you eat this mango you will never
<>become old. It is because you who are perfect in edu-
<>cation and fortune should live long for the benefit of
<>the people that I am giving you this.” So saying he
<>gave the mango and left the place. When the sannya1si1
<>left him Bhartr2hari thought thus: “After some years
<>my wife would become old and die. What is the use
<>of my remaining alive after she is gone. So this mango
<>should be given to her. Let her live long.” He,
<>therefore, gave the mango to his wife explaining
<>to her the greatness of the fruit. That wife whom poor
<>Bhartr2hari accepted to be very loving and chaste
<>had a lover. He was none other than the horse- [Page125-b+ 64]
<>groom of her husband and that prostitute thought
<>thus: “Of what use is my life when my lover is dead.
<>Let him live for ever”. So thinking she sent for her
<>lover and giving him the mango explained to him the
<>greatness of the thing. But the horse-groom was well
<>devoted to his wife and desiring that his wife should
<>remain young always he gave it to her. The groom's
<>wife was a servant-maid of the palace and it was at the
<>time of her departure from the palace after her day's
<>work that she was given the mango by the horse-groom
<>and so she carried it home in her hand. Bhartr2hari
<>who was returning from somewhere to the palace saw
<>the woman carrying the mango and questioned her and
<>knew how she got it. On coming to the palace he sent
<>for the horse-groom and though at first the horse-groom
<>tried to shield the queen, he was compelled to tell the
<>truth in the end. The truth pained him much “Phew!
<>It was this wretched and unfaithful woman whom I was
<>believing to be very chaste and loving. Woman can never
<>be believed. Look, she has fallen in love with an ugly-
<>looking servant of mine and unless he was her lover she
<>would not have given this mango to him. It is better
<>that this matter is not pursued any further now. We
<>will decide about the future course of action later”.
<>Thinking thus, he sent away the horse-groom and went
<>to his bed-chamber and lay there immersed in thoughts.
<>The horse-groom immediately informed the queen of
<>everything that took place and the queen felt sorry that
<>her secret had come to light. She knew that besides the
<>punishment that her lover would get her name would
<>also be blackened and so decided to kill her husband
<>before the affair became public. At once she made an
<>ot2t2at2a (flat bread made of wheat or rice) with poison in
<>it and bringing it to the bed-room told her husband thus
<>with much pretended affection. “It will take some time
<>for your lunch to be ready. Let not you lie with an
<>empty stomach. Eat this now”. So saying she placed
<>the bread in the hands of Bhartr2hari and left the room.
<NI>Bhartr2hari suspected foul play and mused “This bread
<>has been prepared by her with poison to kill me. It is
<>better to give up living with her. There is no doubt
<>about it. Of the four a1s4ramas the fourth, Sannya1sa, is
<>the best. It is pleasant and sorrowless. Therefore gra-
<>dually that a1s4rama should be accepted”. Thinking thus
<>he came out from his room with the bread and placed
<>it hidden somewhere in the lower end of the roof of the
<>house remarking “Ot2t2appam Vi1t2t2eccut2um” (The ot2t2at2a
<>will burn the house). Then taking an earthern bowl for
<>begging he left the house unnoticed by any. The moment
<>Bhartr2hari left the house the house caught fire and
<>everything in it was burnt to ashes.
<NI>Bhartr2hari renouncing all and changing himself into
<>the robes of a sannya1si1 went about living by begging.
<>After some time he decided that he would never beg but
<>would eat only that which others gave him voluntarily.
<>He had by that time reached a big temple
<F>2) This temple is believed to be the Chidam3baram3 Temple.</F> in south
<>India where in one of the entrances to the temple, the
<>east one, was sitting the famous sannya1si1 Pat2t2anatt
<>Pillaya1r. Bhartr2hari, therefore, went and sat at the
<>western entrance with his bowl before him. Whatever
<>food fell into the bowl was eaten by him. Sometimes
<>for days together he went without food. But he never
<>was sick or felt weak. [Page126-a+ 65]
<NI>One day a beggar went to the eastern gate and asked
<>Pat2t2anatt Pillaya1r for alms. Pillaya1r said, “I am also a
<>beggar like you. I have nothing in my hand to give you.
<>But there is a man sitting in the western gate. He is a
<>rich man and he may give you something”. The beggar
<>went to Bhartr2hari and asked for alms. Bhartr2hari
<>also expressed his helplessness in the matter. But then
<>the beggar said “The man sitting at the opposite entrance
<>told me that you were a rich man.” Bhartr2hari smiled.
<>He knew then why Pat2t2anatt Pillaya1r said so. A man who
<>has renounced everything need not keep even a bowl to
<>beg. The bowl was a luxury and a sign of wealth.
<>Bhartr2hari threw away the bowl and remained at the
<>temple entrance till the end of his life. It was while
<>sitting there that he wrote his famous poems.

<HI>BHA1RYA1. Wife. Bhi1s2ma points out the sanctified impor-
<>tance of Bha1rya1 (wife) as follows:--
<NI>Without her even the palace will prove itself to be just a
<>forest. She will be a great support (to the husband) in
<>the matter of dharma (duty) artha (wealth) and Ka1ma
<>(enjoyment of material comforts). (These three precede
<>the ultimate state of Moks2a (salvation) and the wife will
<>be a great support in fulfilling the conditions during the
<>first three stages.) While on tour in foreign places she
<>will remain faithful to him and instil confidence in him.
<>Bha1rya1 is great wealth to man.
<NI>In his forlorn life on earth the wife is of great help to
<>man. To him, who is suffering from diseases and is
<>otherwise in distress there is no remedy (medicine) like
<>a good wife. There is no relative like a wife. In the
<>matter of practising dharma there is no other support to
<>match the wife. If one has no good wife to take care of
<>domestic affairs one will be driven to the forest; the
<>home will be like a forest. (S4a1nti Parva, Chapter 144).
<HI>BHA1SA. A very prominent dramatist in Sanskrit. As
<>Ba1n2a and Ka1lida1sa have noticed him in their works,
<>it may safely be assumed that Bha1sa lived in an earlier
<>period. Since Pratima1 na1t2aka and Abhis2eka na1t2aka,
<>which are universally admitted to have been composed
<>by Bha1sa, depend for their themes on the Va1lmi1ki [Page126-b+ 65]
<>Ra1ma1yan2a, it is clear that Bha1sa lived after Va1lmi1ki.
<>Ka1lida1sa's period has not yet been fixed beyond doubt.
<>It is almost settled that Ba1n2a lived sometime between the
<>sixth and seventh centuries A.D. Prof. Keith has been
<>of the view that Va1lmi1ki lived before the 4th century
<>B.C. In the light of all such factors it could be presumed
<>that Bha1sa lived in the period between the 4th century
<>B.C. and 6th century A.D.
<NI>Though Bha1sa has so many glories to his credit he does
<>not fully match Ka1lida1sa, and this might have been the
<>reason why Indians did almost forget Bha1sa with the
<>advent of Ka1lida1sa on the literary arena.
<NI>We are not in a position to name with unerring definite-
<>ness the works of Bha1sa. The general belief is that he
<>has written nearly twenty three dramas. In 1922, Pandit
<>Gan2apati S4a1stri1 unearthed from various sources thirteen
<>dramas of Bha1sa. Three out of them, viz. Pratijn5a1-
<>yaugandhara1yan2a, S4vapnava1savadatta and Avima1-
<>raka owe their themes to the Br2hatkatha1, while Prati-
<>ma1 na1t2aka and Abhis2eka na1t2aka have Ra1ma1yan2a as
<>their basic inspiration. Out of the eight remaining
<>dramas six, viz. Madhyama vya1yoga, Pan5cara1tra, Du1ta-
<>va1kya, Du1taghat2otkaca, Karn2abha1ra and U1rubhan3ga
<>owe their themes to the Maha1bha1rata, while the last
<>two dramas, Ba1lacarita and Ca1rudatta are dependent
<>for their themes on anecdotes about S4ri1 Kr2s2n2a and on
<>yaks2a stories.
<NI>Though Dr. Gan2apati S4a1stri1 has strongly contended that
<>all the above thirteen dramas were written by Bha1sa
<>himself, his view has not yet received universal accep-
<>tance. Whatever that be, it is an admitted fact that
<>Svapnava1savadatta is by far the best among the thirteen
<>dramas. It is a continuation of the story of Pratijn5a1yau-
<>gandhara1yan2a. The wedding of king Udayana and
<>Va1savadatta1 is the subject matter of Svapnava1sava-
<>datta.
<NI>With his killing of the Ra1ks2asa ‘Avi’ the Sauvi1ra king
<>comes to be known as Avima1ra, and Avima1ra's falling
<>in love with Kuran3gi1, daughter of Kuntibhoja forms the
<>theme of the drama, Avima1raka. In Pratima1 na1t2aka
<>is told the story narrated in Ra1ma1yan2a from Ayodhya1-
<>ka1n2d2a to Pat2t2a1bhis2eka. Abhis2eka na1t2aka codifies the
<>stories in the Kis2kindha1 Ka1n2d2a and Sundara Ka1n2d2a of
<>the R2a1ma1yan2a and describes the coronations of Sugri1va
<>and of S4ri1 Ra1ma. Though the characters in Pan5cara1tra
<>are borrowed from Maha1bha1rata the story is entirely
<>Bha1sa's. Madhyama vya1yoga, Du1tava1kya, Du1taghat2ot-
<>kaca, Karn2abha1ra and U1rubhan3ga are one-act plays.
<>These five plays have as their basis some of the anecdotes
<>in the Maha1bha1rata. In Ca1rudatta is treated the love
<>story of Ca1rudatta and Vasantasena1.
<NI>Indisputable evidence is still lacking to credit Bha1sa
<>with the authorship of all the thirteen plays referred to
<>above.

<HI>BHAVABHU1TI. A Sanskrit poet who lived in the 7th
<>century A.D. His important works are the three dramas,
<>Ma1lati1ma1dhava, Maha1vi1racarita and Uttarara1ma-
<>carita. Bhavabhu1ti was a brahmin of the Kas4yapa-

<>karn2i1. He was a great devotee of S4iva and he got his

<>pura in the state of Vidarbha. But Bhavabhu1ti spent
<>most of his life in the palace of Yas4odharma1, king of
<>Kannauj.
<NI>Bhavabhu1ti's first drama is believed to be Maha1vi1ra-
<>carita. There are seven acts in this. The theme is based
<>on the story of S4ri1 Ra1ma. But there are some variations
<>from the original Ra1ma1yan2a in this drama. Bhava-
<>bhu1ti states that even at the time of the svayam3vara of
<>Si1ta1, Ra1van2a was a suitor. There is an opinion among
<>certain critics that Bhavabhu1ti did write only up to the
<>46th verse in the fourth act and the rest was written
<>by another poet named Subrahman2ya.
<NI>Ma1lati1ma1dhava is a drama of ten acts. It is a love-story
<>of Ma1lati1 and Ma1dhava. Ma1lati1 was the daughter of the
<>minister of Ujjayini1 and Madhava was the son of the
<>minister of Vidarbha. It was while Ma1dhava was having
<>his education in Ujjayini1 that he came to love Ma1lati1.
<>It was Ka1mandaki1 who was a classmate of both the
<>ministers and who became a sannya1sini1 later who pulls
<>the strings of this love story. The king of Ujjayini1 wanted
<>Ma1lati1 to marry Nandana, a friend of his. But Ma1lati1
<>did not love him. Yet afraid of getting the displeasure of
<>the King, Ma1lati1's father decided to give her in marriage
<>to Nandana. In despair Ma1dhava was about to commit
<>suicide when from the temple nearby Ma1dhava heard
<>an agonised weeping. He rushed to the site of the sound
<>and found to his bewilderment the gruesome sight of
<>Ma1lati1 being dragged for sacrifice by a sorceress named
<>Kapa1lakun2d2ala1 helped by her guru Aghoraghan2t2a.
<>Ma1dhava killed Aghoraghan2t2a and saved Ma1lati1.
<>Disappointed Kapa1lakun2d2ala1 swore that she would
<>seek revenge on Ma1dhava and disappeared. Ma1lati1
<>went back to her palace. At this time Madayantika1,
<>sister of Nandana and Makaranda, a friend of Ma1dhava
<>became lovers. Once Madayantika1 was attacked by a
<>tiger in a S4iva temple and Makaranda saved her from [Page128-a+ 63]
<>the wild beast, and they became lovers thereafter. The
<>proposal to give Ma1lati1 in marriage to Nandana was
<>still pending then. The marriage day was fixed and a
<>day before that by a clever ruse played by the keen-
<>witted Ka1mandaki1 Ma1lati1 and Ma1dhava eloped together.
<>Makaranda, friend of Ma1dhava, was disguised and dressed
<>in bridal robes and Nandana little suspecting the trick
<>played on him married the substitute. Before they went
<>to the bridal chamber for the night Nandana's sister
<>came to see her new sister-in-law and the moment she
<>put her eyes on her she understood who it was and that
<>night they also eloped. Thus poor Nandana was left
<>alone.
<NI>Kapa1lakun2d2ala1 by her sorcery separated Ma1lati1 from
<>Ma1dhava but luckily Sauda1mini1 a disciple of Ka1man-
<>taki1 saved her from Kapa1lakun2d2ala1. Later Ma1lati1
<>married Ma1dhava with the permission of the King.
<>This is the story of Ma1lati1-Ma1dhava.
<NI>Uttarara1macarita is a play of seven acts. The theme is
<>the story of Ra1ma after his return to Ayodhya1 after the
<>exile. It begins with the renouncement of Si1ta1 by Ra1ma
<>and ends with the vanishing of Si1ta1.
<NI>All these three dramas used to be enacted during the
<>festival of ‘Ka1lapriyana1tha’ at Ujjayini1. The predomi-
<>nant emotion in Maha1vi1racarita is ‘Vi1ra’, in Ma1lati1-
<>Ma1dhava, ‘Sr2n3ga1ra’ and in Uttarara1macarita ‘Karun2a’.
<>In expressing and elaborating the emotion of ‘Karun2a’
<>Bhavabhu1ti excels Ka1lida1sa. The one draw-back which
<>keeps him second to Ka1lida1sa is his lack of humour and
<>wit. The sanskrit poet Dhanapa1la in his book’
<>‘Tilakaman5jari’ speaks about Bhavabhu1ti thus:
<Poem>Spas2t2abha1varasa1 citraih2
<>Padanya1saih2 pravarttita1 /
<>Na1t2akes2u nat2astri1va
<>Bha1rati1 Bhavabhu1tina1 //</Poem>

<HI>BHAVYA III. One of the Saptars2is (seven sacred
