<>form of Varun2a, was greatly pleased. Seeing the pleased
<>celestial maids standing in rows passion arose in Brahma1.
<>He had seminal discharge. A1ditya (the Sun) took the
<>earth on which the semen fell and threw it into the sacri-
<>ficial fire, which blazed furiously. Brahma1 who became
<>the offerer of oblation had discharge again. He got it in
<>the sacrificial ladle and with recitation of spells offered
<>it in the sacrificial fire as if it was ghee. The powerful
<>semen contained the three attributes of Sattva, Rajas
<>and Tamas. From the attribute of Rajas arose the
<>living world which had activity as its purpose. Radiance
<>is the quality of Sattva which entered the living and non-
<>living world. It gives light to everything. It also gives
<>the power of discrimination. From the semen offered
<>in the fire, three sons with body and the attributes of
<>demi-gods came out. The male who had originated from
<>the flame called ‘Bharjana’ became An3giras. The male
<>who originated from the burning coals became Kavi.
<>Over and above these three radiances, other radiances
<>also originated from the sacrificial fire. From the Mari1cis [Page151-a+ 65]
<>(rays) of the fire Mari1ci the father of Kas4yapa was born.
<>From the Kus4a-grass spread on the floor of the hall of
<>sacrifice, the Ba1lakhilyas were born. Atri also was
<>born from the Kus4a-grass. The noble hermits called
<>Vaikha1nasas, who have grown powerful by penance and
<>became the seat of all good qualities, were born from
<>the sacrificial ashes. From the stream of fire which are
<>the eyes of the sacrificial fire the handsome As4vins
<>were born. The rest of the Praja1patis were born from
<>the ears of the fire. From the pores of the skin of fire
<>were born the hermits, from the sweat the ‘chandas’
<>(metre of Vedas) and from might, the mind. For this
<>reason the Vedic scholars and knowers of the S4ruti and
<>those who see the superiority of Vedas, say that fire is a
<>combination of all the gods. The trees that feed the fire
<>are called Ma1sas (months), the sap of the tree is called
<>Paks2a (half of the month i.e. full moon to new moon and
<>new moon to full moon), the pith of the tree is called
<>night and day and the flames are called Muhu1rtas
<>(auspicious moments). Rudras were born from the
<>blood of fire and the golden coloured gods, the Maitras,
<>also were born from the blood of fire. From the smoke
<>of the fire, the Vasus, from the flame of the fire Rudras
<>and the A1dityas of the extreme radiance were born.
<>The planets and stars which stand in their places in
<>the sky are the charcoal of the fire. Brahma1 declared that
<>fire was Brahma1 the supreme spirit, eternal and the
<>giver of all wishes. The sinless Maha1deva, who was in
<>the form of Varun2a said, “This sacrificial fire is mine,
<>and I am the sacrificer. So the children born as the first
<>fruit of this sacrifice are mine. So Bhr2gu, An3giras and
<>Kavi are my sons, no doubt. Let everybody who moves
<>along the sky know that these three are my sons, the
<>fruit of my sacrifice.” Agni said, “These are born from
<>my organs. They had been depending on me for their
<>sustenance. So they are my sons.” Brahma1 the teacher
<>of all the worlds and the grandfather of all living beings
<>said, “They are my sons. I offered my sperm to the fire,
<>and these sons were born therefrom, How can they be
<>claimed by anybody else? Here, I am the utterer of the
<>spell and the offerer of the sperm. I am the owner of
<>the sperm. Am I not the owner of the fruit? There is no
<>contest on the point that the birth of Bhr2gu and others is
<>from the oblation of my sperm.” The Gods who heard
<>these arguments bowed their heads before Brahma1 and
<>greeting him said, “Lord Brahma1, It is not only us that
<>are born from you, but the entire world of living and
<>non-living is born from you. So let Agni and Deva in
<>the shape of Varun2a have their wishes.”
<NI>12) {%Brahma1 and the giantess Karkkat2i1.%} In days of old, a
<>ferocious giantess named Karkkat2i1 lived on the northern
<>side of the Hima1layas. Because of the hugeness of her
<>body and the scarcity of food she became famished. She
<>began to do penance for the alleviation of her hunger
<>and thirst. She stood on one leg in the Hima1laya Moun-
<>tain, fixing her eyes on the sun in the day and on the
<>Moon in the night, and the severe penance continued
<>for a thousand years. Then Brahma1 appeared before her
<>and told her that she could ask for any boon. She said
<>“I wish to become a Su1ci1 (Needle) as hard as iron, hav-
<>ing the form of disease. This is the boon I pray for,
<>Brahma1”. Brahma1 blessed her and said, “Let it be so,
<>my daughter, you shall be a su1cika1 (needle) with the
<>prefix ‘V’ that is Vis4u1cika1 (Spasmodic cholera) You
<>can eat those who eat stale food, those who are wicked, [Page151-b+ 65]
<>those who sit in places where they ought not to sit and
<>those who are hard-hearted. Enter into their bodies and
<>affect their hearts, spleens and life-breaths. Thus carry
<>out the work of destruction? You can affect both the
<>good and the bad. But good people should not be killed.
<>So to save them from death, you can use this mantra
<>(spell).
<NI>“om3, hra1m3, hri1m3, s4ri1m3, s4a1m3, vis2n2us4aktaye, namah2;
<>bhagavati vis2n2us4akti ehi, ena1m3 hara hara: dehe hana
<>hana? paca paca! matha matha: utsa1daya utsa1-
<>daya: du1re kuru kuru sva1ha1--vis2u1cike! tvam3 hima-
<>vantam3 gaccha? ji1vasarah2 candraman2d2alam3 gata1si
<>sva1ha1.”
<NI>Saying these words Brahma1 disappeared. Karkkat2i1 made
<>her body smaller and smaller and finally assumed the
<>shape of a needle. After that she began her work by
<>entering the body of those who had lost their energy
<>because of illness, in the form of a needle of gout and
<>caused Cholera, and the body of those who were lean
<>and worn and weary, in the form of internal cholera.
<>Thus assuming two bodies and killing countless people
<>she travelled for a long time in the earth and the sky,
<>until she herself felt aversion to her work. When she
<>remembered her original form she loathed the needle-
<>form. Karkkat2i1 again went to the vicinity of the Hima1-
<>layas and began to do penance, which lasted for another
<>thousand years. Thus she became pure and by the
<>power of thinking, she acquired spiritual knowledge.
<>Then she wanted to be saved from the mirage of birth
<>and death, to attain which, she did penance for yet
<>another thousand years at the end of which, the highly
<>pleased Brahma1 appeared before her and said, “daugh-
<>ter Karkkat2i1, all the darkness is erased from your heart
<>and you have attained deliverance from delusion. Now
<>I will bless you according to your wish. Receive your
<>original body and walk about in the worlds eating the
<>ignorant, bad beginners, the imprudent, those who sit in
<>bad places and dwell in bad places.”
<NI>She became a giantess again, but as she had attained
<>purity of soul, she engaged herself in deep meditation
<>which was free from doubt. The meditation lasted for a
<>long time. Finally her mind waved. Then she remem-
<>bered about worldly things and felt hunger. She remem-
<>bered, “Brahma1 has ordained the wicked as my food.
<>So I shall seek them.” Thinking thus she went to a jungle
<>in the vicinity of the Hima1layas. That was the dwelling
<>place of foresters. In that dark night the King and the
<>minister of the foresters approached the place where
<>Karkkat2i1 sat. Karkkat2i1 seeing them thought, “let me
<>know first if they are good or bad.” So she decided to
<>ask them some questions.
<NI>She asked them in a voice like thunder. “Who are you?
<>Are you enlightened sages or ignorant fools? say quickly.”
<>The king of the foresters hearing this replied in a cynical
<>way. “You insignificant ghost? What is your form?
<>Where do you sit? Let us see your powerless body. Who
<>is there to fear at your sound which is like the humming
<>of a she-beetle?”
<NI>Hearing this reply Karkkat2i1 showed her tusks and lau-
<>ghed loud, to spread light, and to terrify them. They
<>heard the loud roaring sound of her laugh and saw her
<>huge form in the light. But those mighty heroes were
<>not at all shaken by the sound they heard or the sight
<>they witnessed. The Minister looked at her and said,
<>“Hei, Giantess? These delusions of yours will have no [Page152-a+ 65]
<>effect on us. Even if a number of mosquitoes like you
<>come before us, they will be only dry leaves before wind.
<>You are acting like this merely for food. So what is
<>your need? Tell us, we will satisfy you”.
<NI>Hearing the words of the minister Karkkat2i1 knew that
<>her might was useless before such valiant men. She
<>thought them to be sages. So she said “Hey mighty
<>heroes. Who are you? Tell me the truth”. The minister
<>said: “This the King of foresters and I am his minister.
<>We came for a night-walk to find out and punish wicked
<>people like you.”
<NI>The giantess appreciated them and said: “I am about to
<>put some questions to you. If you give me suitable ans-
<>wers you will be saved.” The King agreed. The ques-
<>tions put by her are given below in the order they were
<>asked:--
<NI>1) Inside what atom which is unit and multiple at the
<>same time, do the lakhs of universes originate and vanish
<>as bubbles in a sea?
<NI>2) What does shine as ether and non-ether?
<NI>3) What is it that is something and nothing?
<NI>4) What is it that is going and not going at the same
<>time?
<NI>5) What is it that has taken form from voidness?
<NI>6) What is it that is moving and at the same time station-
<>ary as a rock?
<NI>7) Who draws pictures in clear sky?
<NI>8) In what seed do the worlds exist as a big tree in its
<>seed?
<NI>9) From what substance can the living and non-living
<>things in the worlds, not be separated as the foam and
<>waves cannot be separated from the ocean?
<NI>10) What substance is dualism not separate from
<>(others) as fluidity from water?
<NI>The reply given by the minister to each question is given
<>below in the order of the questions.
<NI>1) Hey, Giantess: All the questions you have put be-
<>fore us encased in figurative words discernible only to
<>the Vedic sages, are concerned with the supreme Being.
<>The atom is the supreme Spirit which is beyond all
<>knowledge of the mind and the senses. As it is without
<>organs of action it is as unmanifested as the sky. And
<>inside that supreme atom which could be felt only by the
<>mind, crores and crores of bubbles of universes originate
<>and vanish due to frailty.
<NI>2) The impersonal Supreme Being, having no substance,
<>pervades everything. This Supreme Being, Brahman,
<>is pure knowledge (Jn5a1nasvaru1pa). It is the sky and the
<>non-sky.
<NI>3) As it is indescribable the Impersonal Supreme Spirit
<>is nothing, but as it is a reality it is something.
<NI>4) As it pervades everything accessible, the Impersonal
<>Supreme Spirit has the quality of motion. But as it has
<>no emotions and as there exists no space beyond, it is
<>motionless.
<NI>5) As it is inaccessible to others the Supreme Spirit is
<>having no existence, but as it is the essence of everything
<>that is good, it has existence.
<NI>6) The Supreme Spirit is the radiance of motion or
<>activity and as there is nothing else to be found out, that
<>is, as it doesn't know anything else it is as rigid as a
<>rock.
<NI>7) In the clear sky the Supreme Spirit draws the pictures
<>of the universe.
<NI>8) Exists in the Supreme Atom. [Page152-b+ 65]
<NI>9) As the entire visible world cannot be separated from
<>the Supreme Spirit nothing is other than the Impersonal
<>Supreme Spirit.
<NI>10) The differentiations of the various worlds are mere
<>appearances of the Supreme Spirit and so dualism is of the
<>same figure.
<NI>The King drew Karkkat2i1 nearer to the Impersonal
<>Supreme Spirit. He advised her not to commit slaughter.
<>She accepted the advice. The King asked her to come
<>to his palace assuming the form of a beautiful woman.
<>She asked, what could he offer to a giantess as food.
<>The King said: “You good woman, we shall find out a
<>way for that. You change yourself to a beautiful woman
<>wearing golden ornaments and stay in my palace for a
<>while. I shall arrange to gather sinners, robbers and
<>such other wicked people who are to be given capital
<>punishment, in thousands from various places, and shall
<>give them to you. You can take your original form and
<>take them all to the peaks of the Himalaya and feed on
<>them.
<NI>Gluttonous people like to eat in solitude. After having
<>eaten sumptuously, take some sleep, and a little rest in
<>meditation. When you wake up you come again and
<>take away people destined to be killed. The slaughter
<>done for the sake of righteousness is not a sin. It is said
<>to be equal to mercy. When your desire for meditation
<>is over you must come to me. If an attachment of love
<>is formed even with bad persons it is difficult to break
<>the love.”
<NI>Karkkat2i1 obeyed the King. They all reached the palace.
<>Within six days three thousand wicked persons, who
<>were sentenced to death, were brought before the King,
<>from his own country and the various dependent coun-
<>tries, and were given to Karkkat2i1. In the night she took
<>her original form, gathered all the wicked people, who
<>deserved killing and bidding good bye to the King,
<>went to the Peaks of the Hima1layas. (Jn5a1nava1sis2t2ha).
<NI>13) {%Brahma1 and Haindavas.%} At the end of the day of
<>Brahma1 there was the great flood, and destroying all the
<>heavens, Brahma1 went to sleep. He woke up next morn-
<>ing and after the usual morning ablutions, prepared for
<>creation. He looked around at the sky, and saw some
<>heavens still engaged in usual activities. He was amazed
<>at this unusual occurrence. Ten Brahma1s and the count-
<>less devas (gods) under them were doing their routine
<>work. Brahma1 stared at the worlds for a long time think-
<>ing “How is it”. Then he by his divine power caused one
<>of the suns, working in a heaven, to come to him, and
<>asked him. “Who are you, shining one? How have these
<>worlds come into existence?”
<NI>The Sun said with humility. “Your sons Mari1ci and
<>others erected a beautiful shed called Suvarn2atat2am,
<>in a corner of Jam3budvi1pa in the valley below Kaila1sa.
<>A Bra1hman2a named Indu arose from that hall.
<>The Brahma1n2a who was a Vedic scholar, righteous
<>and peaceful, led the life of a house-holder with his
<>wife for a long time. But they were childless. So they
<>went to Kaila1sa and did penance to get children. They
<>entered a natural arbour which was quite silent and began
<>severe penance. S4iva appeared and said that they
<>would get children.
<NI>In course of time, ten sons were born to them. After
<>a long time, when the sons had reached manhood the
<>old parents died. The sons felt very sorry. They regarded
<>life as short and full of woe and misery. So they went for [Page153-a+ 65]
<>penance, as they had no relatives left to care for them.
<>They abstained from all the pleasures of the world and
<>reached Kaila1sa. They began to meditate upon the
<>shortness of life. “What is there which is noble in this
<>world? Is there anything except woe and misery? How
<>simple is the desire of man for greatness? One thinks
<>himself prosperous if he becomes a house-holder. The
<>head of the village is more prosperous than a house-
<>holder. The King of a dependent state is more powerful
<>than the head of a village. An independent king is far
<>more prosperous than a dependent King. When we
<>think of the state of an emperor how simple is an inde-
<>pendent king! When compared with Indra, the Lord
<>of the three worlds what is an emperor after all? But
<>there is nothing glorious in the state of Indra also as it
<>ends in a Brahmamuhu1rtta (a period in the life of
<>Brahma1). So what is there substantial and eternal?
<>“Brahma1 alone is eternal and indestructible.” said the
<>eldest brother. “Concentrate your mind and say to your-
<>self, ‘I am the supreme Being Brahma1 seated on a lotus’,
<>and meditate upon it and pray for it and knowledge of
<>Brahma1 will be conferred upon you.” They understood
<>this and thus the ten brothers became Brahma1s. There
<>are ten heavens of them. They are ten worlds in the
<>sky of mind. I am the sun who creates day and night in
<>one of them,” saying thus the Sun went away. Brahma1
<>commenced his activity. (Jn5a1nava1sis2t2ha).
<NI>14) {%The age of Brahma1.%} Forty-three crores twenty lacs
<>of human years is one four-yuga period called ‘Deva-
<>caturyuga’ (a period of four yugas of the devas or gods).
<>One thousand deva caturyugas will make one Maha1yuga
<>(great yuga) A Maha1yuga is a day time of Brahma1.
<>During the period of one Maha1yuga, that is, during
<>one day of Brahma1, fourteen Manus, one after another,
<>help Brahma1 in doing the work of creation. This is the
<>time of the seventh Manu in the present day time
<>(Kalpa) of the present Brahma1. The period of six Manus
<>are over in the present day time of the present Brahma1.
<>Vaivasvata is the present Manu. Brahma1 wakes up in
<>the morning--the beginning of a Kalpa. Those which
<>underwent destruction in the night, are created again
<>in the morning. As Brahma1 wakes up daily in the morn-
<>ing, what are the things created before, which do not
<>undergo destruction? At that time there will remain,
<>without destruction, six or twentyfour tattvas (princi-
<>ples) which are natural creations. These twenty-four
<>principles are not the creation of Brahma1. He who is
<>beyond names, He who created Brahma1 in the lotus
<>flower, created them. Brahma1 who was originated in the
<>lotus which grew from the nave of Na1ra1yan2a performs
<>the work of creation guided by these twentyfour princi-
<>ples. The work of creation performed by Brahma1 is
<>called Pratisarga (substitute creation). Everything
<>Brahma1 created in the day time will be destroyed in the
<>evening. After a day time is over there is the night time
<>and sleep for Brahma1. The life of Brahma1 is hundred
<>years, that is thirty-six thousand Brahma1 days and
<>and Brahma1 nights. At the expiry of this period the life
<>of Brahma1 will come to an end, and will dissolve in
<>Na1ra1yan2a. Thus crores and crores of Brahma1s have
<>appeared and passed away and in future also crores
<>will appear and pass away. (Bha1gavata).
<NI>15) {%The creations and substitute creations of Brahma1.%} The
<>creations performed by Brahma1 are of two kinds. The
<>first is Sarga or creation and the second is Pratisarga or [Page153-b+ 63]
<>substitute creation. In the first Kalpa when a Brahma1
<>is born from the lotus, certain creations are made and
<>the things thus originated do not undergo destruction in
<>the evening or at the end of the Kalpa. They are the
<>sarga or creation. On that day and every day of the life
<>of Brahma1 they help Brahma1 in the work of creation.
<>In a sense they could be called Praja1patis (the lords of
<>emanation). But as most of them have not left sons,
<>and for other reasons also, they are not well known by
<>that name. Only the persons mentioned here have as
<>long a life as that of Brahma1. The Sanakas, Karddama,
<>Mari1ci, Atri, An3giras, Pulastya, Pulaha, Kratu, Bhr2gu,
<>Vasis2t2ha, Daks2a, Na1rada, Dharma, Adharma, Nirr2ti,
<>Sarasvati1, Ka1ma, Atharva, the Manes, Agni, Stha1n2u,
<>and Sva1yam3bhuva: these do not end at the end of a
<>Brahma1's day (or Kalpa) and will be dissolved only when
<>Brahma1 is dissolved. A Brahma1 creates in his first morn-
<>ing not only persons from Sanaka to Sva1yam3bhuva, but
<>also creations which have a history only up to the end
<>of that day, such as the lokas (worlds) of Bhu1loka,
<>Bhuvarloka and Svarloka, and minerals, plants, animals,
<>humanity etc. Sarga or creation is called Pra1kr2ta Sr2s2t2i
<>(natural creation). Pratisarga or substitute creation
<>is called Apra1kr2ta Sr2s2t2i or unnatural creation. At the
<>end of the Kalpa, Sam3varttaka1gni (Fire which destroys
<>the universe) and Sam3varttaka vars2a1 (Rain which des-
<>troys the universe) will occur and all the unnatural crea-
<>tions will be destroyed. In all the morning, following
<>the first morning of a Brahma1 substitute creation takes
<>place, and they are destroyed at the end of each Kalpa
<>by Sam3varttaka1gni and Sam3varttakavars2a1. (Bha1ga-
<>vata).

<NI>(52) In almost all the Pura1n2as Brahma1 is called ‘Catur-
<>mukha’ (having four faces) and to substantiate this the
<>following story is given in the Matsyapura1n2a. Brahma1

<NI>One day S4ataru1pa1 was walking round Brahma1. The
<>daughters born from his mind also were seated close by.
<>So he could not turn round and round and look at his
<>wife as she was moving. To solve this problem, in place
<>of one face, there arose four faces.
<NI>Once S4ataru1pa1 was walking in the air. To see her at
<>that time there arose a fifth head on the top of his matted
<>hair. It is this face that was plucked off by S4iva.
<NI>(53) Various stories occur in the pura1n2as regarding how
<>S4iva plucked off the fifth head of Brahma1. They are
<>given below. [Page155-a+ 65]
<NI>(1) Once Brahma1 praised S4iva and pleased him and
<>asked him to become his son. S4iva did not like it. He
<>got angry and cursed, “I shall become your son. But I
<>will pluck off your fifth head.”
<NI>(2) In the beginning Brahma1 created the incarnation
<>of S4iva, called Ni1lalohita also, along with the creation
<>of worlds and everything in it. In later creations Brahma1
<>did not think of Ni1lalohita. Seeing Brahma1 carrying
<>on the work of creation forgetting him, Ni1lalohita got
<>angry and cursed Brahma1, “Your fifth head will shortly
<>be destroyed.”
<NI>(3) After Brahma1 became five-faced the brightness of
<>the devas began to decrease day by day. Seeing this the
<>conceited Brahma1 said to S4iva: “I am the first who got
<>existence in this world. So by all means I am the eldest.”
<>Hearing this S4iva got angry and plucked off the fifth
<>head of Brahma1 and threw it away. (Matsyapura1n2a,
<>183, 84-86).
<NI>When the fifth head was gone, the rest of the heads were
<>benumbed. The sweat which flowed from them was
<>thrown into the earth by Brahma1. Out of it came out
<>a horrible monster, which began to chase S4iva, who
<>caught hold of it and gave it to Vis2n2u. (Skanda, 5,
<>13, 4).
<NI>(4) In the S4iva-Rudra collections a story occurs about
<>the quarrel between S4iva and Brahma1.
<NI>Once Brahma1 fell in love with Sati1 the wife of S4iva;
<>coming to know of this S4iva tried to kill Brahma1. Maha1-
<>vis2n2u tried to pacify S4iva, but it was of no avail. S4iva
<>took away the fifth head of Brahma1 and made him ugly,
<>and consequently Brahma1 became invincible in the
<>world.
<NI>(5) Once S4iva showed his daughter Sandhya1 to Brahma1
<>who grew lustful instantly; S4iva insulted Brahma1 by
<>showing this ill-owned passion of his to his sons
<>(Brahma1's sons). As a revenge Brahma1 created Sati1
<>and insulted S4iva through Daks2a. (Skanda, 2, 2, 26).
<NI>17) {%Various names of Brahma1 and their meanings:%} In Amara-
<>kos4a, the following names are given to Brahma1.

<HI>BRAHMA. See Parabrahma.
<HI>BRA1HMA. A kind of marriage. The bride is adorn-
<>ed with all kinds of ornaments and is taken to bride-
<>groom; and given to him as wife. This is called Bra1hma
<>marriage. Maha1bha1rata, A1di Parva, Chapter 73,
<>Stanza 8 states that this mode of marriage was allowed
<>to all the four castes of ancient Bha1rata.
<HI>BRAHMABALI. An individual of a dynasty of teachers.
<>(See Guruparam3para1).
<HI>BRAHMACA1RI I. See Brahmacarya.
<HI>BRAHMACA1RI II. A devagandharva (a class of Gods)
<>born to Kas4yapapraja1pati of his wife Pr2tha1. He took
<>part in the Birth festival of Arjuna. (M.B. A1di Parva,
<>Chapters 65 and 122).
<HI>BRAHMACARYA. In ancient India an ideal life was
<>considered to pass through four stages, and Brahma-
<>carya is one of the stages of life. The four stages
<>are Brahmacarya (Vedic student vowed to chastity),
<>Ga1rhasthya (married householder), Va1naprastha (forest
<>hermit) and Sannya1sa (an ascetic who has renounced
<>the world). Brahmacarya, the first stage of the four
<>is considered as the period of education. The rules and
<>conduct of a Brahmaca1ri1 are given in Manusmr2ti,
<>Chapter 2.
<NI>The brahmaca1ri1 who lives with the teacher has to obey
<>certain rules and principles. He should bathe in the
<>river at sun-rise and offer oblations to gods and manes.
<>A brahmaca1ri1 has to abstain from using honey, meat,
<>fragrance, flowers, flower of chrysanthemum (Ind.)
<>women, curd, milk, ghee, churned-curd, slaughter,
<>bath applying oil, anointing the eyes with collyrium,
<>sandals, umbrella, lust, anger, covetousness, dance,
<>music, gambling, slander, ridicule, falsehood, and
<>causing injury to others. A Brahmaca1ri1 should go to
<>bed alone. Semen should not be made to discharge
<>through lust. If seminal discharge occurs in dream he
<>should take bath early in the morning and worship the
<>Sun, pronouncing the mantra or spell ‘Punah2’ thrice.
<>The Brahmaca1ri1 should live on alms. It is the duty of
<>the brahmaca1ri1 to bring water, flower for oblation,
<>cowdung, soil and darbha (grass) for the teacher. He
<>should receive alms only from the houses of those who
<>believe in the Vedas and sacrifices. He should not go
<>to the house of his teacher or his heirs and their families
<>for alms. Provided there are no other houses he may
<>receive alms from the houses of his teacher's relatives
<>and in the absence of such houses he may approach
<>the houses of heirs to his teacher and in the absence
<>of such houses he may receive alms from the house of
<>the teacher. He should fetch ‘Camata’ (fuel chiefly
<>from butea frondosa) from a place of purity in the [Page156-a+ 65]
<>forest, as instructed in rules and dry it in the sun; in
<>the absence of which it may be got dried in a sheltered
<>place. This fuel should be put in the sacrificial fire in
<>the mornings, at noons, and in the evenings. If a healthy
<>Brahmaca1ri1 fails in doing his duties consecutively for
<>seven days it will be considered that his vow of Brahma-
<>carya is broken and then he will have to take the vow
<>of ‘Avaki1rn2i’ again. Without waiting for the order of
<>the teacher, the Brahmaca1ri1 (student) should engage
<>himself in devotion and meditation and in the service
<>of the teacher daily. When the student is before the
<>teacher he should stand with folded hands controlling
<>his body, speech, brains, sensitive organs, and mind.
<>Only when he is asked to sit, should he do so before
<>the teacher, covering the portions of his body, which
<>are to be covered, with clothes. He should rise up in
<>the morning before his teacher wakes up, and go to
<>bed only after the teacher has retired for sleep. When
<>his teacher calls him, he should not give reply lying,
<>sitting, walking, eating or looking in the opposite
<>direction. He should not gainsay the teacher. The
<>student should attend to the orders of the teacher and
<>comply with them, sitting if the teacher is sitting,
<>standing if the teacher is standing, standing back if the
<>teacher is advancing towards him, running behind if
<>he is walking, standing close by when he is facing the
<>opposite direction, folding his limbs when the teacher
<>is lying or sitting near. The student should have his
<>seat and bed one step lower than those of his teacher.
<>The student should not sit in a careless manner in
<>places where the teacher's eyes could reaeh. He should
<>not utter the name of his teacher. If anybody speaks ill
<>of his teacher he should close his ears and run away
<>from the spot. If a Brahmaca1ri1 scoffs his teacher, he
<>will be born as a donkey, and as a dog if he scorns him,
<>and as a worm if he steals the wealth of his teacher
<>and as a beetle if he becomes jealous at the greatness
<>of his teacher. When the teacher is angry the brahma-
<>ca1ri1 should not go near him. The Brahmaca1ri1 should
<>not speak unasked. A Brahmaca1ri1 could sit with his
<>teacher only when they are in a vehicle, or on a camel
<>or in a tower or on the steps or on rocks or in a boat. He
<>should consider the teacher of his teacher as his teacher.
<>He should not address his parents who visit the house
<>of the teacher, without the permission of his teacher. He
<>should respect learned men, holymen, sons of the
<>teacher and those who are born of his family, as a
<>teacher. If the wife of the teacher is of the caste of the
<>teacher, the brahmaca1ri1 should consider her as his
<>teacher. If the wife is not of the same caste the brahma-
<>ca1ri1 needs only to stand up and make obeisance to her.
<>A brahmaca1ri1 should not apply oil to the body of his
<>teacher's wife, wash her, massage her body, and comb
<>her hair. A brahmaca1ri1 may either shave his head or
<>grow matted hair. He should never be asleep at sunrise
<>or sunset. For learning Vedas etc. the student should not
<>give the teacher cows, clothes etc. as free gifts before-
<>hand. On completion of education, when the student
<>asks the teacher for permission to get married, he should
<>give the teacher what he demands. A brahmaca1ri1 who
<>has fully complied with the rules mentioned above,
<>will attain heaven after his death.
<HI>BRAHMADATTA I. A famous king of Ka1m3pilyapura.
<NI>1) {%Birth.%} Brahmadatta was born in the family of
<>Br2hatks2atra who was a scion of the Solar dynasty. [Page156-b+ 65]
<>Br2hatks2atra begot Suhotra, Suhotra begot Hasti, Hasti
<>begot Ajami1dha, Ajami1dha begot Br2hadis2u, Br2hadis2u
<>begot Br2haddhanus, Br2haddhanus begot Br2haddharma1,
<>Br2haddharma1 begot Satyajit, Satyajit begot Vis4vajit,
<>Vis4vajit begot Sena1jit and Sena1jit begot Rucira, Rucira
<>begot Pr2thusena, Pr2thusena begot Ni1pa, Ni1pa begot
<>Para, Para begot Pr2thu, Pr2thu begot Sukr2ta, Sukr2ta be-
<>got Vibhra1ja, Vibhra1ja begot An2uha, and An2uha mar-
<>ried Kr2tvi1, the daughter of hermit S4ukra. Brahmadatta
<>was born to An2uha by Kr2tvi1.
<NI>2) {%Previous birth and kingship.%} In Padmapura1n2a, Part
<>III, Chapter 10, there is a story stating how the
<>seven sons of Kaus4ika, a hermit, became fishermen by
<>a curse and how they got absolution and attainment
<>of heaven by offering oblations to the manes. The
<>story is given below. Brahmadatta was the rebirth of
<>Pitr2vartti, one of those seven sons.
<NI>In days of old there was a hermit named Kaus4ika in
<>Kuruks2etra. His seven sons, S4varu1pa, Krodhana,
<>Him3sra, Pis4una, Kavi, Va1gdus2t2a and Pitr2vartti were
<>studying under Garga, a teacher. Their deeds were as
<>bad as their names indicated. Their father died. The
<>sons were in want. There was no rain anywhere in the
<>world. These brothers had taken the milking cow of
<>their teacher Garga to the forest for grazing. They
<>decided to kill the cow of their teacher. The youngest
<>of them said, “If we kill the cow we can use it as
<>offerings to the manes, and the sin will not be visited
<>on us.” The elder brothers agreed to it. Accordingly
<>Pitr2vartti killed the cow and began sacrifice to the
<>manes. Two of the elder brothers were detailed to give
<>oblations to the devas, and three of them to offer
<>oblations to the manes. One was detailed to be the
<>guest and himself as the sacrificer. Thus remembering
<>the manes the sacrifice was completed with the neces-
<>sary rituals. In short, those seven hermits got divine
<>power and became daring wicked men and ate the cow
<>of their teacher. They told the teacher that the cow
<>was eaten by a leopard and gave him the calf.
<NI>In course of time they all died and were born again as
<>wild men in Das4apura. But because of their offerings to
<>the manes they had retained memory of their previous
<>births. So they fasted in holy baths and discarded their
<>lives before the people. They were born again as deer
<>in the mount of Ka1lan5jara. Because of the power of
<>knowledge and the penance and fast they discarded
<>the body of deer in Prapatana and got rebirth as
<>Cakrava1ka (ruddy goose) in the lake called Ma1nasa-
<>saras. In this birth their names were Sumanas, Kusuma,
<>Vasu, Citradars4i, Sudars4i, Jn5a1ta and Jn5a1napa1raga,
<>names suitable to their character. Those seven loving
<>brothers were saints by virtue of their harmony with
<>the Supreme spirit. But owing to mental disturbance
<>three of them lost their attainments. Seeing the plea-
<>sures that King An2uha of Pa1n5ca1la was enjoying by
<>his wealth, vehicles, big army and beautiful women
<>one of the Cakrava1kas, Pitr2vartti, the devoted Bra1h-
<>man2a, who being loyal to his father had performed
<>sacrifice and given offerings to the manes, desired to
<>become a king. The other two brothers seeing the
<>wealth, glory and power of the two ministers, wished
<>to become ministers. Thus Pitr2vartti was born as the
<>son of An2uha with the name Brahmadatta and the
<>other two were born as Pun2d2ari1ka and Suba1laka, sons of
<>the two ministers. Brahmadatta was anointed as King of [Page157-a+ 65]
<>Pa1n5ca1la in the great city of Ka1m3pilya. The King
<>loved his father. He was mighty and powerful. He was
<>regular in performing sacrifices and giving offerings to
<>the manes. He had the attainment of harmony with
<>the supreme spirit. He understood the thought and
<>speech of birds and animals. He married Sannati, the
<>daughter of Sudeva (Devala); she was in her former
<>birth the cow of Garga.
<NI>Once as the King was walking in the garden with his
<>wife, he saw two ants. The minute creatures, who
<>were husband and wife were engaged in a petty quar-
<>rel. The male one, with loving words, pacified the
<>female one, who instantly came close to her husband
<>and they became happy. The King felt amazed at this
<>conversation of the ants, and he laughed. Because of
<>the attainments of his previous lives, he had the power
<>to understand the language of animals, birds, etc.
<>Seeing the King laughing without any reason the queen
<>Sannati asked him why he laughed. She said that
<>there was no reason whatsoever for the king to laugh
<>just then. The King replied, “Oh, beautiful one! just
<>now there was a quarrel between a female and a male
<>ant. I heard it, That is why I laughed. There is no
<>other reason.” The queen did not believe the King.
<>She thought that the King had been scoffing her. She
<>asked him how he came to understand the language
<>of such creatures. The King could not answer her
<>question. He fasted for seven days. On the seventh
<>day Brahma1 appeared before him and told him, “The
<>King will know everything from the words of a
<>Bra1hmin, whom he would meet when he goes to walk
<>in the streets.”
<NI>Brahmadatta returned to the palace and spent the
<>night there. Next morning he started with his wife and
<>ministers, for a walk. When they left the palace they
<>saw an old Brahmin coming towards them. Seeing the
<>king the Bra1hmin recited a verse:
<Poem>“Bra1hman2as in Kuruks2etra
<>which seven as wild men in Das4apura?
<>Stags in Ka1lan5jara, ruddy geese in Ma1nasa,
<>Then, are we, now living here”.</Poem>
<NI>When the King heard this verse the memory of his
<>former births revived and he fell down in a swoon.
<NI>There is a reason why the old Bra1hmin recited the
<>verse. The Ruddy geese mentioned before took their
<>next birth as sons of a poor Bra1hmin in the same city.
<>They had the memory of their previous births. They
<>were called Dhr2tima1n, Sarvadars4i, Vidya1candra and
<>Tapodhika, names suitable to their character. The
<>Brahmin boys decided to go for penance to attain su-
<>preme bliss. Then the poor father asked them, “My dear
<>sons, what is this? You are going away leaving your
<>poor and aged father. Is that dutiful? The sons replied,
<>“We will tell you a way to earn wealth. You recite to
<>the King, to-morrow morning this verse, which we
<>will teach you now. You will get plenty of wealth.”
<>The boys taught their father this verse and then they
<>went to the forest. Their father, the old Brahmin, came
<>to the gate of the palace at Ka1m3pilya. The King was
<>Brahmadatta.
<NI>There is another story how Brahmadatta became the
<>King of this place. Long ago An2uha, the King of Pa1n5ca1la
<>did penance before Brahma1, who appeared before the
<>King and asked him what he desired. The King made
<>his request that he might have a son who should be a [Page157-b+ 64]
<>mighty hero, a learned man, a great hermit and who
<>could understand the language of all living creatures.
<>Brahma1 granted him boon and thus Brahmadatta was
<>born to An2uha. It was before this Brahmadatta that
<>the Bra1hman2a recited his verse and it was that King
<>who swooned and fell down.
<NI>The two ministers were Suba1laka the son of Ba1bhravya
<>who was the author of the Ka1mas4a1stra and Pun2d2ari1ka
<>the son of Pa1n5ca1la who propagated the science of
<>medicine. They also remembered about their former
<>births and also fell down unconscious. When they
<>recovered the King Brahmadatta and the minister
<>Subala and Pun2d2ari1ka bemoaned the loss of powers
<>they had attained before by devotion, meditation,
<>penance and sacrifice. They cried, “Fate has made us
<>desirous of the objects of our senses and we have lost our
<>harmony with the Supreme Spirit.” They repeated the
<>cry again and again and they spoke about the blessings
<>obtained by offering oblation to their manes. Brahma-
<>datta gave the old Bra1hman2a plenty of wealth and
<>several villages and sent him fully satisfied. Then he
<>anointed his capable and fully qualified son Vis2vaksena
<>as King and with his retinue retired to Ma1nasa-saras
<>for penance.
<NI>3) {%A bird as his wife when he was King.%} When Brahma-
<>datta was the King of Ka1m3pilyapura a bird named
<>Tapani1ya1 become his friend. Her head was purple
<>coloured and body black. By and by this bird became
<>his wife. A daughter named Sarvasena1 was born to
<>Brahmadatta by his bird-wife. The King had sons by
<>other wives. Tapani1ya1 would wash her child and place
<>her in the cradle early in the morning and then
<>go to the forest and fly about in the air. She would
<>return in the evening and tell the King news all over
<>the world. It went on like this for a long time.
<NI>Once, when there was nobody in the room a prince
<>took Sarvasena1 from the cage and choked her to death.
<>Tapani1ya1 returned and seeing the dead body of her
<>daughter, waited for a long time. Finally she pecked
<>out the eyes of the prince and flew away into the air.
<>(Harivam3s4a, Chapter 20).

<HI>BRAHMADATTA IV. A king who ruled over the coun-
<>try of Ka1s4i. One night he saw two swans flying in the
<>air. They were of golden colour. There were a num-
<>ber of swans around them. They seemed like two
<>lightnings in the midst of white clouds. Brahmadatta
<>wished to see them again. His desire increased to such
<>an extent that he had desire for nothing else. He
<>consulted his ministers and made a lake. Every crea-
<>ture which seemed beautiful to him was brought to
<>that place. Hearing this those two swans also came
<>there. By and by he tamed them and asked them how
<>they got the golden colour. The swans said thus:
<>“Oh King! you might have heard of the famous moun-
<>tain Mandara, which is the field of games of the Devas
<>(gods). At the time of the churning of the milk sea
<>when this mountain was used as churn-drill, Amr2tam3
<>(celestial nectar of immortality) touched this moun-
<>tain. So the flowers, fruits and roots in the vicinity of
<>this mountain got the power of removing death, wrin-
<>kles etc. Candracu1d2a also will come to this mountain
<>occasionally to play on the top of it and so it became
<>more glorious than the mount Kaila1sa. One day
<>while Maha1deva was playing in those gardens he had
<>to leave on an urgent matter concerning Gods. So he
<>left Pa1rvati1 there and went away. Devi1 began to walk
<>about in those gardens with pangs of separation.
<>Spring season came. The Gan2as (servants of S4iva) and
<>the maids of Pa1rvati1 attended on her. One day Man2i-
<>pus2pes4vara, a Gan2a, hankered after Candralekha1,
<>daughter of Jaya1. She had been engaged in fanning
<>Pa1rvati1. Both were young and their figures suited each
<>other. Candralekha1 reciprocated his love. Under-
<>standing this Pin3ges4vara and Guhes4vara, two gan2as
<>looked at each other and smiled. Seeing this Devi1
<>looked round with anger to see what caused them to
<>smile. They squinted their eyes and showed Manipus2-
<>pe vara and Candralekha1 to Devi1. Because of separation
<>she had been much worried, weary and sad. So all
<>these affairs made her angry. She said: “It is good
<>that you behave like this when I am in this plight !
<>The two are making love. Seeing that, you are laughing.
<>May these two dotards be born on the earth and be-
<>come husband and wife there. Let you two who
<>laughed before me without humility also be born on
<>the earth, first as poor Bra1hman2as, and then as
<>Brahmaraks2as (demon friends), outcasts, robbers,
<>dogs with tails cut off and finally as various kind of
<>birds.” Thus she cursed the four of gan2as. Then a
<>gan2a named Dhu1rjat2a said to Pa1rvati1: “Devi1, this is
<>unfortunate. They have not commited any severe
<>crime.” Devi1 got angry at him and cursed him too:
<>“May you also be born on the earth as a human be-
<>ing.” After this Jaya the loved maid of Pa1rvati1 reques-
<>ted her for absolution from curse, for all. The Devi1
<>said that all of them would get absolution at the place
<>of penance of Brahma1 and other gods and would re-
<>turn here. Of these, Candralekha1 and her husband and
<>Dhu1rjat2a would be happy on the earth. The two
<>others would lead a miserable life on the earth.”
<>(Katha1saritsa1gara, Padma1vati1lam3baka, Taran3ga 1).
<HI>BRAHMADEVA. A warrior who fought on the side of

<HI>BRAHMA1DUM3BARA. A holy place in Kuruks2etra. In
<>Maha1bha1rata, Vana Parva, Chapter 83, Stanza 71, it
<>is said that this place is auspicious to Brahma1.
<HI>BRAHMAHATYA1. Killing a Bra1hman2a is called Brahma-
<>hatya1. In ancient India killing a Bra1hmin was consi-
<>dered to be a great sin. He, who committed this sin
<>had to build a hut in the forest and live there for a
<>period of twelve years, or make an alms-pot called
<>‘S4ivas4irodhvaja’ and wander about begging alms, to
<>purify his soul, or else to attempt to jump into a
<>blazing fire thrice with bowed head, or perform one
<>of the three sacrifices As4vamedha, Svarjit or Gosava.
<>If none of these was possible, he had to give all the

<HI>BRA1HMAN2A. (BRA1HMIN).
<NI>(1) {%Origin.%} Bra1hman2as, Ks2atriyas, Vais4yas, and S4u1dras
<>are the caturvarn2as or the four castes. The Pura1n2as say
<>that the four castes originated from different parts of
<>the body of Brahma1. See Manusmr2ti, Chapter 1,
<>Stanza 87
<Poem>“Sarvasya1sya tu sargasya
<>Guptyartham3 sa maha1dyutih2
<>Mukhaba1hu1ru1pajja1na1m3
<>Pr2thakkarma1n2yakalpayat.</Poem>
<NI>(With a view to sustain the world, Brahma1 ordered acti-
<>vities, for the four castes (Bra1hman2a Ks2atriya, Vais4ya,
<>and S4u1dra) who were born from his face, arms, thighs
<>and feet). From this statement it is seen that the Bra1h-
<>man2as were born from the face, Ks2atriyas from the
<>arms, Vais4yas from the thighs and Su1dras from the feet
<>of Brahma1.
<NI>2) {%The activities of a Bra1hman2a.%} The duties of a Bra1hman2a
<>are, performing sacrifice, and encouraging others to
<>perform sacrifice, learning Vedas and teaching Vedas,
<>giving gifts and getting remuneration. A Bra1hman2a has
<>two births in one life. Till the time of investiture with
<>the sacred string is one birth and from that period on-
<>wards is the second birth. So a Bra1hman2a is called
<>‘dvija’ or twice-born.
<NI>The Bra1hman2as were allowed to do the works of agri-
<>culture, keeping cows, trade and commerce and Kusi1da
<>(money-lending). Living on the interest of money
<>giving out as loan is Kusi1da. But they should not trade
<>on products from cow, jaggery, salt, lac and flesh.
<>The suffix ‘S4arma1’ should be added to the name of
<>Bra1hman2a. A Bra1hman2a can have four wives.
<HI>BRA1HMAN2A(M). See Veda.
<HI>BRAHMA1N2D2A. The word Brahma1n2d2a means the an2d2a
<>of Brahma1 (an2d2a-egg), the Supreme Being which is
<>the root of the origin of the universe. At a time long
<>ago, which is beyond reckoning, there was nothing but
<>an egg. This egg was split into two and from it a male [Page159-a+ 64]
<>child with the radiance of innumerable suns came out.
<>This wonderful child was helpless. After his birth he
<>cried for a while, because of hunger and thirst. He
<>got no help. There was nobody to feed it. So this
<>wonderful child looked up and lay there. This help-
<>less child was called ‘Vira1t2purus2a’ (the next emanation
<>from Brahma1) by those versed in the Pura1n2as. The
<>boy was so called because he was the most material
<>of materiality. But he was an atom of atoms and the
<>first figuration of God. From each pore of the skin of
<>the great vira1t2, who was the base of all the worlds a
<>universe came into being. Thus all the countless uni-
<>verses were born. Each universe has its own trinity of
<>Brahma1, Vis2n2u, S4iva and devas (the bright ones), the
<>protectors of the eight zones. One universe comprises
<>fourteen worlds or realms, from Pa1ta1la to the Brahma-
<>loka (from the nether world to the upper world or
<>the realm of Brahma1). Countless such universes exist.
<>Over and above all these universes there is Vaikun2t2ha,
<>and above Vaikun2t2ha there is another world with an
<>area of fifty crore yojanas (leagues), called ‘Goloka’ (the
<>world of cow). Only the two worlds Vaikun2t2ha and
<>the Goloka are eternal. Bhu1loka (the earth) consists
<>of seven islands and sixty-four peninsulas. There are
<>seven worlds above and seven worlds below. The seven
<>upper worlds are Bhu1loka, Bhuvarloka, Svarloka, Jana-
<>loka, Tapoloka. Satyaloka and Brahmaloka. Thus a
<>universe consists of fourteen worlds. This is the idea
<>given by the Pura1n2as about Brahma1n2d2a. (Devi1 Bha1ga-
<>vata, Skandhas 9).
<NI>Mention is made, in the Vis2n2u Pura1n2a, Am3s4a 2,
<>Chapter 7, about the construction of Brahma1n2d2a.
<NI>This Brahma1n2d2a, like the seed of the Kapittha tree
<>(Vitar--the wood-apple), is covered under, above and
<>all around by an2d2akat2a1ha (egg-shell). The an2d2a (egg)
<>is covered by water ten times the egg in volume. This
<>water-shell is covered by a shell of fire. Around the
<>shell of fire there is the shell of air and the air-shell
<>is covered with ether. Around the shell of ether there
<>is the shell of Egoism of darkness. Over the shell of
<>Egoism there is the shell of glory and the shell of
<>glory is covered by Prakr2ti, the Divine Nature. It is
<>immeasurable. Prakr2ti contains numberless Brah-
<>ma1n2d2as. As oil exists in sesame, God pervades the whole
<>universe. This is the connection between Prakr2ti and
<>Purus2a. (The Nature and the Supreme Spirit).

<HI>BRAHMAYAJN5A. A special sacrifice to be performed
<>by a Bra1hmin only. The rules and rituals of the sacri-
<>fice are given below:--
<NI>The Bra1hman2a should rinse his mouth three times.
<>Then he should wipe his mouth twice and face once.
<>Then he should take water in his hand and sprinkle
<>it on his hands and legs, and then sprinkle it on his
<>head, eyes, nose, ears, chest and forehead. After that
<>uttering the name of the place, date, time, star etc. he
<>should say to himself, “I am beginning to perform
<>Brahmayajn5a.” For this he should have to place two
<>darbhas (grass--Poa cynosuroides) in his right hand,
<>three in his left hand, one on the seat, one each on his
<>Brahma-string (sacred string worn by brahmins), hair
<>and legs, and then think of the time, place, etc. and say
<>to himself, “I am about to perform Brahmayajn5a to
<>please the gods known in the Su1ktas (hymns in the
<>Vedas) for the absolution of all my sins.” Then recite
<>Ga1yatri1 (spell) thrice. Then recite the following Vedic
<>mantras or spells, in the order given: Mantras beginn-
<>ing with; “Agnimi1le”; “Yadan3ga”; ‘Agnirvai’; ‘Maha1-
<>vrata1m’; ‘Pantha1 etacca’; ‘Sam3hitavida1maghavat’;
<>‘Maha1vratasya’; “Is2e tvorjje”, “Agna a1ya1hi” and s4anno-
<>devi1h2.” Then in conformity with the number of letters
<>used, he should recite the grammatic S4utra known as
<>Vr2ddhira1daic. (Devi1 Bha1gavata, Skandha 11).

<NI>1) {%Birth.%} The father of Br2haspati was An3giras, the
<>son of Brahma1. Brahma1 grew amorous, at the sight of
<>some celestial maids who were present at a sacrifice
<>performed by Rudra; and he had seminal flow.
<>Brahma1 put the semen in fire. From that fire the devas
<>such as Mari1ci, Bhr2gu, An3giras and others were born.
<>The name An3giras was given because he was born out
<>of an3ga1ra (live-coal). Eight sons were born to An3giras
<>by his wife Vasuda1. They were Utathya, Br2haspati,
<>Vayasya, S4a1nti, Ghora, Viru1pa, Sam3varta and Sudh-
<>anva1. All of them were sages who had attained one-
<>ness with the supreme Spirit by knowledge, and who
<>had been free from worldly pain. Of them Br2haspati,
<>Utathya and Sam3varta became famous through all
<>the worlds. In some pura1n2as Vasuda1, the mother of
<>Br2haspati, is given the name S4raddha1 also.
<NI>It is stated that Br2haspati had a sister named A1n3girasi1.
<>She was a follower of the Brahma1 cult. She became
<>the wife of Prabha1sa the last one of the eight Vasus.

<>A1n3girasi1 had the name Bha1numati1 also.</F>
<>Vis4vakarma1 was her son. (Bha1gavata Skandha 4, Maha1-

<NI>2) {%Is Br2haspati the son of Agni?%} In some pura1n2as
<>Br2haspati is described as the son of Agni. Its authority
<>is given below. An3giras, the father of Br2haspati was
<>once doing penance in his hermitage. When the fire of
<>penance increased the brightness of the real fire
<>decreased. Agni (fire) stood before An3giras and said
<>“Oh Lord! your brightness surpasses mine. From this
<>day onwards you are the real fire. So you shall be the
<>first fire and I will be the fire of Praja1pati which is the
<>second fire.”
<NI>Because of this boon of Agni, the devas (gods), recog-
<>nized An3giras also as a fire-god. So in some Pura1n2as
<>Br2haspati is mentioned as the son of Agni (fire-god).
<>(Bha1gavata, Skandha 4). [Page162-b+ 62]
<NI>3) {%Teacher of the Devas.%} The story of how Br2haspati
<>became the teacher of the devas, is given in the
<>Bha1s2a1bha1rata, Chapter 76 as shown below:
<NI>“The Suras and the asuras (the gods and the demons)
<>became enemies from time immemorial, regarding the
<>possession of wealth and prosperity in the three worlds.
<>To secure victory in the battles the gods made Br2has-
<>pati their teacher and likewise the asuras made S4ukra
<>their teacher.”
<NI>The devas and asuras began to fight for prosperity
<>and wealth. At that time the devas selected Br2haspati
<>and the asuras selected S4ukra, as their teacher.
<NI>4) {%The conjugality of Br2haspati.%} Ta1ra1 was the wife of
<>Br2haspati. She was very beautiful. Seeing Candra's

<>quarrels over this affair. Finally the devas intervened
<>and Ta1ra1 was given back to Br2haspati1. Budha was
<>born to Candra by Ta1ra1.
<NI>The individual called Ta1ra in the family of the monkeys,
<>was a son of Br2haspati. It is mentioned in Va1lmi1ki

<>(fires): In sacrifices the burnt offerings and the ghee

<>the wife of S4am3yu. The name of the wonderful being

<>the Agni is said to be the son of the Agni who is Praja1- [Page163-a+ 65]
<>pati Bharata. Thus Bharata got the famous name ‘the

<NI>5) {%Growing amorous on the wife of elder brother.%} A story
<>stating how Br2haspati begot a son by the wife of
<>Utathya is given in Skandha 9 of Bha1gavata. Mamata1,
<>the wife of Utathya, was pregnant. Br2haspati had
<>coition with her when her husband was away. The
<>mother and the child in the womb who opposed the
<>act of Br2haspati were cursed. Mamata1 gave birth to two
<>children. Fearing that her husband might cast her out
<>she left the son of Br2haspati in the forest and was
<>about to go, when there was a divine voice from above,
<>“Mu1d2he, Bharadva1jamimam bhara dva1jam3 Br2has-
<>pate.” “You senseless woman, bring up this one born

<>him up. After that the child was given to emperor
<>Bharata. The famous archer Dron2a was the son of this
<>Bharadva1ja.
<NI>6) {%Personation of Br2haspati.%} The enmity between the
<>devas and asuras increased day by day. S4ukra1ca1rya
<>the teacher of the asuras began to do penance before
<>S4iva in the Hima1layas, with a view to get a divine
<>spell or incantation to destroy the devas. The duration
<>of the penance was thousand years. Indra came to
<>know of this secret and sent his daughter Jayanti1 to
<>get the spell from S4ukra by deceit. She stayed with
<>S4ukra as his disciple and servant. Thousand years passed
<>by. S4iva appeared before S4ukra and gave him the spell,
<>capable of destroying the devas. When he was about
<>to return Jayanti1 accepted him as her husband. Be-
<>cause of his familiarity with her, of a long standing,
<>he could not refuse her request. S4ukra told her that
<>he would become her husband, for a period of ten
<>years and that during that period both of them would
<>be invisible to the world. Thus the couple began an
<>invisible life.
<NI>Br2haspati thought of making the best use of this period.
<>He personated himself as S4ukra1ca1rya and went to the
<>Asuras, who thinking that their teacher had returned
<>after a long penance gave him a loving and sincere
<>welcome. Br2haspati sat on the seat of S4ukra1ca1rya and
<>began to exhort the asuras in such a manner that [Page163-b+ 64]
<>within the period of ten years he was able to remove
<>factionalism and hatred from them.
<NI>At the expiry of ten years' invisible life S4ukra returned
<>having sent Jayanti1 away. The asuras saw two S4ukras
<>together and were amazed. They declared that the
<>real S4ukra was he who had been teaching them for the
<>last ten years. Being dismayed at the ingratitude of the
<>asuras he cursed them that they would shortly be
<>destroyed and then left the place. At this juncture
<>Br2haspati also assumed his real form and returned to
<>heaven. Thus the asuras became a people without a
<>leader like sheep without a shepherd. At last they
<>approached their teacher S4ukra who became their
<>teacher again, when they begged for his pardon. But
<>he said that his curse could not be recalled. But he
<>gave them absolution by saying that they would regain
<>their lost power during the time of Manu Sa1varn2i.
<>(Devi1 Bha1gavata, Skandha 4).
<NI>7) {%Cursing Ra1van2a.%} Once Ra1van2a was returning haugh-
<>ty and proud after having defeated the devas and con-
<>quered heaven, when Sulekha1 the daughter of Br2has-
<>pati, got terrified and ran away to hide herself from
<>him. Ra1van2a chased her and when she was caught he
<>tried to ravish her. Br2haspati got angry and cursed him.
<>“You, who have grown rank by the dart of Cupid,
<>will meet with death by the dart of Ra1ma”. (Kampa
<>Ra1ma1yan2a, Yuddha Ka1n2d2a).
<NI>8) {%Br2haspati and Hanu1ma1n.%} An5jana1 the mother of
<>Hanu1ma1n was a servant of Br2haspati in her previous
<>birth. Her name then was Pun5jika1sthali1. She once went
<>to fetch water. At that time many Vidya1dhara young
<>people, both male and female, came there and engaged
<>in amorous acts. Pun5jika1sthali1 witnessed these love
<>scenes for a long time and then returned home. It is
<>mentioned in Kampa Ra1ma1yan2a that Br2haspati
<>cursed her to be born in the next birth as a female
<>monkey.
<NI>An5jana1 gave birth to Hanu1ma1n. When he grew up
<>Hanu1ma1n desired to learn Vedas and S4a1stras (scrip-
<>tures). Hanu1ma1n approached Br2haspati to learn from
<>him. But Br2haspati was not prepared to teach a monkey
<>who jumped about everywhere. The disappointed
<>Hanu1ma1n went to the Sun, who asked him how it
<>could be done by him as he was engaged in travelling
<>without stop. Hanu1ma1n said that he would move in
<>front of the sun always. Thus Hanu1ma1n who had been
<>rejected by Br2haspati became the disciple of the Sun.
<>(Uttara Ra1ma1yan2a).

<NI>(10) {%Conclusion.%} Br2haspati was a deep thinker and one
<>who had firm convictions in many matters. He was a
<>man of vast knowledge. Every movement of the gods
<>had its origin in the brains of Br2haspati. There is no
<>philosophy which does not contain the exhortations

<>or kings or hermits.

<HI>BUDHA I. Son of Candra.
<NI>1) Birth. Br2haspati the teacher-priest of the devas
<>had a very beautiful wife named Ta1ra1. One day seeing
<>the handsome figure of Candra, Ta1ra1 became amorous.
<>Candra was the Disciple of Br2haspati. It was not right
<>on the part of the wife of the teacher to feel amorous
<>towards a disciple. Br2haspati came to know of the
<>affair and sending for Candra asked him to return his
<>wife. Both Candra and Ta1ra1 did not pay any heed to
<>Br2haspati. Both sides were about to engage in a battle.
<>The devas arrayed themselves on both sides. Seeing
<>that this was not an honourable thing some devas
<>tried for a compromise. Finally Candra returned Ta1ra1
<>to Br2haspati. At this time Ta1ra1 was pregnant. Both
<>Candra and Br2haspati claimed the paternity of the
<>child in the womb of Ta1ra1, who witnessed that Candra [Page165-a+ 65]
<>was the father of the child. All accepted the words of
<>Ta1ra1. Budha was the child Ta1ra1 gave birth to.
<NI>2) {%Marriage and birth of a child.%} Budha grew up to be
<>a youth. During the period the King Sudyumna one
<>day entered Kuma1ravana (a forest) and was changed to
<>a woman. When the King became a woman the name
<>adopted was Ila1, who grew amorous of Budha. The
<>famous King Puru1ravas was the son born to them.
<>(For more information see Ila1, Candra and Puru1-
<>ravas).

<>Carpus Seeds) and has a bow. (Agni Pura1n2a, Chap-
<>ter 51).
<NI>(2) Candra performed the christening ceremony of
<>Budha. (Bha1gavata Skandha 9).
<NI>(3) Budha is a luminous member of the assembly of
<>Brahma1. (M.B. Sabha Parva, Chapter 11, Stanza 29).
<HI>UDHA II. A hermit. Once he spoke about the impor-

<HI>BUDHA III. A scientist on ethics. He has written a

<HI>BUDHA IV. A Bra1hman2a who lived in Gaud2ades4a.
<>Budha who was the incarnation of all vices drank too
<>much and spent a whole night lying unconscious on
<>the veranda of the house of a harlot. His father became
<>anxious about him as he had not returned though the
<>night had advanced much. He searched everywhere
<>and finally came to the place where he was lying un-
<>conscious. He reviled the son, who getting angry
<>killed his father then and there.
<NI>After that he returned home. His mother advised him
<>to leave off his vices. His wife shed tears before him.
<>Seeing that his mother and wife were impediments
<>on his way, he put an end to their lives.
<NI>Once this Bra1hman2a carried away Sulabha1 the beauti-
<>ful wife of hermit Ka1labhu1ti and ravished her. Sulabha1
<>cursed him and made him a leper.
<NI>Budha wandered about and reached the city of King
<>S4u1rasena. On that day the King was about to go to
<>Heaven with the entire city by aeroplane. However
<>hard the pilots might try the plane would not move an
<>inch. Then the devas told the King: “Drive away
<>the leper there. The vices of that sinful murderer
<>have made the plane stationary.”
<NI>S4u1rasena who was kind and righteous, neared the leper
<>and cured him with catura1ks2ari1mantra (a spell of four
<>letters) and got ready to take him also to heaven.
<>(Gan2es4a Pura1n2a 1:76).
<HI>BUDDHA. The founder of Buddhism. Some consider
<>him to be an incarnation of Vis2n2u. In days of old in
<>a battle the devas were defeated by the asuras and the
<>gods approached Vis2n2u with their grievance. Maha1-
<>vis2n2u incarnated as the son of S4uddhodana with the [Page165-b+ 65]
<>name Gautamabuddha (Siddha1rtha). Then he went
<>to the asuras and made them reject the Vedas and the
<>laws thereof. All the Daityas (Asuras) became Bud-
<>dhists. There is a story in Agnipura1n2a, Chapter 16,
<>that thus it was the purpose of Buddha to convert
<>every asura to Buddhism and send him to hell.
<NI>The story given above is in accordance with the Pura1-
<>n2as. The following are the facts gained by historic
<>investigations.
<NI>Gautama Buddha was born in B.C. 560, in Kapilavastu
<>near the Hima1layas. His father was S4uddhodana. He
<>was born in the family of the S4a1kyas. The word ‘S4a1kya’
<>is another form of the word Ks2atriya. The real name of
<>Buddha was Siddha1rtha. S4uddhodana brought up his
<>son in such a way that he should not be subjected to
<>any sort of mental pain or worry. So he kept Buddha
<>aloof from the outer world. Thus he spent his childhood
<>in comfort and pleasure. Once by chance he happened
<>to see a sick man, an old man and a dead body. The
<>sight made him thoughtful. He began to think upon
<>a way to remove sorrow and pain from the world and
<>to bring about peace and comfort.
<NI>The change that appeared in the son worried the
<>father. So at the age of sixteen he made Siddha1rtha
<>marry Yas4odhara1. A son was born to them. But the
<>mind of Siddha1rtha was restless, distressed and agi-
<>tated. One day Siddha1rtha discarded everything and
<>went out of the palace alone.
<NI>Siddha1rtha wandered from place to place learning
<>from various teachers. But he did not find peace.
<>Once on a full-moon day while he was sitting in medi-
<>tation under a banian tree he got ‘Bodha’. (insight or
<>conviction). From that day onwards he began to be
<>known by the name ‘Buddha’. After that he came to
<>Ka1s4i, and told his disciples how he got Bodha or con-
<>viction. The number of his followers increased day by
<>day. Thus Buddhism came into being. Buddha said
<>that the reason for pain and sorrow in the world was
<>desire and that sorrow could be exterminated only
<>by controlling and overcoming all desire. To attain
<>Eternal Bliss one should be true and righteous in
<>thought, deed and word and that ‘Not Killing’ was
<>the foundation of righteousness. Buddhism spread
<>everywhere in Bha1rata.
<NI>Gautama Buddha died at the age of eighty.
<HI>BUDDHAGHOS2A. An ancient Sanskrit poet. It is be-
<>lieved that he lived in the 4th century A.D. The
<>most important work of this poet who was a Buddhist,
<>is ‘Padyacu1d2a1man2i’. The life of Buddha is described
<>in this Book in ten Ka1n2d2as (sections). A similarity to
<>the poems of Ka1lida1sa and As4vaghos2a is seen in this
<>poem. Buddhaghos2a is said to have reached Ceylon
<>in 357 A.D. and translated some Buddhist literature.
<HI>BUDDHI. A wife of Dharmadeva. In Vis2n2u Pura1n2a
<>Am3s4a 1, Chapter 7, it is mentioned that Dharmadeva
<>had married thirteen daughters of Daks2a. They are
<>S4raddha1, Laks2mi1, Dhr2ti, Tus2t2i, Medha1, Pus2t2i, Kriya1,

<HI>BUDDHIKA1MA. An attendant of S4ubrahman2ya. (M.B.
<>Salya Parva, Chapter 46, Stanza 12).
<HI>BUDDHISM. (BUDDHA-MATA). The origin of Bud-
<>dhism was in the sixth century B.C. This new religion
<>was shaped out of Hinduism that prevailed at that
<>time. Buddhism which had its origin in North India,
<>prevailed everywhere in India up to the eleventh [Page166-a+ 65]
<>century A.D. During that period there was an over-
<>flow of this religion to the other parts of Asia. Now
<>this religion has its full sway over the people of Burma.
<>90% of the population there, are adherents of Bud-
<>dhism. 80% of the people of Thailand, 60% of the
<>people in Ceylon and Japan and 17% in China are
<>now Buddhists. In India, Pakistan and the Philippines
<>the Buddhists are only a minority. Even though Indo-
<>nesia is a Muslim country there is a small class of
<>Buddhists there. Combodia, Lavos and Tibet are consi-
<>dered to be Buddhist countries. In Nepal there are
<>only two religions, Hinduism and Buddhism. In count-
<>ries outside Asia there are followers of Buddhism.
<>Statistics reveal that there are one hundred and sixty-
<>five thousand Buddhists in North America, one
<>hundred and thirtyfive thousand in South America,
<>and ten thousand in Europe. The number of Buddhists
<>in the world today is 150.31 crores.

<HI>CAKRA(M3) I. Sudars4ana Cakra (disc) of Maha1vis2n2u.

<>There is a story in the Maha1bha1rata as to how the

<>Maha1vis2n2u. While S4ri1 Kr2s2n2a and Arjuna were picnic-
<>ing on the shores of the Yamuna1 Agnideva went to

<HI>CAKRAVYU1HA. A Phalanx.
<NI>A particular pattern of army formation on ground. In

<>The great preceptor made the Cakravyu1ha and posted

<>Abhimanyu. Duryodhana's son, Laks2man2a, led the
<>onrush. In the centre (of the Cakravyu1ha) stood
<>Duryodhana in the company of heroes like Karn2a,
<>Dus4s4a1sana and Kr2pa. And, at the head of the army
<>stood Dron2a, the captain of the army, who looked like
<>the rising sun. Then there was the King of Sindhu,

<>front of them thirty Kauravas, all of them equal to
<>Devas. On the flanks were great heroes like S4alya,

<HI>CAKRIKA. A forest hunter. The following story is

<>Pura1n2a.
<NI>Devoted much to his parents, Cakrika was a great
<>devotee of Vis2n2u. He used to worship Vis2n2u daily with

<>himself tasting them. One day while thus tasting a

<>the Dva1raka1 temple, and thus attained salvation.

<NI>3) {%Former life.%} The following story is told in the
<>Ma1rkan2d2eya Pura1n2a about the former life of Ca1ks2us2a

<HI>CA1KS2US2I1. An art, the study of which will enable one

<HI>CA1KYA1RKU1TTU. A very important art practised in
<>temples mainly in Kerala. It is called so because it is
<>exhibited by Ca1kya1rs (a sect attached to temples). In
<>important temples a place called Ku1ttambala is set
<>apart for displaying the art. The Ku1ttu now prevalent
<>in Kerala temples is not exactly of its old form. Kula-
<>s4ekharavarma1 peruma1l (elected ruler of old Kerala)
<>effected some changes in the old art form, and Bha1skara-
<>ravivarma1 peruma1l and Tolakavi made further reforms
<>in it. The art of Ku1ttu was evolved with the object of
<>relating pura1n2ic stories in such a manner as will help
<>devotional feelings grow in man.
<NI>The main aim of the Ku1ttu is to narrate pura1n2ic
<>stories in such a way as to instil Bhakti in the hearers
<>and to create an interest in them in the Pura1n2as. The
<>Ca1kya1r (the man who gives the discourse who usually
<>belongs to the Brahmin sect called Ca1kya1rs) tells the
<>story in a very interesting way, reciting appropriate
<>passages from the Pura1n2as and illustrating them with
<>apt examples and similes. The narration is from the
<>beginning till end humorous. The Ca1kya1r who delivers
<>the discourse is usually a man with a good sense of
<>humour and a sense of what is odd, strange or eccentric
<>in nature and human character. There are three varie- [Page168-a+ 65]
<>ties of Ku1ttu: Prabandham3 Ku1ttu, Nam3biar Ku1ttu and
<>Ku1t2ia1t2t2am. Of these the first is narrative (A1khya1na-
<>para) while in the second and third type, the element
<>of acting is more important (Abhinayapara).
<HI>CALA1. Laks2mi1devi1. The following story is told in the
<>Devi1-bha1gavata as to how this name came to be
<>applied to the devi1. Once Revanta the very handsome
<>son of Su1ryadeva came to Vaikun2t2ha riding Uccaih2-
<>s4ravas, Indra's horse. Devi1, who was at that time with
<>Vis2n2u gazed for a few minutes in wonder at the horse.
<>She did not, therefore, attend to Vis2n2u's talk. Angered
<>at this Vis2n2u told the devi1: “Since your eyes find
<>enjoyment on unnecessary things and move about
<>among such objects, you shall be called from today
<>onwards Rama1 and Cala1. Also you will be born as a
<>mare on earth. Accordingly Maha1laks2mi1 was born as
<>a mare on the banks of river Sarasvati1, and regained
<>her old form only after delivering a son by Vis2n2u. That
<>son was Ekavi1ra, founder of the Hehaya kingdom. (Devi1
<>Bha1gavata, Skandha 6).

<HI>CAMPU1. A literary form of presenting a story in a
<>mixture of prose and verse, and abounding in beauti-
<>ful descriptions: “Gadyapadyamayam3 ka1vyam3 Cam-
<>pu1rityabhidhi1yate”. Over and above prose and verse
<>the Campu1s use dan2d2akas (very long, involved and
<>poetical prose). This literary form had its origin
<>first in Sanskrit and other Indian languages adopted it
<>with some variations. For instance, though prose in
<>Sanskrit Campu1s is really prose, that in Malayalam
<>is something akin to metrical prose. There are more
<>than two hundred Campu1s in Malayalam. The most
<>famous of the Campu1 writers in Malaya1lam are
<>Punam Namboothiri, Maha1man3galam Namboothiri
<>and Ni1lakan2t2ha.

<HI>CA1N2AKYA (KAUT2ILYA). The famous author of
<>“Arthas4a1stra” a treatise on political economy.
<NI>Eastern and western scholars have made exhaustive
<>researches on this intellectual giant of ancient India,
<>Ca1n2akya. But, nothing definite has yet been established
<>about his time or life. Indians have accepted as a fact
<>the traditional legend that he was a minister of Candra-
<>gupta, the founder of the Maurya dynasty. It is also
<>firmly believed that it was this mighty intellect of a
<>brahmin who made Candragupta a powerful emperor
<>and steered the ship of his state. The phrase ‘Ca1n2akya's
<>kut2ilani1ti’ (crooked tactics) has become proverbial.
<>Some scholars hold the opinion that he came to be
<>called ‘Kaut2ilya’ because of his Kut2ila (crooked)
<>tactics; but evidence to establish this view-point is yet
<>to be adduced.
<NI>It is his famous Arthas4a1stra, which has made Ca1n2akya's
<>name a world famous one. The great German thinker
<>Jolly has described the Arthas4a1stra as a unique composi-
<>tion in the Sanskrit language. And, Johann Meyer, the
<>great western scholar has the following to say about
<>the book:--
<NI>“Kaut2ilya's Arthas4a1stra is not one single text, it is a
<>collection and collateration of all books in ancient
<>India. To study the greatness of the theories and
<>principles contained in it today requires continuous and
<>constant study at least for twenty years and the un-
<>remitting efforts of a scholar with unabounded know-
<>ledge are required.” [Page169-a+ 65]
<>Even his name has not yet been confirmed beyond
<>doubts. Kaut2ilya is also called Vis2n2ugupta, Ca1n2akya,
<>Dramila, and An3gula. Modern researches place the
<>date of the Arthas4a1stra at near about B.C. 400. In
<>Vis4a1khadatta's Mudra1ra1ksasa (drama) the name
<>Kaut2ilya is used.
<NI>The Authors of Ni1tis4a1stra maintain that the name of
<>the author of Arthas4a1stra was Vis2n2ugupta. It may be
<>gathered from the Mudra1ra1ks2asa that he became famous
<>as Ca1n2akya as he was the son of Can2aka, and that
<>he possessed deep erudition in Ni1tis4a1stra, Dharma-
<>s4a1stra and astrology. There is another legend to the
<>effect that this brahmin (Ca1n2akya) belonged to Ka1n5ci1-
<>pura and that he migrated to Pa1t2ali1putra, the seat of
<>learning and scholarship. As Ka1mandaka, who lived
<>in the fourth century B.C. has eulogised Ca1n2akya in
<>his ni1tis4a1stra it may be surmised that Ca1n2akya lived
<>sometime near about that. Candragupta and Ca1n2akya
<>are referred to in the Vis2n2u and the Va1mana Pura1n2as.
<>Kaut2ilya was keen-eyed and scholar par excellence.
<>“Arthas4a1stra” is a great book written by him after
<>thoroughly examining the viewpoints of previous
<>scholars, and then establishing his own views and
<>theories. From the Das4akuma1racarita of Dan2d2i1 (A.D.
<>700) it may be seen that there are 6000 verses in the
<>Anus2t2up metre (eight letters in one line) in the Artha-
<>s4a1stra composed by Vis2n2ugupta for the use of Candra-
<>gupta Maurya. The Artha a1stra has been held in praise
<>by Ba1n2abhat2t2a who lived in the 7th Century, Soma-
<>devasu1ri, who lived in the 10th century and also by
<>Hemacandra, the author of Ka1vya1nus4a1sana, who lived
<>in the 12th century. The book is a discourse on the
<>principles and practices that are necessary to establish
<>a strong government of an ideal empire. Ca1n2akya has
<>mentioned in his book with respect the old scholars like
<>Manu, S4ukra, Br2haspati, Na1ra1yan2a, Ka1tya1yana and
<>Govinda di1ks2ita. (For the pura1n2ic story about Ca1n2akya
<>see Vararuci, Para 6).
<HI>CAN5CALA1KS2I1. A notorious harlot who lived in dva1-

<HI>CAN2D2AKA. A forest hunter who attained salvation by
<>the worship of S4ivalin3ga. (Phallus of S4iva).
<NI>While once hunting in the woods Can2d2aka saw a S4iva
<>temple in ruins, and the S4ivalin3ga lying on the ground

<>lin3ga. To his query as to whether the s4a1stra permitted
<>a hunter like himself to worship S4ivalin3ga, Sim3haketu
<>answered there were injunctions in the S4a1stras for
<>people like him (Can2d2akas) to instal S4ivalin3ga on
<>rock and then worship it daily, the worshipper wear-
<>ing ashes from the burning gha1t2 on his body. Accord-
<>ingly Can2d2aka installed the lin3ga on a rock and began
<>worshipping it daily along with his wife, Pulindi1. But
<>after sometime there was left no ashes in the burning
<>gha1t2 and Can2d2aka and Pulindi1 discussed between them
<>the means to get ashes for the next day. Then Pulindi1
<>suggested that she should burn herself to ashes and that
<>Can2d2aka should continue worshipping the S4ivalin3ga
<>wearing on his body the ashes. Very unwillingly
<>Can2d2aka agreed with the sad proposal of his wife, and
<>the next day he worshipped the S4ivalin3ga wearing on
<>his body the ashes of Pulindi1. And, at the close of the

<>than formerly. Moreover a vima1na descended from the

<HI>CAN2D2AMAHA1SENA.
<NI>1) {%General.%} A very prominent and reputed king of
<>Ujjayini. Va1savadatta1, wife of Udayana was his
<>daughter.
<NI>2) {%The name Can2d2amaha1sena.%} His real name was Maha1-
<>sena. After ascending the throne he began doing
<>tapas to get a noble wife and a good sword. When he
<>cut his own flesh and made an offering of the same to
<>the sacred fire after a long period of tapas the Devi1
<>became pleased, and offered him a sword of exceptional
<>excellence and said that as long as he had that sword
<>with him no enemy would defeat him. The Devi1 also
<>assured him that ere long An3ga1ravati1, daughter of the
<>asura called An3ga1raka and the fairest damsel in the
<>three worlds would become his wife. Since he (Maha1-
<>sena) did the most austere rites and rituals, the Devi1
<>said, he would come to be called Can2d2amaha1sena.
<>Afterwards the sword given by the Devi1 and the ele-
<>phant called nad2a1giri proved as successful and effective
<>to him as were Vajra1yudha and his elephant Aira1vata
<>to Indra. [Page170-a+ 65]
<NI>3) {%Marriage.%} One day, during a hunting expedition
<>a boar escaped unhurt by the king's arrows and after
<>turning turtle his chariot it went into a cave. The king
<>in great anger followed the pig into the cave, and going
<>forward some distance he saw a beautiful animal where-
<>upon he seated himself on the side of a tank. There he
<>saw, amidst a number of women an exceptionally
<>beautiful woman moving about. Casting her charming
<>eyes glistering with love the woman approached the
<>King. During their conversation, when the king told
<>her about himself she began crying. To the query as
<>to the reason for her crying she answered as follows:--
<>“The boar you saw is my father, An3ga1raka1sura. His
<>body is harder than even diamond, and none can inflict
<>any injury on it. The women you see around are
<>princesses captured by my father from various palaces
<>for my help, and my name is An3ga1ravati1. My father
<>became a Ra1ks2asa as the result of a curse; he is now
<>asleep in the assumed form of a boar. On awaking he
<>is sure to eat you for food, and it was that thought which
<>brought tears to my eyes”. To these words of An3ga1ra-
<>vati1, the king replied as follows:--“If you have any
<>love for me you would go to your father and begin
<>crying when he wakes up, and to his question as to
<>why you are crying you should reply that thoughts
<>about your future in the event of your fathers's death
<>brought tears in your eyes.”
<NI>An3ga1ravati1 agreed to the above proposal of the King.
<>She hid the King in a safe place and went and stood
<>by the side of her father. Everything happened as was
<>anticipated and An3ga1raka consoled his daughter by
<>saying that none would be able to kill him whose
<>body was as hard and strong as diamond, there being
<>only one opening through which he might be struck on
<>the left wrist which might be concealed by the bow.
<>Can2d2amaha1sena who heard the above talk came out of
<>his hiding place and challenged the asura to war. He
<>raised his left hand to show the sign, “just coming,
<>please wait”, and the king then shot his arrow through
<>the opening on his left wrist. The asura fell dead.
<NI>4) {%Birth of Va1savadatta1:%} And, now Can2d2amaha1sena
<>married An3ga1ravati1 and returned to his kingdom with
<>his wife. Two sons, Gopa1laka and Pa1laka were born to
<>the royal couple, and the King celebrated an Indro-
<>tsava for the welfare and prosperity of the children.
<>Pleased at the utsava Indra appeared to the king in
<>sleep and blessed him saying that a daughter would be
<>born to him and that her son would be crowned empe-
<>ror of the Vidya1dharas. Accordingly a daughter was
<>born to the King, and she was called Va1savadatta1.
<>(given by Va1sava, i.e. Indra). Udayana married Va1sava-
<>datta1. (See Udayana).

<HI>CANDRA IV. (CANDRA DEVA).
<NI>1) {%Birth.%} The child born to Atri by Anasuya1. (See
<>Puru1ravas). In Skandha 4 of the Devi1bha1gavata it is

<NI>2) {%Candra led married life with the wife of his guru.%} Ta1ra1,
<>the very beautiful wife of Br2haspati, preceptor of the
<>Devas happened to reach Candra's home during her
