<>with each other at first sight and lived in conjugal
<>happiness. And thus days passed by. Br2haspati, being
<>informed of the fact on enquiry, deputed his disciples
<>to bring Ta1ra1 back, but to no purpose. Br2haspati sent
<>his disciples again and again to Ta1ra1, but all to no
<>purpose. Then Br2haspati himself went to the house of
<>Candra and invited Ta1ra1, this time also to no purpose.
<>Enraged at this the Deva guru spoke to Candra as
<>follows: “The brahmin-killer, gold-thief, drunkard, he
<>who marries another's wife and he who associates him-
<>self with the above three types are responsible for the
<>most terrible five sins, and you, therefore, are not fit
<>enough to reside in Devaloka. Unless you return my
<>wife to me I will curse you.” None of the threats of
<>Br2haspati could shake Candra. He told the Devaguru
<>that Ta1ra1 who had gone to his house on her own accord
<>would also leave him when she was satiated with him.
<>These words of Candra made Br2haspati all the more
<>angry. He returned home and waited sometime more
<>for Ta1ra1's return. But, he got disappointed, and getting
<>impatient he started again for Candra's house. But,
<>this time the gatekeepers did not let him in.
<NI>Terribly angry at the cruel rebuff Br2haspati sought
<>help of Indra. Indra sent word to Candra asking him
<>to send Ta1ra1 back home or be prepared for war. Even
<>then Candra refused to yield, and Indra started for
<>war against Candra. But, there was somehow some dif-
<>ference among the devas about all this, and the news
<>reached the asuras. At once S4ukra, preceptor of the
<>asuras and an old enemy of Br2haspati met Candra and [Page171-b+ 65]
<>assured him all support in case war broke out between
<>Indra and Candra. He also strongly advised Candra
<>not to return Ta1ra1 to Br2haspati. And, ultimately a
<>fierce war began between Indra and Candra. All acti-
<>vities in the world were thrown into confusion and
<>chaos. At this Brahma1 on his ham3sa (swan) came to
<>the scene and admonished Candra and S4ukra. They
<>could not but obey Brahma1 and so were forced to stop
<>fighting. Moreover, Candra returned Ta1ra1 to Br2has-
<>pati.
<NI>The quarrel and fighting thus ended for the time
<>being, but another problem cropped up. At the time
<>Candra returned Ta1ra1 to Br2haspati she was carrying,
<>and Br2haspati was not aware of the fact. And at last
<>Ta1ra1 delivered an exceptionally beautiful male child.
<>The naming ceremony of the child was duly performed,
<>Br2haspati himself acting as its father. When the news
<>reached Candra he sent a messenger to Br2haspati
<>claiming the child was his. Br2haspati too claimed its
<>fatherhood. This controversy developed almost to the

<>went to Br2haspati's house and questioned Ta1ra1 as to
<>who really was her child's father, and she named Candra.
<>Upon this Brahma1 asked Br2haspati to release the child
<>to Candra. Br2haspati did so. (Devi1 Bha1gavata, Pratha-
<>ma Skandha).
<NI>3) {%Wives of Candra.%} Candra took twentyseven daugh-
<>ters of Daks2a as his wives. (Devi1 Bha1gavata, Saptama
<>Skandha). These twentyseven wives are the twenty-
<>seven stars. Candra circumambulates Maha1meru along
<>with these, his twentyseven wives (Stars). (M.B. Vana
<>Parva, Chapter 163, Verse 33). The names of the
<>twentyseven wives are given hereunder: As4vini1, Bha-
<>ran2i1, Kr2ttika1, Rohin2i1, Mr2gas4iras, A1rdra1, Punarvasu,
<>Pus2ya, A1s4les2a1, Janakam3, Pha1lguni1, Uttarapha1lguni1,
<>Hasta, Citra1, Sva1ti, Vis4a1kha1, Anura1dha1, Jyes2t2ha1,
<>Mu1la1, Purva1s2a1d2ha1, Uttara1s2a1d2ha1, S4ron2a1, S4ravis2t2ha,
<>Pracetas, Pu1rvapros2t2hapada1, Uttarapros2t2hapada1,
<>Revati1.
<NI>{%Solar eclipse according to the Pura1n2as.%} The Devas and
<>the asuras jointly churned Ks2i1ra1bdhi wherefrom emerg-
<>ed Dhanvantari with the Amr2takumbha (pot of nectar).
<>(See Amr2tam).
<NI>But an asura ma1ya1vi1 (magician) called Saim3hikeya
<>absconded to Pa1ta1la with the Amr2takumbha which
<>nobody noticed as everybody was busy with dividing
<>other divine objects. Only after the ma1ya1vi1's disap-
<>pearance was it noticed that the Amr2ta Kumbha was
<>missing. At once Maha1vis2n2u assumed the figure of a
<>beautiful woman, got back the Kumbha and gave it to
<>the devas. The devas began drinking the amr2tam when,
<>at the instance of some other devas, Saim3hikeya, the
<>ma1ya1vi1 assuming the form of an old brahmin reached
<>svarga, got a share of the amr2ta and began to drink it.
<>Su1rya and Candra (Sun and Moon) who were on
<>guard at the gates divined the secret of the ‘old
<>brahmin’ and informed Maha1vis2n2u about it. He cut
<>the throat of the pseudo-brahmin with his Sudars4ana
<>Cakra. But, half of the nectar he had drunk stayed
<>above the throat and the other half below it. Therefore,
<>though the head and the trunk were severed they
<>remained alive. These two parts, in course of time,
<>evolved as Ra1hu and Ketu.
<NI>When the throat was cut some blood as well as some
<>amr2ta dropped on two places on the ground, and they [Page172-a+ 64]
<>became the red onion and the white onion respecti-
<>vely. Some vaidika brahmins used to consider the red
<>onion objectionable for consumption as it was evolved
<>from blood, while the white onion was considered
<>usable as it was evolved from amr2tam.
<NI>R2a1hu and Ketu still maintain their hatred for Su1rya
<>and Candra who had betrayed the asura, who, disguis-
<>ed as brahmin tried to drink the amr2ta. Eclipse is the
<>phenomenon of Ra1hu and Ketu swallowing Su1rya and
<>Candra as and when opportunity presents itself for it.
<>But, since the throat is severed from the body, Su1rya
<>and Candra thus swallowed get out through the throat.
<>That is the reason why Su1rya and Candra become
<>visible after the eclipse in over. (Kampa Ra1ma1yan2a,
<>Yuddha Ka1n2d2a and Bha1gavata As2t2ama Skandha).
<NI>5) {%Candra besame Calf.%} Once emperor Pr2thu trans-
<>formed Bha1midevi1 into a cow and milked from her all
<>things and provisions. On that occasion it was Brahma1,
<>who acted as Calf. And, following Pr2thu when the
<>R2s2is milked the cow Candra served as Calf. (For
<>details see Pr2thu).
<NI>6) {%Waxing and waning of Candra, the pura1n2ic story regard-
<>ding it.%} Of the twentyseven daughters of Daks2a whom
<>Candra had married he loved Rohin2i1 much more than
<>the other twentysix wives, and so kept her always with
<>him. This annoyed the twentysix wives, who complained
<>about it to Daks2a. Daks2a's advice to Candra to treat
<>all the wives on an equal footing had no effect on him.
<>So, the twentysix neglected wives again complained to
<>Daks2a as follows: “We shall stay in the a1s4rama and
<>serve you. Soma (Candra) does not associate with us,
<>he will not accept your advice.”
<NI>Though Daks2a warned Candra a second time, that too
<>had no effect on him. So the twentysix wives, for the
<>third time, complained to Daks2a. Daks2a got angry at
<>this and cursed that Candra should suffer from tuber-
<>culosis. Thus Candra was afflicted by consumption.
<>Though Candra performed many a yajn5a to get cured
<>of the fell disease, they did not produce the desired
<>effect. Candra remaining a tubercular patient, the
<>growth of medicinal plants stopped with the result that
<>all living things contracted consumption. When people
<>began becoming thinner, the devas asked Candra for
<>an explanation, and he told them all the details. They
<>then sought the help of Daks2a, who gave Candra
<>redemption from the curse by ordaining that if he dived
<>in the Sarasvati1 ti1rtha in the western sea he would be
<>free from consumption for half of every month. Thence-
<>forth Candra made it a practice to dive in the Saras-
<>vati1ti1rtha and that is the reason why Candra is exempt-
<>ed from Ks2ayaroga for fifteen days. (M.B. S4alya Parva,
<>Chapter 35).
<NI>7) {%Candra--King of stars and of medicines.%} During the
<>reign of emperor Pr2thu, he changed Bhu1midevi1 into a
<>cow. Later the R2s2is also changed bhu1midevi1 into a
<>cow and milked her. It was Candra who served as
<>calf then. Pleased at this Brahman crowned Candra as
<>king of the stars and medicines. (Harivam3s4a, Chapter
<>4, Verse 2).

<NI>(1) Candra is 11,000 sq. yojanas in area, 33,000 yojanas
<>in circumference and a volume of 5,900 cubic yojanas.
<>(M.B. Bhi1s2ma Parva, Chapter 12).

<>Sumati to Subrahman2ya (S4alya Parva, Chapter 45,
<>Verse 32).
<NI>(3) Candra once made a discourse on the superior
<>qualities of brahmins to S4ambara1sura. (Anus4a1sana
<>Parva, Chapter 36, Verse 13, Southern Text).
<NI>(4) All welfare and prosperity accrue to him, who on

<>of bread in copper vessels with honey poured into it.
<>(Anus4a1sana Parva, Chapter 36, Verse, 13, Southern
<>Text).
<NI>(5) Candra is one of the as2t2a-vasus. Candra had
<>four sons, Varcas, S4is4ira, Pra1n2a and Raman2a by his
<>wife named Manohara1. (A1di Parva, Chapter 66,
<>Verse 18).
<NI>(6) Abhimanyu was Candra's son, Varcas, reborn as
<>the son of Arjuna. (See Abhimanyu).

<HI>CANDRAHA1SA. Son of King Sudha1rmika of Kerala.
<>The following story about him is told in Jaimini As4va-
<>medha Parva.
<NI>Candraha1sa, born under the star Mu1lam had a sixth
<>finger on the left foot indicative of poverty and of all
<>other evils. And, therefore, on his birth enemies killed
<>Sudha1rmika and his wife followed him soon to the
<>other world. The child thus left an orphan was taken
<>to Kaun2d2alakapuri1 by a female inmate of the palace.
<>But unfortunately the woman died within three years
<>of the above incident. The child, just for very existence,
<>took to begging. After sometime some women jointly
<>took up charge of the boy. Once he went to the house
<>of Dhr2s2t2abuddhi, minister of Kaun2d2ala where a feast
<>was in progress. The munis present there were impres-
<>sed by Candraha1sa and prophesied that he would be-
<>come a King. To Dhr2s2t2abuddhi, the munis said that the
<>child would guard his wealth. Angry and suspicious at
<>the prophesy of the munis Dhr2s2t2abuddhi asked his men
<>to kill the boy. They led him to the forest. On the
<>way Candraha1sa got a S4a1lagra1ma (a small sacred stone
<>which represented some upadevata1) which he applied
<>very devotedly to his face. They did not in fact kill the
<>boy, but cut off his sixth finger on the left foot and
<>showed it to their master.
<NI>Thus left alive in the forest by the murderers the
<>boy was roaming about when the King af Kalinda on [Page173-a+ 65]
<>a hunting expedition saw the destitute boy, and taking
<>pity on him took him back with him to the palace.
<>The boy was put under the care of Medha1vati1, the
<>King's consort. The king named him Candraha1sa. Since
<>the King took a special interest in the education of
<>the child he became adept in all arts and sciences. At
<>the age of twelve Candraha1sa, with the permission of
<>the king, set out on a triumphal tour, and the king
<>welcomed Candraha1sa on his return after his glorious
<>victory that brought him wealth.
<NI>At the instance of the Kalinda king, Candraha1sa, sent by
<>his messengers tribute to the Kaun2d2ala king, who, under-
<>standing from them that so much of wealth had been
<>brought to the Kalinda king by Candraha1sa started at
<>once for the Kalinda palace. On being told the whole
<>story about Candraha1sa, Dhr2s2t2abuddhi, the minister of
<>the King of Kaun2d2ala made up his mind to do away with
<>him somehow or other for which he despatched a letter
<>to his son, Madana, through Candraha1sa.
<NI>Candraha1sa on his way to Kaun2d2ala slept on the banks
<>of a river. Campakama1lini1, the daughter of the King
<>of Kaun2d2ala and Vis2aya1, daughter of Dhr2s2t2abuddhi
<>happened to go to the river bank where Candraha1sa
<>was sleeping. Campakama1lini1, who became subject to
<>great love for Candraha1sa removed the anklets on her
<>feet and approached him slowly, and when she took up
<>and read a letter she found lying beside him, she felt
<>awe and sorrow to find therein a suggestion to poison
<>him to death. Without wasting much time in thought she
<>changed the word ‘vis2amasmai’ (give him poison) to
<>‘Vis2aya1smai’ (give him Vis2aya1) and put it in its former
<>place. After some time when Candraha1sa awoke from
<>sleep and reached Kaun2d2ala city and delivered the letter
<>to Madana its content pleased him very much. As for
<>Vis2aya1, she was spending her days in constant prayer
<>that Candraha1sa should marry her. In the cir-
<>cumstances the marriage of Candraha1sa and Vis2aya1 was
<>solemnised on the earliest auspicious day.
<NI>In due course Dhr2s2t2abuddhi returned to Kaun2d2ala with
<>all the wealth he could lay his hands upon after capturing
<>Kalinda. He seethed with grief and rage at the news
<>that Vis2aya1 had been married to Candraha1sa. All the
<>explanations offered by Madana did not satisfy him. He
<>persisted in his determination to kill Candraha1sa.
<NI>The evil-minded Dhr2s2t2abuddhi one day asked Candra-
<>ha1sa to go and worship at the Devi1 temple after having
<>made arrangements for his murder there. But, as fate
<>would have it, it was Madana who, instead of Candra-
<>ha1sa, was killed. Even before the above incident had
<>happened the Kaun2dala King had married his daughter
<>Candrama1lini1 to Candraha1sa, and after relinquishing
<>the kingdom also in his favour the King took to the forest
<>to lead a hermit's life.
<NI>The above developments added fuel to the fire of anger
<>in respect of Dhr2s2t2abuddhi, and when he saw in the
<>temple precincts, his son's corpse he became well-nigh
<>mad. Crying out that all this was the result of his having
<>troubled the Vais2n2ava Candraha1sa he dashed his head
<>against the pillars of the temple and died.
<NI>Candraha1sa was grieved over the death of Dhr2s2t2abuddhi.
<>He worshipped Devi1 offering his flesh in the sacrificial
<>fire. Devi1 appeared and granted him two boons which
<>helped him to bring Dhr2s2t2abuddhi and Madana back to
<>life. And, when the Kalinda king and his wife, fearing the
<>wicked Dhr2s2t2abuddhi were about to end their lives in [Page173-b+ 65]
<>flaming fire, Dhr2s2t2abuddhi went to them and imparted
<>to them the secrets about the life of Candraha1sa. The
<>Kalinda king and his wife dropped their former decision
<>to end their life, and Candraha1sa took up the reins of
<>government at the behest of the King. Candraha1sa,
<>who won repute as a very powerful and effective ruler,
<>during the As4vamedha sacrifice of Yudhis2t2hira captured
<>the sacrificial horse, and Arjuna, at the instance of
<>Kr2s2n2a had to enter into a pact with Candraha1sa, who
<>promised him help for the success of the sacrifice. Candra-
<>ha1sa had a son called Makara1ks2a by Vis2aya1 and another
<>son, Padma1ks2a by Campakama1lini1.
<HI>CANDRAHA1SA (M). Ra1van2a's sword. Ra1van2a, in the
<>course of his triumphal tour after receiving the boons

<HI>CANDRAS4ARMA1 I. A great sinner, who killed his
<>preceptor. The Padma Pura1n2a relates the story of four
<>sinners named Vidura, Candras4arman, Vedas4arman
<>and Van5jula, who shed their sin by bathing in the
<>Gan3ga1. The sins committed by the four were respectively
<>Brahmahatya1 (killing of brahmin), Guruhatya1 (killing
<>of the preceptor), Agamya1gamana (having sexual con-
<>tact with women like the wife of guru which is debarred)
<>and Govadha (killing of cow) respectively. The leader
<>of the above four sinners was one Vidura of the Pa1n5ca1la
<>des4a. He was begging in the streets without the tuft
<>of hair, the sacred thread and tilaka (mark of sandal
<>paste etc. on the forehead). He went from house to
<>house begging for alms crying thus: “Here is come the
<>man (himself) who committed Brahmahatya1. Please [Page174-b+ 65]
<>give alms to the brahmin-killer who is a drunkard also.”
<>During his begging tour he visited all the ti1rthas also,
<>yet brahmahatya1 stuck on to him.
<NI>While this sinner Vidura, his heart rent with sorrow and
<>remorse, was sitting under a tree, a Ma1gadha brahmin
<>called Candras4arman happened to come there. This
<>brahmin had committed the sin of guruhatya1 (killing the
<>preceptor). He told Vidura:--“While residing with the
<>guru, blinded by desire, I killed the guru, and am now
<>burning myself in the fire of repentance.”
<NI>While they were engaged thus in conversation a third
<>person, called Vedas4arman also came there in an absolu-
<>tely tired condition. He told Vidura and Candras4arman
<>that his relatives and others had cast him out as he once
<>did the sin of agamya1gamana, and by the time he had
<>finished telling his story a Vais4ya called Van5jula came
<>there. Besides drinking liquor he had committed govadha
<>(killing of cow) also. Though these four sinners assemb-
<>led at the same place, they only talked, but did not touch
<>one another, take food together, rest on the same seat
<>or lie on the same bedsheet.
<NI>According to the advice of a Siddha (an evolved soul)
<>the four sinners dived in the holy waters of Gan3ga1 and
<>came out redeemed of their sins. (Padma Pura1n2a,
<>Chapter 91).

<>the Pan5cama Skandha of Devi1bha1gavata.
<HI>CANDRASENA II. A king of Ujjayani1 and a great
<>devotee of S4iva. As he performed great sacrifices and
<>gave away money and materials in gift to those who pray-
<>ed for them, an attendant of S4iva called Man2ibhadra

<>in the Maha1ka1la temple in Ujjayani1 the idol installed in
<>which was S4ivalin3ga. Lord S4iva then appeared to him

<HI>CANDRASI1TA1. A female attendant of Skandadeva.
<>(M.B. S4alya Parva, Chapter 46, Verse 11).
<HI>CANDRAS4RI1. A woman, who, though actually in love
<>with her husband enjoyed life with her paramour.
<>Candras4ri1 is the heroine of a story narrated in the Katha1-
<>saritsa1gara to prove that the interest married women take
<>in clandestine relationship is only due to fleeting temp-
<>tations. Candras4ri1 was the wife of a vais4ya called Ba1la-
<>varman, who lived in the city of Pratis2t2ha1na. One day,
<>looking out through the windows in her house she saw
<>the very handsome vais4ya youth called S4i1laha1ra and
<>felt great desire for him. She brought him secretly to
<>the house of her companion and satisfied her desire.
<>When their meeting became a routine affair her relations
<>and servants knew about it. And, her husband alone was
<>unaware of it. Once he became ill with severe fever.
<>Even while he was in his death-bed Candras4ri1 continued
<>her affair with the paramour. When one day she was
<>with her paramour thus, she was told that her husband
<>was no more. At once she took leave of him, returned
<>home and committed suicide by jumping into the funeral
<>pyre of the husband. (Katha1saritsa1gara, S4aktiyas4olam-
<>baka, Taran3ga 2).

<HI>CANDRASVA1MI1. A brahmin, who got back his life by
<>worshipping Su1rya. He stayed with his wife, Devamati1
<>in Kamalapura ruled by King Kamalavarman. A son
<>called Mahi1pa1la was born to him, and at the time of the
<>birth of the child a celestial voice declared that Mahi1pa1la
<>would become King. A few years later a daughter called
<>Candramati1 also was born to Candrasva1mi1. [Page175-b+ 65]
<>During this period, rains having failed, very severe
<>famine stalked Kamalapura. Even the king unmindful of
<>justice and righteousness began extracting from the
<>people whatever they possessed. Finding the situation
<>deteriorated so much Candrasva1mi1 set out for his father-
<>in-law's house with Mahi1pa1la and Candramati1, and on
<>the way they had to cross what was once a wild forest,
<>but which had by then been denuded of green trees or
<>other foliage due to the failure of the rains. The children
<>were severely afflicted by thirst, and so the father, after
<>leaving them at the foot of a tree went in search of water
<>when he was caught by the people of the forest king and
<>taken before him. When he knew that he was about to
<>be given in sacrifice to Devi1 the brahmin folded his
<>hands and prayed to Su1ryadeva, who appeared before
<>him and assured him that not only will death not visit
<>him, but also he would be united with his wife and
<>children.
<NI>The children left under the tree began crying, their
<>father having not returned to them, and a Vais4ya called
<>Sa1rthadha1ra who came that way felt pity for the crying
<>children and took them to his house. One day
<>Anantasva1mi1, a brahmin and a minister of King
<>Ta1ra1na1tha of Ta1ra1pura happened to visit Sa1rthadha1ra.
<>Anantasva1mi1, who had no children of his own took away
<>with him the children from Sa1rthadha1ra.
<NI>One day Su1ryadeva appeared to the forest king in his
<>sleep and asked him not to kill the brahmin (Candra-
<>sva1mi) held captive by him. The very next morning
<>Candrasva1mi1 was released from captivity. Roaming
<>about in quest of his children, Candrasva1mi1 reached the
<>city called Jalapura where he was put up as the guest
<>of a house-holder. One day the host told Candrasva1mi1
<>that a vais4ya called Kanakavarman had, a few days back,
<>told him that he (Kanakavarman) had got two children,
<>one male and the other female from the forest, and taken
<>them to the Na1likera island. The very next day Candra-
<>sva1mi1, along with a vais4ya called Vis2n2uvarman arrived
<>at the Na1likera island, and on enquiry they were told
<>that Kanakavarman, with the children had left for
<>Kat2a1ha island. They then went to Kat2a1ha island only
<>to be told that Kanakavarman had gone to Karpu1ra
<>island. With another Vais4ya Candrasva1mi landed on the
<>Karpu1ra island to no purpose. After thus going to the
<>Suvarn2a and Sim3hala islands too Candrasva1mi met
<>Kanakavarman at Citraku1t2apura. Kanakavarman
<>showed the children to Candrasva1mi, but they were not
<>his. He burst into tears, and returned from Citraku1t2a-
<>pura. He passed on his way back many a temple and
<>city and at dusk reached a big forest. He satisfied his
<>hunger by eating some roots and fruits, and got upon
<>a tree and sat on the top of it. It was mid-night, yet
<>sleep did not oblige him. Then he saw a ma1tr2san3gha
<>(a group of women) consisting of Na1ra1yan2i1 and others
<>come and dance at the foot of the tree. Na1ra1yan2i1 saw
<>Candrasva1mi1 and after the dance was over and her
<>companions had left the place Na1ra1yan2i1 called him down
<>from the tree and gave him a flower. And, according
<>to the advice of Na1ra1yan2i1 the next day morning Candra-
<>sva1mi1 went to Ta1ra1pura where, accidentally he got into
<>the house of Anantasva1mi1, minister of the kingdom, and,
<>for food, repeated vedic hymns. The minister invited him
<>for meals, and there he saw Mahi1pa1la and Candra1vati1.
<>He placed the flower presented by Na1ra1yan2i1 on Mahi1-
<>pa1la's nose whereupon the children recognised him, [Page176-a+ 65]
<>their father. The people celebrated it as a happy day.
<>King Ta1ra1varman gave his daughter, Vasumati1, in
<>marriage to Mahi1pa1la along with half his kingdom.
<>They went to Kamalapura and returned with their
<>mother. All of them spent their days very happily at
<>Ta1ra1pura. (Katha1saritsa1gara, Alan3ka1ravati1 Lam3baka,
<>Taran3ga 6).
<HI>CANDRA1VALOKA. A King of Citraku1t2anagara.
<NI>When once the King in the course of a hunting expedition

<>there the very beautiful lady, Indi1varaprabha1, daughter

<>to kill animals in future, and Kan2va married his daugh-

<>couple went to sleep on the banks of a pool which be-
<>longed to a Brahmaraks2as, who caught hold of them,
<>but released them on condition that they gave him in
<>their stead a brahmin boy aged seven. Thus they re-

<>minister Sumati. As advised by Sumati an idol in gold
<>equal in size to a brahmin boy aged seven was made and
<>a proclamation was issued that the golden idol would
<>be given to him who, in return, give a brahmin boy
<>seven years old. A brahmin boy was got, and he was
<>given to the Brahmaraks2as. (Katha1saritsa1gara S4as4a1n3ka-
<>vati1 Lam3baka, Taran3ga 27).

<HI>CANDRAVARMAN. A king of Kamboja. As handsome
<>as Candra, he was born in the dynasty of the asura called

<HI>CANDRA1VATI1. Daughter of Suna1bha, the asura.
<>Pradyumna, son born to S4ri1 Kr2s2n2a of Rukmin2i1, married
<>Prabha1vati1, daughter of the asura called Vajrana1bha.
<>Suna1bha was the younger brother of Vajrana1bha.
<>Suna1bha had two daughters called Candra1vati1 and
<>Gun2avati1. These girls once saw Pradyumna and Prabha1-
<>vati1 engaged in love-talk and they were attracted by
<>Pradyumna. They requested Prabha1vati1 to select hus-
<>bands for them also from among the Ya1davas. Prabha1-
<>vati1, in her childhood had learnt from Durva1sas a
<>mantra, the repetition of which by a woman thinking
<>in mind of any male would help to secure that male as the
<>husband for her. Prabha1vati1 taught that mantra to
<>Candra1vati1 and Gun2avati1. They thought respectively
<>of Gada and Sa1mba and repeated the mantra with the
<>result that Gada married Candra1vati1 and Sa1mba married
<>Gun2avati1.

<HI>CATURIKA1. A harlot about whom the following story
<>is told in Katha1saritsa1gara.
<NI>Once a poor Brahmin got a piece of gold as Pratigraha
<>(fee or daks2in2a1 for performing a religious rite). While
<>he was perplexed not knowing what to do with the gold,
<>a Vit2a advised him to go on tour with the money got by [Page179-a+ 65]
<>selling the gold. The poor Brahmin did not even know
<>how to go on tour. Then the Vit2a advised him to visit a
<>harlot called Caturika1, who lived close by and who,
<>the Vita said, would teach him how to go on tour.
<NI>Brahmin:--What shall I do first after going to the
<>harlot's house?
<NI>Vit2a:--Caturika1 will become friendly if you would
<>give her the gold. Then you should use sweet and charm-
<>ing words, and that is all.
<NI>The Brahmin went immediately to Caturika1's house and
<>he was received honourably by her. Then giving the
<>gold to her he requested her to instruct him about going
<>on tour. This request of his evoked laughter from people
<>around him. Then the Brahmin who was versed in the
<>S4rutis began reciting the Sa1man with his palm formed
<>in the shape of the ears of the cow. All those who were
<>present there wildly laughed at him, and somehow or
<>other he escaped from the none too pleasant scene, and
<>reported his experience at the harlot's to the Vit2a.
<>He very easily understood that the reason for the Brah-
<>min being ridiculed was that misreading his advice to
<>use ‘sa1ma’ (sweet words) the Brahmin recited the sa1ma-
<>veda. He took the brahmin back to Caturika1 and asked
<>her to return the ‘grass’ (gold given her by the brah-
<>min) to ‘the cow’, the Brahmin who was as simple as
<>the cow. Laughingly Caturika1 returned the gold to
<>him. (Katha1saritsa1gara, Katha1pi1t2ha Lam3bakam, Ta-
<>ran3ga 6).

<HI>CATURMUKHALIN3GA. A Brahma lin3ga installed on

<>ful girl, born of herself appeared before him. Brahma1

<>the Stha1n2uti1rtha, which washes off all sins, on the north-

<>as the Caturmukhalin3ga. This installation of lin3ga
<>redeemed him from sins. (Va1mana Pura1n2a, Chapter 49).
<HI>CATURTHI1VRATA. A fast undertaken so that one
<>may be happy for one year, may achieve all desires and
<>attain S4ivaloka. While observing this fast one should
<>worship Gan2apati on the fourth day after New Moon
<>(Caturthi1) in the month of Makara (February-March).
<>Next day the fast should be broken by eating cooked
<>rice and oil seeds. The Mu1lamantra of Gan2apati is
<>‘gam3 sva1ha1’; hr2daya1dis2ad2an3gas beginning with ‘gam’.
<>‘A1gaccha1tika1ya’ is the a1va1hana mantra and ‘Gaccha1-
<>tika1ya’ the visarjana mantra. Gan2apati should be
<>worshipped with mantras ending with ‘tun3ka1ya’ and
<>beginning with ‘gaka1ra’ as also with offering of sandal
<>paste, rice balls etc. “Om3 Mahotka1ya1ya vidmahe vakra-
<>tun2d2a1ya dhi1mahi tanno danti1 pracodaya1t” is the Gan2es4a
<>ga1yatri1 mantra. He or she who performs the Caturthi1
<>vrata will attain S4ivaloka. If Gan2apati is worshipped
<>on a Tuesday which is also Caturthi1 the devotee will
<>get all his desires satisfied. Caturthi1 occurring in Mi1na
<>(March-April) is called Avighnacaturthi1, and that day
<>fasting should be in the night. On Caturthi1 in April-
<>May Gan2apati should be worshipped with damanaka [Page179-b+ 65]
<>flowers, and then the worshipper will become very
<>happy. (Agni Pura1n2a, Chapter 179). Also see Vina1yaka
<>Caturthi1.
<HI>CATURUPA1YAM. The four means used by Kings in
<>olden times to achieve their objects are called Caturu-
<>pa1yam. Sa1ma, da1na, bheda and dan2d2a are the Catur-
<>upa1yas (four tactics). There are three other upa1yas
<>also, almost equally important as the first four, and the
<>seven upa1yas are collectively called Saptopa1yas. There
<>are also yet other upa1yas, but they are not important
<>enough to merit special mention.
<NI>1) {%Sa1ma.%} It is the best means to attract and convert
<>others to one's side. It consists in winning people with
<>sweet words and looks. People who are friendly by
<>temperament and straightforward may be brought
<>round by sa1ma. Sa1ma is employed in dealing with sons,
<>brothers and other relatives.
<NI>2) {%Da1na.%} There are five kinds or varieties of da1na
<>(gift) viz. pri1tida1na, dravyada1na, svayam3gra1ha, deya
<>and pratimoks2a. If a person gets help from another
<>and acknowledges help by reward that reward is
<>called pri1tida1na. The miser and the poor should be
<>brought round by pri1tida1na. Military captains, heroes
<>and citizens should be won over by this da1na. Those
<>who fall at feet should be honoured by da1na.
<NI>3) {%Bheda.%} Bhedopa1ya is of three kinds: to destroy or
<>end the friendship subsisting between people, to create
<>dissension and to make the parties quarrel with each
<>other. He who is falsely praised, he who does not desire
<>wealth or welfare, he who has been invited to come and
<>then insulted, traitor to king, he who has been exorbi-
<>tantly taxed, the angry one, the honourable one, the
<>insulted, the unreasonably forsaken one, he who har-
<>bours hatred in his mind, the pacified one, one whose
<>wealth and wife have been taken away or stolen, he
<>who has not been respected though deserving of respect--
<>if such people are in one's opposite camp, suspicion
<>should be raked up among them and thus division crea-
<>ted in their ranks. If subordinate chieftains, and forest
<>tribes commit offences they should be brought round
<>by the use of sa1ma and bheda upa1yas.
<NI>4) {%Dan2d2a.%} Dan2d2opa1ya is of three kinds, viz, killing,
<>denuding of wealth, and inflicting pain on the body or
<>torture. Dan2d2a has two other forms, praka1s4a (open)
<>and apraka1s4a (secret). Those who have become objects
<>of hatred to all people should be subjected to praka1s4a-
<>dan2d2a. People, whose killing the world will detest,
<>should not be killed openly, but only secretly. Enemies
<>should be killed with weapons etc. Brahmins should not
<>be killed. Friends and allies should not be subjected
<>to dan2d2a; friends, who are made objects of dan2d2a will
<>wither away like worm-eaten trees. The King, who
<>possesses the three powers (of wealth, army and people's
<>support) and is fully conscious of the time and environ-
<>mental factors should annihilate enemies by the instru-
<>ment of dan2d2a. Evil people should be defeated by
<>dan2d2a itself.
<NI>The upa1ya ma1ya1 consists in practising deception by
<>magic or other yogic powers. The powers can be
<>acquired by the worship of the idols of certain Devata1s.
<>People who employ go about at night in various dis-
<>guises. They disguise themselves as beautiful women or
<>even as animals. They also deceive people by creating
<>illusions of clouds, fire or lightning. For instance, Bhi1ma
<>killed Ki1caka by going to him in the guise of a woman. [Page180-a+ 65]
<>Not to dissuade people who indulge in unjustifiable
<>grief, war etc. is the principal aim of the upa1ya called
<>upeks2a1. Hid2imbi1 gave up his brother Hidimba by
<>practising Upeks2a1. Next is the Indraja1lopa1ya (magic).
<>For example, to create illusions of clouds, darkness, rain,
<>fire, and other magic in order to instil fear among
<>troops of the enemy etc. To scare the enemy is the aim
<>of Indraja1la. (For details of Caturupa1yas see Kan2ika).
<>(Agni Pura1n2a, Chapter 241).
<HI>CA1TURVARN2YAM. (The four Castes, Classes).
<NI>1) {%General.%} The pura1n2as tell that Brahma1 created the
<>four castes like Brahmins, Ks2atriyas, Vais4yas and S4u1dras
<>and assigned to them duties and rules of procedure.
<NI>2) {%Origin.%} Brahma1 got ready for the work of Crea-
<>tion, concentrating his mind on the universal soul, the
<>embodiment of Truth. And, the first to be born from his
<>face were people with sublime qualities, and them he
<>called Brahmins. Then emerged from his chest people
<>in whom the heroic or militant qualities preponderated.
<>(Ra1jasa). Them the Creator named Ks2atriyas. Then
<>from his thighs were born people in whom rajas and
<>tamas preponderated, and they were called Vais4yas.
<>And, lastly Brahma1 brought forth from his feet another
<>sect of people, and as tamas (darkness, cultural darkness)
<>preponderated in them they were named S4u1dras. The
<>system of four castes is called Ca1turvarn2yam. (Vis2n2u
<>Pura1n2a, Part 1, Chapter 6).
<NI>3) {%Code of conduct.%} Ahim3sa1 (non-violence), Satyava1da
<>(speaking truth), Bhu1tada1ya (love for all living beings),
<>Ti1rthaseva1 (resort to holy places), Da1na (gifts), Brahma-
<>carya (celibacy), Vimatsaratva (absence of malice),
<>Service of Devas, Brahmins and Gurus (elders), to attend
<>to all dharmas (duties), Pitr2pu1ja1 (worship of elders)
<>eternal loyalty to King, to accomplish objects in con-
<>formity with the injunction of the S4a1stras, not to commit
<>cruel deeds, Titiks2a1 (forbearance, to put up with
<>equanimity the pair of opposites such as pleasure and
<>pain, heat and cold), belief in God--these codes of con-
<>duct are common to all Castes and in all the four stations
<>in life. Now, the special codes for each of the four
<>castes.
<NI>1) {%Brahmins.%} To perform and get performed Yajn5as,
<>to study and to teach the Vedas, to give gifts and to
<>receive gifts--these form the dharma (duties) of the
<>Brahmin. Brahmins take their second birth (dvija)
<>on the upanayana (wearing of the sacred thread).
<>The caste of the mother is the caste of the child. But
<>children born to Brahmins of their non-Brahmin wives
<>are not Brahmins. Children born of a Brahmin woman
<>to a S4u1dra1 are called Can2d2a1las. Sons born of Brahmin
<>women to Ks2atriyas are called Su1tas, and sons born of a
<>Brahmin woman to a Vais4ya are called Vaidehikas.
<>The Brahmin shall not adopt the profession of the other
<>castes. The twice-born are, however, allowed in certain
<>circumstances, the profession of agriculture, protection
<>of cows, commerce and kusi1da (to lend money on inte-
<>rest). But, he shall not trade in gorasa (milk and other
<>cow products) lavan2a (salt) and flesh. If he be an
<>agriculturist, the Brahmin may till the earth, cut medi-
<>cinal plants and destroy pests. But, he must purify the
<>soul by performing yajn5as and worshipping Devas.
<>The soil should be ploughed with eight oxen attached to
<>the plough. He may sustain himself either with R2ta
<>(food got by begging) or Amr2ta (food got not by beg-
<>ging). The suffix “S4arma1” should be added to the names [Page180-b+ 64]
<>of Brahmin children. Upanayana (wearing of the sacred
<>thread) should be done when the child is eight years
<>old. The maun5ji (girdle around the waist) to be worn
<>after upanayana should be made either of mun5ja grass
<>or of the bark of trees. Brahmin brahmaca1rins should
<>wear hides, and when they beg for alms the sentence
<>should commence with the word ‘Bhavati’, e.g. “Bhavati,
<>bhiks2a1m3 dehi). The Brahmin can marry in all the castes,
<>but he shall perform ritual only along with the wife of
<>his own caste.
<NI>{%ii) Ks2atriya.%} To give gifts according to rules, study
<>Vedas and perform yajn5as form the duty of the Ks2atriya.
<>To look after and take care of good people and to punish
<>evil-doers are his especial duties. Names of Ks2atriya
<>children should take the suffix “Varma1”. After upana-
<>yana the child should wear tiger skin. He should carry
<>a dan2d2a (rod) of the Araya1l (peepal) tree. When the
<>Ks2atriya brahmaca1rin begs for alms the word ‘bhavati’
<>should be used in the middle of the sentence, e.g. “Bhiks2a1m3
<>bhavati dehi”. The Ks2atriya can marry in the other
<>castes, except the Brahmin. At the time of wedding the
<>Ks2atriya woman should hold an arrow in her hand.
<NI>{%iii) Vais4ya.%} Agriculture, protection of cows and trade
<>are the special duties of the Vais4ya. Children born of
<>Brahmin women to Vais4yas are called Vaidehikas.
<>Names of Vais4ya children should have the suffix ‘gupta’
<>after them. After upanayana the Vais4ya brahmaca1rin
<>should wear sheep's skin. He should carry a dan2d2a made
<>of a branch of the Ku1vala tree. The Vais4ya may have a
<>wife of his own caste and one of the S4u1dra caste. At
<>the time of wedding the Vais4ya woman should hold a
<>cane in her hand.
<NI>{%iv) S4u1dra.%} Service to the Brahmin and architecture
<>form the duties of the S4u1dra. Children born to Ks2atriya
<>women by S4u1dras are called Pulkasas. Children born
<>to Brahmin women by S4u1dras are called Can2d2a1las,
<>and sons born to S4u1dras by Vais4ya women are called
<>A1yogavas.
<NI>The Can2d2a1la's profession is to hang criminals sentenced
<>to death, and to live by women. The Pulkasa must
<>live by hunting. A1yogava is to act on the stage and live
<>by architecture. The Can2d2a1la should live outside the
<>village. The Can2d2a1la has a right to take the clothes on
<>corpses. He shall not have any physical contacts with
<>the others. He will attain salvation if he died in the
<>course of protecting others. The suffix ‘da1sa’ should be
<>added to his name. He shall marry only from his own
<>caste. (Agni Pura1n2a, 4 Chapters from 151).
<HI>CATURVEDA. One of the Saptapitr2s (seven manes).
<>Vaira1jas, agnis2va1ttas, ga1rhapatyas, somapa1s, Ekas4r2n3gas,
<>Caturvedas and Ka1las are the seven manes. (M.B.
<>Sabha1 Parva, Chapter 11, Verse 47).
<HI>CATURYUGA. (See Manvantara).
<HI>CATUS2KAR2N2I1. One of the female attendants of Skanda-
<>deva. (M.B. S4alya Parva, Chapter 45, Verse 25).
<HI>CATUS2PATHARATA1. A female attendant of Skanda.
<>(M.B. S4alya Parva, Chapter 46, Verse 27).
<HI>CATVARAVA1SINI1. A woman attendant of Skanda.
<>(M.B. S4alya Parva, Chapter 46, Verse 12).
<HI>CA1VET2T2UPAT2A. (CA1VERU). (Pat2a = army).
<NI>A particular division of the army which in olden days
<>used to come forward to lay down their lives in the
<>service of the country. When defeat in war was almost
<>certain for their king an army division under royal [Page181-a+ 64]
<>leadership was formed of heroes, who took their last
<>leave of their homes and people. The soldiers left their
<>homes with their head and eyebrows shaved, and people
<>gave them a hearty send-off. Either victory or death
<>in battlefield was their slogan. This system originated
<>in Kerala. Imitating Kerala, Turkey and Egypt formed
<>their own division of Ca1vet2t2upat2a in the 15th century.
<>The Ca1vet2t2upat2a of Turkey called the Janissary was
<>formed out of poor Christians captured in war, and that
<>of Egypt called Mamaluk was formed out of slaves.
<>But, the Ca1vet2t2upat2a of ancient Kerala was self-orga-
<>nised out of burning love for their king and country.
<>The families of those warriors of the pata who died in
<>fight were allotted royal favours. Even before, in the 14th
<>century A.D. there existed in Kerala this system of
<>Ca1vet2t2upat2a. From A.D. 1090 to 1111 A.D. many fierce
<>wars were fought between Kerala and the Cola country,
<>and it was perhaps during this period that this system of

<>varti who ruled Kerala during the above period drove

<HI>CERIPPU. (FOOTWEAR). There is a story in
<>Maha1bha1rata of how Cerippu and Kut2a (footwear and
<>umbrella) were born. Once the heat of the Sun be-
<>came unbearable to Jamadagni and enraged at this
<>the sage started sending arrows against the Sun. His
<>wife Ren2uka1 was supplying him with arrows. When a
<>set of arrows was finished Ren2uka1 brought another set.
<>This continued without break and the Sun began to
<>feel the attack. Unable to do anything against the sage
<>the Sun heated the head and foot of Ren2uka1 on her way
<>to supply the arrows so fiercely that Ren2uka1 fell down
<>under a banyan tree exhausted. When she became well
<>enough to walk she took the arrows to her husband who
<>was very angry for her being late. She then explained
<>to him how because of the extreme heat of the sun she
<>fell down on the way. Jamadagni then started with
<>increased fury his shower of arrows and the Sun in the
<>disguise of a Brahmin approached and advised him that it
<>was not possible to strike down the Sun because he was
<>a swift-mover in the sky and so it was better to withdraw
<>from that attempt. But Jamadagni said the Sun would [Page182-a+ 65]
<>be stationary for some time at midday and then he would
<>hit the Sun down. When the Sun found that Jamadagni
<>would never drop his attempt he accepted defeat and
<>presented Jamadagni with a pair of sandals and an
<>umbrella to protect against the heat from below and
<>above. From that day onwards footwear and umbrella
<>came into vogue.
<NI>The practice of presenting sandals and umbrella is
<>being carried down from generation to generation and
<>even today it is being followed; these two are given as
<>gifts on the ‘S4ra1ddha’ day. (Chapters 95 and 96, Anus4a1-
<>sana Parva, M.B.)

<HI>CIRADA1TA1. There was a king called Cirada1ta1 in the
<>country called Cirapura. Though himself a good man he
<>was surrounded by bad advisers. Once a foreigner,
<>Prasan3ga, came along with two friends to the palace to
<>see the King. But due to the bad advice of his courtiers
<>the King did not care to see them even. For five years
<>they stayed there without even getting a look at the
<>King. One day the only son of the King died. All the
<>courtiers gathered round the child and pretended to
<>be greatly grieved. Prasan3ga and his friends also came
<>to the scene. They saw the king and spoke to him thus:
<>“For five years you have not cared either to see or talk to
<>us. We were cherishing a hope that at least your son
<>would give us a better treatment when he grew old and
<>became King. We were living here on that hope and now
<>that hope also has been shattered and so we are leaving
<>the place this instant.” The King was surprised to hear
<>that they were prepared even to wait for his son to
<>become the King and gave them very many presents.
<>(Taran3ga 5, Alan3ka1ravati1 Lam3baka, Katha1saritsa1gara).

<HI>CIRAKA1RI1. A son of Gautama Mahars2i. He used to
<>think deeply before doing any deed and so he earned the
<>name Ciraka1ri1.
<NI>Once the sage Gautama found out proof against the
<>chastity of his own wife and enraged at this commanded
<>his son Ciraka1ri1 to chop off the head of the latter's
<>mother. Giving this command Gautama went into the
<>forest and Ciraka1ri1 in obedience to his father's order
<>came before his mother, weapon in hand. He did not
<>kill her immediately but pondered over the consequences
<>of the deed. Matricide is a great sin, he mused. Then
<>again who would be there to look after father if mother
<>died. Perhaps his father, when he cools down, might
<>regret his command and come back repentant. Thus
<>he was sitting undecided when Gautama returned full
<>of remorse for his hasty order. On seeing his wife [Page183-a+ 63]
<>alive he was greatly relieved and immensely happy and
<>he blessed Ciraka1ri1. (Chapter 266, S4a1nti Parva).

<HI>CIRA1YUS. An ancient King of the country Cira1yu.
<>Na1ga1rjuna was a minister of this King. Na1ga1rjuna knew
<>the secret of ‘Rasa1yanasiddhi’. Once Na1ga1rjuna pre-
<>pared a medicine for himself and the King which if
<>taken would keep them eternally young. They took
<>medicine accordingly.
<NI>After some days a child of the minister died. Grief-
<>stricken Na1ga1rjuna decided to prepare Amr2ta which
<>would eradicate death from this world. He had pre-
<>pared it but there remained one more medicine to be
<>added to this. The auspicious moment to add the same
<>was to come only after five days and Na1ga1rjuna waited.
<>The devas were frightened. If Amr2ta was made avail-
<>able on earth men would become Devas. Devas could
<>not bear this and so they sent the As4vini1kuma1ras to
<>the earth to persuade Na1ga1rjuna to desist from his work
<>of preparing Amr2ta. Further they informed him that his
<>dead child was living happily in heaven. He agreed to
<>stop his work.
<NI>After some time Cira1yus crowned his son Ji1vahara as the
<>heir-apparent. Overjoyed Ji1vahara ran to his mother
<>Dhanapara1 to give her the glad tidings. Dhanapara1
<>told her son thus: “My child, why should you be so
<>happy to think that you have become the heir-apparent.
<>Several sons of your father had already become yuvara1jas
<>and died before becoming King. Your father has taken
<>a medicine from Na1ga1rjuna and that will keep him
<>eternally young. None of his sons can aspire to be the
<>King. Your father is now eight hundred years old and
<>will live long. Many of you will become yuvara1jas but
<>not the King. I will therefore suggest a way to tide over
<>this difficulty. Everyday in the afternoon Na1ga1rjuna
<>would come outside the palace and enquire whether
<>anybody is in need of anything. At that time he would
<>give whatever was asked for. It is an opportunity when
<>you can ask for his head. When Na1ga1rjuna is dead your
<>father would either die of grief or go to the forests.
<>Then you can become the King.”
<NI>Ji1vahara was pleased to hear this suggestion and he went
<>that after-noon to the palace of Na1ga1rjuna and when
<>Na1ga1rjuna as usual came out with his query Ji1vahara
<>asked for his head. Most willingly Na1ga1rjuna offered
<>his head to be chopped off. But the effect of the medicine
<>made even the strongest cut by the sword ineffective.
<>Ji1vahara lost many swords without Na1ga1rjuna getting
<>even a small scratch on his neck. The hubbub brought
<>the King to the scene and he immediately asked Na1ga1r-
<>juna to withdraw his head but Na1ga1rjuna refused saying,
<>“Oh, King, in my ninetynine previous births I have
<>offered my head like this and please do not ask me to
<>desist from making this offer for the hundredth time”. [Page183-b+ 65]
<>So saying he embraced the King and taking a powder
<>from his body smeared it on the sword and asked Ji1va-
<>hara to strike again. This time very easily Ji1vahara
<>severed the head from the body of Na1ga1rjuna. The
<>King was greatly aggrieved and he renounced all and
<>went to the forest and led a pious life.
<NI>Cira1yus' son Ji1vahara became King. But Na1ga1rjuna's
<>sons killed him and his mother died of grief. (Taran3ga,
<>Ratnaprabha1 Lambaka, Katha1saritsa1gara).

<HI>CITAL. (Termites). Cital has got its own place in the
<>pura1n2as. Devi1 Bha1gavata has the following story about
<>the origin of it.
<NI>Maha1vis2n2u once looking at the face of Laks2mi1 laughed
<>without any apparent reason. Laks2mi1, thinking that
<>Vis2n2u was laughing at her and that he had an eye on
<>some other woman more beautiful than her lost her
<>temper and cursed Vis2n2u that his head would be severed
<>from his body.
<NI>No sooner was the curse pronounced than the asuras came
<>in batches fully armed and challenged Vis2n2u to war.
<>Single-handed the Lord fought the asuras with one bow.
<>The fight did not end though it continued for thousands
<>of years. Vis2n2u felt tired and decided to rest awhile.
<>He planted one end of the untied bow on the ground,
<>rested his chin on the other and sat in Padma1sana.
<>Being very tired the Lord remained asleep for a long
<>time in this posture.
<NI>About this time the devas made preparations to perform
<>a yajn5a. All the devas except Maha1vis2n2u attended that
<>yajn5a. Since he was the master of yajn5as performed for
<>purposes of the devas they could not begin it in his
<>absence. So, Brahma1 and others went in search
<>of Vis2n2u to Vaikun2t2ha. But he was not to be found there.
<>Then Brahma1 and others found out with their
<>eyes of knowledge where Vis2n2u was and they went to
<>the place where he was sleeping. They waited there
<>for a long time, yet Vis2n2u did not awake from sleep.
<>Then Brahma1 hit at a plan to awaken Vis2n2u. It was
<>to create citals (termites) to eat the end of the bow.
<>When they had eaten away the end of the bow
<>planted on the ground the cord binding the two ends
<>of it would break, the bow would straighten up and the
<>speedy movement of it would awaken Vis2n2u.
<NI>According to this plan Brahma1 created citals, but his
<>other plans were not acceptable to the citals. They
<>argued that the advantage of awakening the Lord from
<>sleep would go only to the devas, while its sin would
<>fall upon them.
<NI>They argued,
<Poem>Nidra1bhan3gah2 katha1chedo
<>Dampatyoh2 pri1tibhedanam /
<>S4is4uma1tr2vibhedas4ca
<>Brahmahatya1samam3 smr2tam //</Poem>
<F>*) To disturb one in sleep, to interrupt a story, to separate husband and wife as also mother and child from each other--these things
<>are tantamount to Brahmahatya1 (killing of the brahmin).</F>
<>Brahma1 conceded the justness of this argument and
<>agreed that, in future, a part of the result of yajn5as
<>shall go to citals. It was after this that havis (sa crificial
<>offering) which, in the course of being submitted to the
<>fire falls on the sides of the pit of fire became the share
<>of citals (termites). This promise of Brahma1 pleased the
<>the citals, and they did as was bidden by Brahma1 and [Page184-a+ 65]
<>the bow of Vis2n2u straightened up with a terrific sound.
<>The devas were terror-stricken, the whole universe
<>shook, the earth experienced a convulsion and the oceans
<>too were shaken. Moreover, the head of Vis2n2u was
<>severed from the body, rose high up in the sky and fell
<>into the sea. Brahma1, S4iva and others opened their eyes
<>only to find the body of Vis2n2u lying thus without the
<>head.
<NI>This loss of Vis2n2u's head proved to be useful in another
<>way. Now, Hayagri1va, after securing the boon from
<>Brahma1 that he would be killed only by one with horse's
<>head, was running rough-shod over the whole earth.
<>The devas cut off the head of a horse and attached it to
<>the trunk of Vis2n2u. Thus Vis2n2u came to life again, and
<>according to his orders the citals gnawed away the cord
<>of Hayagri1va's bow as a result of which he was killed.
<>(Devi1ma1ha1tmya, Prathma Skandha).

<HI>CITRAKETU I. An emperor, who remained childless
<>for a long time. At last a son was born to him owing to [Page184-b+ 65]

<HI>CITRA1N3GADA1 III. Daughter of Vis4vakarma1. Once

<HI>CITRARATHA I. (AN3GA1RAPARN3A). A devagan-
<>dharva.
<NI>1) {%Birth.%} Citraratha was the gandharva son of
<>Kas4yapapraja1pati of his wife Muni. (S4loka 43, Chapter
<>65, A1di Parva).
<NI>2) {%Citraratha and Arjuna.%} The greatest event in the
<>life of Citraratha alias An3ga1raparn2a was the defeat he
<>suffered at the hands of Arjuna. The Pa1n2d2avas after
<>their escape from the lac palace (La1ks2a1gr2ha) through
<>a tunnel arrived in a forest and there they killed the
<>demons Hid2im3ba and Baka. One night they were
<>walking along the shores of the river Gan3ga1 when they
<>heard a sound of somebody bathing in the river. Arjuna
<>who was walking ahead waving a country torch went
<>to see who was bathing at that time of the night. Arjuna
<>then saw Citraratha enjoying a bath with his wife
<>Kumbhi1nasi1. That period of the night was allotted to
<>the gandharvas, and human beings were not expected
<>to be out walking at that time. Citraratha felt it
<>impertinent that Arjuna a human being should be out
<>walking at night and peep into the privacy of the
<>gandharvas. The gandharva and Arjuna so entered into
<>a combat. Citraratha who was a great fighter was,
<>after a grim battle, subdued, bound hand and foot, and
<>brought before his brothers by Arjuna. Kumbhi1nasi1
<>followed her exhausted, powerless and spiritless husband
<>and pleaded to Dharmaputra to release him. Dharma-
<>putra advised Arjuna to do so and on getting his release
<>he taught Arjuna the famous Ca1ks2us2i1vidya1--the magic
<>art of seeing by one's own eyes anything and everything
<>in the three worlds: Heaven, Earth and the nether-
<>world. Manu was the author of this magic art and from
<>him Soma learnt it and from Soma Vis4va1vasu, from
<>Vis4va1vasu, Citraratha and from Citraratha, Arjuna.
<>Besides this Citraratha gave Arjuna many chariots
<>and horses.
<NI>In return Arjuna taught him the secret of the missile
<>Agnis4ira. Exchanging faith and affection thus Citraratha
<>and the Pa1n2d2avas became friends. He then narrated to
<>the Pa1n2d2avas many interesting stories, of which the
<>following are a few. The story of the feud between
<>Vasis2t2ha and Vis4va1mitra, story of Tapati1sam3varan2a
<>and tales about the sage Para1s4ara, Kalma1s2apa1da,
<>Adr2s4yanti1, Bha1rgavas, Kr2tavi1rya and Aurva. Citra-
<>ratha advised the Pa1n2d2avas to have a priest for the
<>sacrificial rites and it was thus that they engaged
<>Dhaumya as their family priest.
<NI>Since Arjuna had destroyed all the chariots belonging to
<>Citraratha, the latter got a name ‘Bhagnaratha’ (devoid
<>of chariots). Citraratha admitted that Arjuna could [Page186-b+ 65]
<>conquer him because of his vow of celibacy. (Chapters
<>165 to 183, A1di Parva, M.B.).
<NI>3) {%Other details.%}
<NI>(1) Citraratha attended one of the birthday festivals of
<>Arjuna. (S4loka 52, Chapter 122, A1di Parva, M.B.).
<NI>(2) Citraratha worshipped Kubera sitting in the latter's
<>council. (Sabha1 Parva, Chapter 10, S4loka 26).
<NI>(3) He offered as a gift to Dharmaputra four hundred
<>excellent horses which could fly like wind. (Chapter 52,
<>S4loka 23, Vana Parva).
<NI>(4) When the gandharvas milked the earth during the
<>time of the emperor, Pr2thu, Citraratha acted as the calf.
<>(S4loka 39, Chapter 6, Harivam3s4a).
<NI>(5) S4iva once sent Citraratha as a messenger to Asura
<>S4am3khacu1d2a, with a message that S4am3khacu1d2a should
<>abandon his satanic activities. (Devi1 Bha1gavata, Nava-
<>ma Skandha).

<HI>CITRASENA I. A gandharva. There is reference to
<>this gandharva in many places in the pura1n2as. He had
<>two wives Sandhya1vali1 and Ratna1vali1.
<NI>1) {%How Arjuna saved him.%} Once when Citrasena with
<>his wives was travelling in the aerial car the spittings of
<>Citrasena fell upon the sage Ga1lava who was doing his
<>sandhya1 rites then. The sage complained about the
<>incident to S4ri1 Kr2s2n2a who promised to bring to him the
<>head of Citrasena before sun-set. Sage Na1rada informed
<>Citrasena of this vow of Kr2s2n2a. The gandharva was
<>taken aback and did not know what to do. But Sandhya1-
<>vali1 and Ratna1vali1 went and sought the help of Subhadra1.
<>They made a fire pit in front of her house and decided
<>to end their lives along with Citrasena by jumping into
<>the fire. While Citrasena was circling the fire-pit be-
<>fore jumping into it his wives wept loudly and hearing
<>the noise Subhadra1 came out and saw what was happen-
<>ing. They then took from Subhadra1 a boon to the effect
<>that they should be allowed to live with their husband.
<>It was only after granting them the boon that Subhadra1
<>understood the whole situation. Subhadra1 was in a
<>fix but Arjuna assured her that Citrasena would be
<>protected at any cost. Arjuna very cleverly shielded all
<>the arrows sent against the gandharva by Kr2s2n2a and
<>gradually the fight came to be one between Arjuna and
<>S4ri1 Kr2s2n2a. Subhadra1 came between them and it was
<>found difficult to continue the fight. S4ri1 Kr2s2n2a advised
<>Citrasena to bow down and touch the feet of Ga1lava.
<>Citrasena obeyed and the issue settled without harm to
<>both the parties.
<NI>2) {%Arjuna defeats Citrasena.%} While the Pa1n2d2avas were
<>in exile, Duryodhana knew through a spy that they
<>were camping in the Dvaitavana forest. Prompted by
<>Karn2a Duryodhana programmed to go to Dvaitavana
<>with his retinue to enjoy the sight of the suffering Pa1n2d2a-
<>vas. So they started to the forests under a pretext of an
<>annual stock-taking of the cows. They reached Dvaita-
<>vana with a huge army. There they split into parties
<>and roamed about making merry by themselves. Soon
<>one of the parties reached a pond near the hermitage of
<>the Pa1n2d2avas. They saw a few gandharvas making
<>merry in the pond. With the usual haughtiness they
<>commanded the gandharvas to leave the pond and make
<>room for Duryodhana to come and bathe. The gandhar-
<>vas did not pay heed to their words and on being inform-
<>ed of this Duryodhana went to fight with the gandhar-
<>vas. It was Citrasena who led the gandharvas and by
<>his incessant shower of piercing arrows split the Kaurava
<>forces and made them flee for life. Duryodhana was
<>isolated from his army, was bound hand and foot and
<>taken a prisoner. In their sheer helplessness they approa-
<>ched Dharmaputra in his hermitage and acquainted
<>him with the pitiable plight of Duryodhana. Dharma-
<>putra asked Arjuna to go to the help of the Kauravas.
<>Arjuna faced Citrasena in a grim combat. Citrasena
<>then appeared in his real form and Arjuna knew that
<>he was fighting a friend. Then at the request of Arjuna
<>Citraratha released Duryodhana. (Chapters 239 to
<>243, Vana Parva).
<NI>3) {%Other details.%} (1) This gandharva occupied a seat
<>in the court of Yudhis2t2hira along with twentyseven other [Page187-b+ 65]
<>gandharvas and a few Apsara1 maidens. (S4loka 32,
<>Chapter 4, Sabha1 Parva).
<NI>(2) Citrasena is a member of the court of Kubera also.
<>(S4loka 26, Chapter 10, Sabha1 Parva).
<NI>(3) Citrasena used to attend the court of Indra also
<>at times. (S4loka 22, Chapter 7, Sabha1 Parva).
<NI>(4) At the invitation of Indra Arjuna went to devaloka
<>and Citrasena taught him dance and music. It was
<>during this visit that Arjuna threw a cold blanket on the
<>amorous approaches of Urvas4i1 and was consequently
<>cursed by her to be an eunuch. It was through Citrasena
<>that Indra sent word to Urvas4i1 to console Arjuna and
<>give relief from the curse. (Chapters 45 and 46, Vana
<>Parva).

<HI>COLA. A very righteous emperor of Ka1n5ci1pura. Be-
<>cause of the virtuous rule of this emperor his country
<>got the name Cola. There is an interesting story in
<>Padma Pura1n2a of how the emperor attained Vaikun2-
<>t2haloka (The abode of Maha1vis2n2u).
<NI>Once Cola went to Vaikun2t2ha to worship Maha1vis2n2u.
<>As he was worshipping him with pearls and flowers
<>of gold a brahmin subject of his came there to worship
<>him with water and Tulasi1 leaves. The simple offer-
<>ing of Tulasi1 leaves and flowers by this brahmar2s2i, Vis2n2u-
<>da1sa, eclipsed the expensive one by the King and the
<>latter was greatly offended and he rebuked the Brahmin
<>saying that he was a poor man who did not know what
<>devotion to Vis2n2u was. But Vis2n2uda1sa did not take
<>the taunt unchallenged. He asked the king to wait
<>and see who would merge with the glowing entity of
<>the Lord earlier.
<NI>The King on returning to his palace started a Vais2n2ava-
<>sattra. (An almonry dedicated to Maha1vis2n2u). This
<>almshouse was as comfortable and luxurious as the
<>one once started by Brahma1 in the temple of Gaya.
<>Vis2n2uda1sa on the other hand spent his life in a Vis2n2u
<>temple leading a celibate life and observing rites pleas-
<>ing to Vis2n2u.
<NI>One day after finishing his daily morning rites Vis2n2u-
<>da1sa cooked his food as usual and kept it in its place
<>to be taken later. But on returning to it after some
<>time he found his food eaten by someone else. He
<>went without food that day and it happened the next
<>day also. It continued to happen thus for a week and
<>all these days Vis2n2uda1sa went fasting. Vis2n2uda1sa
<>then decided to watch and see who the thief was.
<>On keeping a vigil he found a Can2d2a1la (a harijan of
<>the lowest Cadre) timidly coming and grabbing at
<>the food with gluttonous greed to appease his hunger.
<>Seeing the famished skeletonous body of the thief Vis2n2u-
<>da1sa felt compassion rather than resentment and calling
<>him back to take the food went after him crying “Hi,
<>come here and take this ghee also with you. That food
<>has no fat in it.” But the Gan2d2a1la frightened at the
<>sight of the owner of the food ran away as fast as his
<>legs could carry him, Vis2n2uda1sa following him. But
<>soon the Can2d2a1la fell fainting on the road exhausted and
<>tired. Vis2n2uda1sa reaching his side started fanning him
<>with his cloth. Very soon the Can2d2a1la changed
<>himself into Maha1vis2n2u adorned with S4am3kha, Cakra
<>and Gada1 and Vis2n2u embracing his devotee took him
<>to Vaikun2t2ha.
<NI>Hearing this Cola called Mudgala the priest of the
<>Vais2n2avasattra, to his side and lamented “I started
<>this sattra to spite Vis2n2uda1sa and now he has gone to [Page189-a+ 65]
<>Vaikun2t2ha. It was due to his true devotion to God
<>that he has attained salvation. What we have done
<>is of no use.” So saying he crowned his nephew as
<>King. Even while young he had become an ascetic and as
<>such had no sons. From that time the crowning of
<>the nephew as King became a precedent in Cola.
<>Renouncing the kingdom Cola went to the sacrificial
<>hall and circling the sacrificial fire stood before it and
<>addressing Mukunda said “Oh Vis2n2u, grant me un-
<>wavering devotion to you in thought, speech and deed.”
<>Repeating this three times the king jumped into the
<>fire. At this Mudgala, the priest, burst into anger and
<>tore off the lock of hair on his head. So even today
<>the decendants of Mudgala do not have hair-locks.
<>Vis2n2u who is always affectionate to his devotees graci-
<>ously appeared from the sacrificial fire and embraced
<>the King. The King was then taken to Vaikun2t2ha.
<>(Chapters 110, 111, Padma Pura1n2a).

<HI>CURAYKKA1. While emperor Pr2thu was ruling the

<HI>CU1T2U. (Dice). It is an imaginative paura1n2ic assump-
<>tion that there is a satanic force latent in dice, hunting,
<>wine and women. There is a story to support the pre-
<>sence of this evil power within the above four. Indra
<>once turned the As4vini1devas out of his court labelling
<>them as outcastes and banned wine to them. Discon-
<>tented at this the As4vini1devas approached Cyavana and
<>the latter performing a ya1ga invited the As4vini1devas and
<>gave them the ya1ga wine, ‘Soma’. Infuriated at this
<>Indra raised his celebrated Vajra1yudha (Weapon of
<>thunder) to kill Cyavana. But the sage by his yogic
<>powers paralysed the hands of Indra and instantly from
<>the sacrificial fire there arose Mada a genie to kill Indra.
<>Frightened by this sight Indra fell at the feet of Cyavana
<>and craved for pardon. The sage then tore the genie into
<>four pieces and threw one each into dice, hunting, wine
<>and women. That is why all the four are as alluring as
<>destructive. (Saptama Skandha, Devi1 Bha1gavata).
<HI>CYAVANA. A celebrated sage of the Bha1rgava dynasty.
<NI>1) {%Genealogy.%} Descending in order from Brahma1--
<>Bhr2gu--Cyavana.
<NI>2) {%Birth.%} Bhr2gu is the son of Brahma1 born of Agni at the
<>Brahmayajn5a conducted by Varun2a. The beautiful and
<>virtuous lady Puloma1 was the wife of Bhr2gu. Even be-
<>fore Bhr2gu married her she was being loved by the demon
<>Puloma1 and her marriage with Bhr2gu embittered him
<>and he waited for an opportunity to kidnap Puloma1.
<NI>One day when Bhr2gu went to the river for his bath
<>Puloma1 entered the a1s4rama and there he found his love
<>Puloma1 being watched over by Agni. Puloma1 compelled
<>Agni to tell him the truth whether Bhr2gu had married
<>Puloma1 according to the rites enjoined by the scriptures.
<>Agni confessed that it was not so and then Puloma1
<>taking the shape of a hog carried away Puloma1 who was
<>then in a stage of advanced pregnancy. On the way the
<>wife of the sage delivered and the babe dropped to the
<>ground. Because the babe was born with a fall (Cyavana)
<>from the womb the boy was named Cyavana. The
<>radiance of the boy burnt the demon into ashes. Puloma1
<>weeping profusely with tears rolling down her cheeks
<>returned to the a1s4rama. Her tears ran into a river and the
<>river got the name Vadhu1sara1 Bhr2gu on knowing how all
<>happened cursed Agni saying that Agni would thereafter
<>be an all-round eater. (eating anything and everything).
<>(Chapters 5 and 6, A1di Parva).
<NI>3) {%Penance and marriage.%} Even when he was very
<>young Cyavana commenced practising austerities.
<>He went to the forests and sat in meditation without food
<>or sleep, oblivious to what happened outside. Years went
<>by and Cyavana did not stir from his place. Gradually
<>earth began to cover him and soon he was completely
<>enveloped by earth. Creepers grew on it and birds made
<>rests; Cyavana did not know anything about it. [Page190-a+ 65]
<>One day S4arya1ti with his wives and children came to
<>that forest for a picnic. Sukanya1, daughter of King
<>S4arya1ti, along with her companions separated from the
<>King and roamed about in the forest making merry.
<>They soon came to the place where Cyavana sat doing
<>penance. There was an unusual radiance around the
<>heap of earth they saw there and Sukanya1 out of curiosity
<>started striking down the earth. Then from inside came
<>Cyavana's voice advising her not to crumble down the
<>earth as she was doing. Sukanya1 ignored the advice and
<>searched for the source of the voice. She then saw two
<>points of glow and taking a thorn gave two pricks at
<>those points and left the place with her companions.
<NI>Those glow-points were the eyes of Cyavana and Cya-
<>vana felt insufferable pain when his eyes were thus pierced
<>through. But he neither became angry nor cursed the
<>girl who did this havoc. Cyavana went on with his
<>penance. But soon the country of S4arya1ti began to expe-
<>rience the bad effects of this evil-deed. People one by one
<>in the beginning and then the whole lot were disabled
<>from passing either urine or faeces. From men and
<>women the disease spread to the animals also. The country
<>was in a chaos and the subjects flocked to the palace to
<>complain to the King. S4arya1ti knew that somebody must
<>have in a some way tormented Cyavana and enquired
<>of each and everyone of his subjects. Whether anybody
<>had knowingly or unknowingly given pain to Cyavana.
<>Nobody had done so and the King was worried. Then
<>Sukanya1 ran to her father and confessed what she had
<>done. The King ran immediately to the place where
<>Cyavana was doing penance and striking down the earth
<>prostrated before the sage who was sitting there sad and
<>miserable. The King apologised to Cyavana and reques-
<>ted him to pardon his daughter.
<NI>Cyavana then told the King that it was enough if he
<>gave his daughter in marriage to him in expiation of the
<>harm done. The King was shocked to hear this for
<>Cyavana was not only ugly but now blind also. The
<>King returned to the palace sad and worried and there
<>was gloom over the whole palace. But Sukanya1 approa-
<>ched her father and agreed to be the wife of Cyavana.
<>The problem was solved and the King with great reluc-
<>tance took his daughter to the forests and gave her in
<>marriage to Cyavana.
<NI>4) {%Cyavana attains eternal youth.%} It was the time when
<>Indra had banned Somapa1na (drinking of the ya1ga wine)
<>to the As4vini1devas. Discontented at this they roamed
<>about in the forests and soon came to the neighbourhood
<>of the a1s4rama of Cyavana.
<NI>Sukanya1 after becoming the wife of Cyavana did all she
<>could to make her husband comfortable and happy.
<>She would collect very sweet and tasty fruit from the
<>forests and give him. She would bathe him in hot water.
<>After arranging all the materials needed for the morning
<>rites like yava, sesame, darbha and water, she would
<>take her husband to the place of the pu1ja1 leading him
<>by hand. When the morning pu1ja1 was over she would
<>seat him in a suitable place and give him rice and fruits.
<>After the meals she would give him pan to chew. Only
<>after doing all these would she go to do her daily rites
<>and that too only after obtaining her husband's permis-
<>sion. She would finish her routine in no time to come
<>back to her husband to see whether he was in need of
<>anything. Then she would make arrangements for the
<>evening pu1ja1 and after the pu1ja1 was over she would give [Page190-b+ 65]
<>him his dinner. She would eat only what was left by
<>her husband. At night she would spread a soft bedding
<>and lull him to sleep. Then she would take a nap lying
<>at the foot of her husband. During summer she would
<>fan him and during winter she would make fire to give
<>him heat. Early morning she would take him to a dis-
<>tant place for his excretion and after washing him would
<>seat him in a suitable place for cleaning his teeth and
<>face. Then the routine would start with great devotion
<>again.
<NI>One day Sukanya1 was returning from the river after her
<>bath and on the way she came across the As4vini1devas.
<>They were astounded to see such a beautiful damsel in
<>the forest and they accosted her and requested her to
<>select one of them as her husband. Knowing her identity
<>they advised her to forsake her old and blind husband and
<>come and live with one of them. She flew into a fury
<>when she heard her husband spoken of so slightingly
<>and by logical argument convinced them the error of
<>their request. Then they told her thus: “You are aware
<>we are the physicians of the devas. We can give back
<>eyesight to your husband and make him as beautiful
<>as one of us. We will then appear before you as three
<>lovely young men identical in appearance and then you
<>must select one among the three as your husband.”
<NI>Sukanya1 was immensely pleased as well as surprised to
<>hear the offer and was eager to see her husband young
<>and beautiful. But the last condition frightened her.
<>Anyhow she promised to give a reply after consulting her
<>husband and ran to the a1s4rama to tell her husband the
<>news. Cyavana advised her to accept the offer of the
<>As4vini1devas and Sukanya1 ran back to the As4vini1devas
<>and brought them to her husband accepting their terms.
<>The As4vini1devas took the aged and blind Cyavana along
<>with them to the river nearby and the three plunged into
<>the river. When they rose up after a dip the three emer-
<>ged as young, lovely and charming triplets difficult to be
<>distinguished from one another. When Sukanya1 stood
<>before the three to select her real husband she prayed to
<>her goddess and the Devi1 gave her the power to identify
<>Cyavana and so she correctly chose him from the three.
<>(Saptama Skandha, Devi1 Bha1gavata).
<NI>5) {%Cyavana defeated Indra.%} Immensely pleased at re-
<>gaining his eyesight and youth Cyavana asked the As4vini1-
<>devas what they wanted. The As4vini1devas replied that
<>Indra had banned wine to them and they would like to
<>have the ban lifted. Cyavana Mahars2i immediately
<>commenced a Somaya1ga to which were invited all the
<>devas and the As4vini1devas also. When Indra saw the
<>As4vini1devas standing to partake of the Soma wine he was
<>furious and objected to the wine being given to them.
<>Cyavana dissented and a fight ensued between Indra and
<>Cyavana. Indra raised his weapon ‘Vajra1yudha’ to
<>strike at Cyavana and then Cyavana made all his limbs
<>go stiff. He then raised from the sacrificial fire a fiendish
<>demon called Mada to kill the devas. All those present
<>were frightened by this demon and they ran away. But
<>Indra could not run for his limbs were stiff. Standing
<>there he prayed to his preceptor Br2haspati for help and
<>Br2haspati advised Indra to surrender to Cyavana. Indra
<>bowed his head before Cyavana and prayed to be ex-
<>cused. Cyavana was pleased and withdrawing the demon
<>tore him into four pieces and threw one each into Dice,
<>Hunting, Wine and Women. The devas and As4vini1devas
<>then went back to heaven. (Saptama Skandha, Devi1 [Page191-a+ 64]
<>Bha1gavata. R2gveda, Man2d2ala 1, Anuva1ka 17, Su1kta
<>116; Chapter 123, Vana Parva).
<NI>6) {%Other details.%}
<NI>(1) Paras4ura1ma once came and stayed at the a1s4rama of
<>Cyavana. At that time Bhr2gu and Cyavana were in the
<>a1s4rama. They blessed him and advised him to go to
<>Kaila1sa and do penance there to propitiate S4iva. It was
<>thus that Paras4ura1ma happened to go to Kaila1sa to do
<>penance there. (Brahma1n2d2a Pura1n2a, Chapter 62).
<NI>(2) Cyavana got a son named Pramati. This Pramati
<>was the father of Ruru and the grandfather of S4unaka.
<>(Chapter 5, A1di Parva).
<NI>(3) Cyavana married A1rus2i daughter of Manu. Aurva
<>was the son born to A1rus2i from her thigh. In descending
<>order from Aurva were born R2ci1ka--Jamadagni--
<>Paras4ura1ma. (Chapter 66, A1di Parva).
<NI>(4) A1sti1ka learnt Sa1n3ga Vedas from the a1s4rama
<>of Cyavana. (Chapter 48, A1di Parva).
<NI>(5) Cyavana was the guru of Bhi1s2ma. (S4loka 11,

<NI>2) {%The birth of the hermit Sa1rasvata.%} Dadhi1ca erected his

<>She took the child to the hermit Dadhi1ca and told him

<NI>“Brahma1, the gods and the celestial maids will be pleased

<NI>Dadhi1ci then said that the child should be named Sa1ras-
<>vata. He also added that when there was a drought conti-

<NI>3) {%How he destroyed the asuras with the head of a horse.%} Once
<>Dadhi1ci went to the world of the devas (gods).

<HI>DADHYAN3.
<F>1) The stories regarding the two hermits Dadhici and Dadhyan3 in the Pura1n2as are not very clear. In Maha1 bha1rata Dadhi1ca is men-
<>tioned as the son of Bhr2gu, and in R2gveda it occurs that Dadhi1ca was the son of Hermit Atharva who was the son of Vasis2t2ha.
<>Moreover the story of Dadhyan3 teaching the As4vini1devas the ‘Madhuvidya1’ is connected with Dadhici in some other pura1nas
<>Some statements in the R2gveda make it difficult to assume that both Dadhyan3 and Dadhi1ci are one and the same person. So relving
<>on the majority of statements in the various pura1n2as this book has accepted the fact that these hermits are separate persons and that
<>Dadhi1ci was the son of Bhr2gu whereas the hermit who taught Madhuvidya1 to the As4vini1devas was Dadhyan3.</F> A hermit. Once Indra taught this
<>hermit Madhuvidya1 (the art of mead) Indra told the
<>hermit that his head would be cut off if he taught any-
<>body this art. The As4vini1devas approached Dadhyan3
<>to learn this art. Fearing Indra the hermit refused to
<>teach them the art. As4vini1devas cut off his head and
<>buried it in a place. Then they cut off the head of a
<>horse and fixed it on the neck of Dadhyan3. Having
<>the head of the horse he taught the art to the As4vini1-
<>devas. When Dadhyan3 had finished teaching, they

<>head in place. (R2gveda, Man2d2ala 1, Anuva1ka, 17,
