<HI>DAKS2A.
<NI>1) {%Two Daks2as.%} In most of the Pura1n2as references
<>about two Daks2as occur. In some pura1n2as both are
<>referred to as one and the same person, whereas in
<>some others both are considered as separate persons,
<>so much so the stories concerning both are interlocked
<>and entangled very often. The fact is that there was
<>only one Daks2a, whose life was of two stages. The first
<>Daks2a was killed at the sacrifice of Daks2a, at which point,
<>ends the first stage, or the first Daks2a. The second stage
<>was the rebirth of the same Daks2a. A short biography
<>of Daks2a including both stages is given below:--
<NI>Brahma1 created by his mind, the seven great hermits,
<>Mari1ci, An3giras, Atri, Pulastya, Vasis2t2ha, Pulaha and
<>Kratu. So these seven hermits are called the Ma1nasa-
<>putras (sons born from mind) of Brahma1. After this,
<>from the anger of Brahma1, Rudra was born, and from
<>his lap Na1rada, from his right thumb Daks2a, from
<>his mind the Sanakas and from his left thumb a daughter
<>named Vi1ran2i1 were born.
<Poem>Vi1ran2i1 na1ma tasya1 stu
<>asikni1tya1pi sattama1</Poem>
<NI>From this passage which occurs in Ka1lika1 Pura1n2a it
<>may be assumed that ‘Asikni1’ was another name of
<>Vi1ran2i1. Daks2a did penance in the mountain of Vindhya
<>for a long time. It is mentioned in Bha1gavata, Skandha
<>8, that Maha1vis2n2u appeared before Daks2a and gave
<>him Asikni1 as his wife.
<NI>Several sons were born to Daks2a by his wife Asikni1.
<>The last one was a daughter named Sati1 who became
<>the wife of S4iva. At this time Daks2a performed a sacrifice.
<>As he was not invited to that sacrifice, S4iva sent
<>Vi1rabhadra and Bhadraka1li1 and killed Daks2a. Though
<>the devas put the head of a goat in place of the lost
<>head and brought Daks2a to life again, no reference
<>is made about the life of Daks2a after the sacrifice. Thus
<>the first stage of the life of Daks2a ends. (Devi1 Bha1ga-
<>vata, Skandha 7).
<NI>After this, once the Pracetases (the ten sons of Barhis)
<>were engaged in penance and the earth was not pro-
<>perly cultivated in consequence of which big trees [Page193-a+ 65]
<>grew up and the whole of the earth was changed into
<>a big forest. The sky was covered with tree tops. It
<>was difficult even for the wind to pass through them
<>and thus the forest thrived for ten thousand years. The
<>pracetases got out of the sea after penance and entered
<>the shore. When they saw the big forest they got angry
<>and the ten of them discharged fire and wind from
<>their faces. The wind up-rooted the trees and the fire
<>dried them. Thus almost the whole of the trees were
<>consumed. Then Candra, the King of the medicinal
<>herbs came there and told the Pracetases “Oh, Kings,
<>hold your anger. I will make the trees conclude a
<>treaty with you. The beautiful Ma1ris2a1 who was born
<>to the trees is my foster-daughter. I give her to you
<>as your wife. From half the portion of your minds
<>and half the portion of my mind the great and wise
<>praja1pati Daks2a is going to take birth in her womb”.
<NI>The Pracetases controlled their anger and accepted
<>Ma1ris2a1 as their wife, at the words of Somadeva (Candra)
<>and as the son of the ten pracetases the Praja1pati Daks2a
<>was born. With this the second stage of the life of Daks2a
<>begins. The important occurrences in the two stages
<>of the life of Daks2a are given below in their order.
<NI>2) {%Daks2a's creation of the prajas or subjects.%} Once Brahma1
<>called Daks2a and ordered him to create praja1s or sub-
<>jects. Accordingly Daks2a created the gods, the hermits,
<>the Gandharvas (Demi-gods), the Asuras, the serpents
<>etc. Seeing that the subjects he created were not pro-
<>liferating as required he thought of creating subjects
<>by coition of male and female; and he begot by his
<>wife Asikni1 five thousand sons, and they were called
<>Haryas4vas. They in their turn were about to create
<>subjects when Na1rada appeared before them and said
<>“Oh, Haryas4vas, you are mere children and ignorant
<>of the secrets of this world. How do you intend to
<>create subjects? You fools, since you have the power
<>to go up and down and lengthwise and breadthwise,
<>why don't you try to find out the extremities of the
<>earth?” Hearing the words of Na1rada, they all ran
<>in different directions and have not returned since.
<>Thus Daks2a lost the Haryas4vas. Daks2a again begot
<>thousand sons by Asikni1 and they were called S4abala1s4vas.
<>Seeing that they also were about to create subjects
<>Na1rada scattered them also, by some tricks. The
<>S4abala1s4vas who had gone to see the end of the earth
<>have not yet returned.
<NI>Daks2a got angry with Na1rada and cursed him thus:
<>“You also, like my children shall wander from place
<>to place all over the earth”. From that dry onwards
<>Na1rada became a wanderer, without a fixed dwelling
<>place. The wise Praja1pati Daks2a again begot sixty
<>damsels by Asikni1. Ten of the girls were given to Dharma-
<>deva, thirteen of them to Kas4yapa, twentyseven of
<>them to Soma, and four of them to Aris2t2anemi. Of
<>the rest two were given to Ba1huputra, two to An3giras
<>and two to the wise Kr2s4a1s4va. The names of the wives
<>of each are given below:--
<NI>(1) {%Kas4yapa.%} Aditi, Diti, Danu, Aris2t2a1, Surasa1, Khasa1,
<>Surabhi, Vinata1, Ta1mra1, Krodhavas4a1, Ira1, Kadru1,
<>Muni.
<NI>(2) {%Dharmadeva.%} Arundhati1, Vasu, Yami1, Lam3ba1,
<>Bha1nu, Marutvati1, San3kalpa1, Muhu1rta1, Sa1dhya1,
<>Vis4va1.
<NI>(3) {%Soma.%} As4vayuk, Bharan2i, Kr2ttika1, Rohin2i1, Mrga-
<>s4iras, Ta1rakam (Ardra1), Punarvasu, Pus2yam, A1s4le- [Page193-b+ 64]
<>s2am, Janakam, Pha1lguni1, Uttarapha1lgunam, Hastam,
<>Citra1, Sva1ti1, Vis4a1kham, Anura1dha1, Jyes2t2ha1, Mu1lam,
<>Purva1s2a1d2ham, Uttara1s2a1d2ham, S4ron2a, S4ravis2t2ha1, Pracetas,
<>Purvapros2t2hapadam, Uttarapros2t2hapadam, Revati1.
<NI>The names of the wives given to An3giras, Aris2t2anemi,
<>Ba1huputra and Kr2s4a1s4va are not mentioned.
<NI>Besides these damsels, twentyfour daughters were born
<>to Daks2a of his wife Prasu1ti who was the sister of Utta1na-
<>pa1da. Dharmadeva married thirteen of them also, named
<>S4raddha1, Laks2mi1, Dhr2ti, Tus2t2i, Medha1, Pus2t2i, Kriya1,
<>Buddhi, Lajja1, Vapus, S4a1nti, Siddhi, Ki1rti. Of the rest,
<>Khya1ti was given to Bhr2gu, Sati1 to S4iva, Sam3bhu1ti to
<>Mari1ci, Smr2ti to An3giras, Pri1ti to Pulastya, Ks2ama1
<>to Pulaha, Santati to Kratu, Anasu1ya1 to Atri, U1rja1
<>to Vasis2t2ha, Sva1ha1 to Agnideva and Svadha1 to the
<>Manes. (Vis2n2u Pura1n2a, Am3s4a 1, Chapter 15).
<NI>3) {%Sacrifice of Daks2a.%} Once Daks2a performed a sacri-
<>fice called Br2haspatisavana. To this sacrifice he did not
<>invite his daughter Sati1 or her husband S4iva. Daks2a
<>did not invite them because of three reasons according
<>to the Pura1n2as.
<NI>(1) Once Durva1sas, the son of Atri, went to Jambu1nada
<>and meditated with the mantra or spell of ‘Ma1ya1bi1ja’
<>and worshipped Jagadambika1 (mother of the
<>world) the goddess there. The goddess was much plea-
<>sed and gave him as a token of her pleasure the garland
<>of flowers she wore from which nectar was oozing.
<>Durva1sas wore it on his head and went to the palace of
<>Daks2a, the Praja1pati. When he saw such a wonderful
<>garland which was not of this world, he wanted to have
<>it and Durva1sas instantly gave it to Daks2a. He placed it in
<>a prominent place in his bedroom and enjoying the
<>wonderful fragrance of it he conjugated with his wife,
<>and polluted that pure garland by his lust. S4iva and his
<>wife came to know of this and they scolded Daks2a, who
<>kept this bitterness in his heart and when the sacrifice
<>was performed, he decided not to invite them. (Devi1
<>Bha1gavata, Skandha 7).
<NI>(2) S4iva was considered as a polluted man by Daks2a
<>as he had always been carrying with him the skull of
<>Brahma1 and so he thought it wrong on his part to admit
<>his daughter and son-in-law to the sacrificial hall. The
<>story of how S4iva came to have the skull, is given below.
<NI>In days of old when the whole world was under the
<>single ocean (of the great flood) the sun, the moon, the
<>fire, air everything was destroyed and darkness pre-
<>vailed. All the vegetations were destroyed. All the
<>emotions and non-emotions disappeared. The supreme
<>Lord was sleeping for so many thousands of nights
<>together. At the end of the night he assumed the attri-
<>bute of ‘Rajogun2a’ (activity) and got ready to create
<>the universe. From the face of the Supreme Lord and
<>creator of the universes came out Brahma1 with five faces.
<>In the same way, S4iva with three eyes and matted hair,
<>and trident and rosary of beads (Elaeo carpus) and with
<>attribute of ‘Tamas’ (darkness--destruction) also came
<>out. Both Brahma1 and S4iva were filled with egoism and
<>both began to quarrel with each other. It was a contest
<>as to who was greater. The contest ended in attack.
<>S4iva plucked off the fifth head of Brahma1, when the
<>same face, flushed with anger, scolded S4iva. The head
<>fell into the hands of S4iva, who could not throw it down
<>as it didn't come off from his hand. Brahma1 cursed
<>S4iva and made him polluted. Since S4iva became polluted, [Page194-a+ 64]
<>his wife Sati1 also was considered as polluted by Daks2a.
<>(Va1mana Pura1n2a, Chapter 2).
<NI>(3) Once the praja1patis performed a sacrifice. Brahma1,
<>Vis2n2u and S4iva were present there. Daks2a, who had been
<>invited by the praja1patis also was present. When Daks2a
<>entered the hall his son-in-law S4iva did not rise up.
<>This arrogance of S4iva made Daks2a angry. Daks2a made
<>up his mind to take vengeance on S4iva, and performed
<>a sacrifice known as Br2haspatisavana. To that sacrifice
<>he did not invite S4iva or Sati1.
<NI>Hearing that her father was conducting a sacrifice Sati1
<>came uninvited. But Daks2a did not even look at her.
<>Sati1devi whose heart was broken at this treatment made
<>a fire and jumped into it and died. S4iva became furious
<>when he came to know of this. Being overwhelmed with
<>sorrow and anger he beat his matted hair on the ground,
<>from which two monsters, Vi1rabhadra and Bhadraka1li1,
<>came out. They ran to Daks2a's sacrificial hall and des-
<>troyed everything they saw, killed everybody, caught
<>hold of Daks2a and cut off his head. Then they began to
<>create havoc in the whole world. The hermits and
<>sages began to take to flight. Men and animals shivered.
<>Thus the three worlds began to tremble with fear. The
<>gods approached S4iva with supplication to curb his
<>anger. The gracious S4an3kara was pleased to recall the
<>monsters. After that everybody requested him to bring
<>Daks2a back to life. But the search made for the head of
<>Daks2a was futile. At last Brahma1 took the head of a
<>goat and joined it to the headless trunk of Daks2a. Thus
<>he was brought to life again.
<F>1. Though Daks2a was brought to life again with the head of a goat there is no mention of him again in the Pura1n2as. We hear of
<>Daks2a again as the son of Ma1ris2a1.</F> (Devi1 Bha1gavata,
<>Skandha 7).
<NI>4) {%Cursing Candra.%} Candra had married twentyseven
<>daughters of Daks2a. But he showed particular attach-
<>ment to Rohin2i1. The other twentysix wives could not
<>bear this. They made a complaint to their father Daks2a.
<>Daks2a called Candra and advised him to show equal
<>attachment to all his wives and not to show any partia-
<>lity to anyone. Candra did not gainsay his father-in-
<>law, but he did not make any change in his disposition.
<>After a few days all the daughters except Rohin2i went
<>to the palace of Daks2a and told him that they were
<>going to stay with him. This time Daks2a got angry and
<>called Candra and cursed him to become a sick man
<>(of consumption).
<NI>From that day onwards Candra became a patient affec-
<>ted with consumption. All the vegetations in the world
<>began to weaken. It appeared that the world was
<>about to be destroyed. So the gods approached Daks2a
<>and requested him to show some leniency towards
<>Candra. Daks2a accordingly called Candra and told
<>him that he would be affected by consumption only for a
<>fortnight and after that he would recover gradually.
<>Candra went to Prabha1sa ti1rtha and Sarasvati1 ti1rtha and
<>dipped in water. He began to recover from that day.
<>It is according to the curse of Daksa that Candra (Moon)
<>waxes and wanes. (M.B. S4alya Parva, Chapter 35).
<NI>5) {%Other information.%}
<NI>(1) Daks2a has another name ‘Kan’.
<>“Daks2a the Praja1pati is a son of ten fathers. He has
<>two names. They are Daks2a and Kan.” (M.B. S4a1nti
<>Parva, Chapter 208, Stanza 7).
<NI>(2) When the emperor Pr2thu milked the earth, Daks2a [Page194-b+ 63]
<>was crowned as the King of the subjects. (See under
<>Pr2thu).
<NI>(3) It occurs in Maha1bha1rata, Sabha1 Parva, Chapter
<>11, Stanza 18, that Daks2a was a member of the assemibly
<>of Brahma1.
<NI>(4) Daks2a was one of those who visited Bhi1s2ma in his
<>bed of arrows. (M.B. S4a1nti Parva, Chapter 47).
<NI>(5) Mention is made in Maha1bha1rata, S4a1nti Parva,
<>Chapter 166 that the devas, the manes, the gandharvas,
<>the celestial maids, the Ra1ks2asas, animals and birds,
<>fishes and all living creatures were born from the daugh-
<>ters of Daks2a.
<HI>DAKSASA1VARN2I. The name of the ninth Manu.
<>During the regime of this Manu, there will be three
<>classes of devas (gods) called the Parn2as, the Mari1ci-
<>garbhas and the Sudharmans. Each of these gan2as or
<>classes will consist of twelve devas or gods. Indra, their
<>King will be known as Adbhuta. He will be mighty and
<>powerful. In that Manvantara (age of the Manu)
<>Savana, Dyutima1n, Bhavya, Vasu, Medha1tithi,
<>Jyotis2ma1n and Satya will be the Saptars2is (the seven
<>hermits) and Dhr2taketu, Di1ptiketu, Pan5cahasta, Nira1-
<>maya, Pr2thus4rava and others will be the sons of Manu,
<>Daks2asa1varn2i. (Vis2n2u Pura1n2a, Am3s4a 1, Chapter 2).
<HI>DA1KS2A1YAN2I. Daughter of Daks2a. This name is gene-
<>rally used for all the daughters of Daks2a. Occasionally
<>this name is used for the grand daughters of Daks2a
<>also.
<HI>DAKS2IN2A1. A daughter, born to Praja1pati Ruci, by his
<>wife A1ku1ti. The first Manu was Sva1yambhuva, the son
<>of Brahma1. He accepted S4ataru1pa1, his sister who be-
<>came sinless by penance, as his wife. S4ataru1pa1 gave birth
<>to two sons Priyavrata and Utta1napa1da, and two
<>daughters, Prasu1ti and A1ku1ti. Of these two daughters,
<>Prasu1ti1 was given to Praja1pati Daks2a and A1ku1ti to
<>Praja1pati Ruci. A1ku1ti gave birth to twins, a son named
<>Yajn5a and a daughter named Daks2in2a1. To Yajn5a
<>twelve sons were born by Daks2in2a1. They were a class
<>of devas (gods) called the Ya1mas in the regime of Manu
<>Sva1yambhuva (Vis2n2u Pura1n2a, Am3s4a 1, Chapter 7).
<>Now Daks2in2a1 is worshipped as a goddess. The same
<>Daks2in2a1 was reborn in the Goloka under the name
<>Sus4i1la1. At that time she was a cowherd woman and
<>friend of Ra1dha1. She liked to talk with S4ri1 Kr2s2n2a. One
<>day Ra1dha1 saw her sitting in the lap of S4ri1 Kr2s2n2a,
<>engaged in sexual sports, in a secluded place. When
<>Sus4i1la1 saw that Ra1dha1 had found them out, she be-
<>came dumbfounded with shame, and sat with bowed
<>head. S4ri1 Kr2s2n2a slowly placed Sus4i1la1 devi down and
<>instantly vanished. The angry Ra1dha1 cursed Sus4i1la1 to
<>become ashes if ever she entered the Goloka again. Then
<>Ra1dha1 ran about everywhere in search of S4ri1 Kr2s2n2a,
<>but he could not be found.
<NI>Immediately after the curse, Sus4i1la1 got down from the
<>Goloka and began to worship Maha1laks2mi1 with devotion
<>and meditation and very severe fast and vow. After
<>a long time Maha1laks2mi1 appeared to her and blessed
<>her, and Sus4i1la1 became absorbed in Maha1laks2mi1.
<>Since the absorption of Sus4i1la1 devi1, who was the rebirth
<>of Daks2in2a1devi1, in Maha1laks2mi1, the sacrifice of the
<>devas (gods) became fruitless. They were much grieved.
<>They all went to Brahma1 to find a solution. As the matter
<>was serious Brahma1 meditated upon Vis2n2u, who [Page195-a+ 65]
<>became pleased with Brahma1 and to save the devas, he
<>attracted Daks2in2a1devi1 from the body of Maha1laks2mi1
<>and gave her as a present to Brahma1. Brahma1 gave that
<>devi1 (goddess) to Yajn5apurus2a (the god of sacrifice)
<>so that the sacrifices of gods might become fruitful.
<>When Yajn5apurus2a saw that supernatural beauty he was
<>overpowered by lust and swooned. The couple spent a
<>hundred divine years in seclusion enjoying the company
<>of each other, as a result of which devi1 became pregnant.
<>The pregnancy matured in twelve divine years and she
<>gave birth to a child which was named Phalada. It is
<>this same Phalada, the son of Yajn5apurus2a and Daks2in2a1
<>who awards fruits to all actions. The learned men say
<>that Yajn5apurus2a, Daks2in2a1devi1 and Phalada divide the
<>fruits of actions among the doers. (Devi1 Bha1gavata,
<>Skandha 9).

<HI>DAKS2IN2APA1N5CA1LA. A place famous in the Pura1n2as.

<>Cam3pat. It is mentioned in Maha1bha1rata Sabha1 Parva,

<HI>DA1LBHYA (BAKADA1LBHYA). A Mahars2i of Naimi-
<>s4a1ran2ya. In Va1mana Pura1n2a, there is a story of how this
<>sage once performed a homa and burnt Dhr2tara1s2t2ra's
<>kingdom in the sacrificial fire.
<NI>Long ago some of the sages of Naimis4a1ran2ya approached
<>Dhr2tara1s2t2ra with a request for some money. Their
<>leader was the sage Da1lbhya (Baka). It was he who
<>begged Dhr2tara1s2t2ra for money. The king not only
<>refused to give money, but also insulted the sage. Pro-
<>voked at this, Da1lbhya began performing a homa in [Page195-b+ 65]
<>which Dhr2tara1s2t2ra's kingdom was the havis in the form
<>of sliced meat. This homa was performed in Avaki1rn2a-
<>maha1ti1rtha at Pr2thu1daka. As a result of it the kingdom
<>began to decline. The King was alarmed and consulted
<>great scholars and astrologers about the cause of the
<>country's decline. They told him that the cause of the
<>disaster was Da1lbhya's homa. Dhr2tara1s2t2ra at once pro-
<>ceeded with rich presents to propitiate Da1lbhya at
<>Avaki1rn2amaha1ti1rtha. Da1lbhya was pleased and as
<>desired by the King, performed homa with milk and
<>honey and revived all those who had died. (Va1mana
<>Pura1n2a, Chapter 39).
<NI>Maha1bha1rata, Sabha1 Parva, Chapter 4, Verse 11 says
<>that this Mahar2s2i flourished in Yudhis2t2hira's assembly.
<>On another occasion, he is referred to, as coming to

<>comforted Dyumatsena by saying that Satyava1n would be
<>blessed with longevity. (M.B. Vana Parva, Chapter
<>298, Verse 17).

<HI>DAMANAKA I. One of characters in a story, of Pan5ca-

<HI>DAMANAKA II. A daitya (asura) Maha1vis2n2u in his
<>incarnation as Matsya (Matsya1vata1ra), killed this asura
<>who was a dweller of the sea. Vis2n2u threw the body of

<HI>DAMANAKAM. The Ta1tiri tree (Grislea Tomentosa).
<>The ceremony of worshipping this tree is called Dama-
<>naka1rohan2avidhi. The Grislea tree is supposed to be
<>Bhairava, who had been changed to a tree by the curse
<>of S4iva. A story to this effect occurs in the Pura1n2as.
<NI>Bhairava was formed from the anger of S4iva. Bhairava,
<>against the will of S4iva, destroyed the devas, and so
<>S4iva cursed him and turned him to a Ta1tiri tree. It is
<>called Damanaka because Bhairava tried to do ‘damana’
<>(restraint or punishment) to the gods.
<NI>Bhairava felt sorry at the curse of S4iva. He requested
<>S4iva to withdraw the curse. At last S4iva had pity on him
<>and said that all would worship Damanaka tree. The
<>method of that worship is called Damanaka1rohan2a-
<>vidhi. It is given below:
<NI>This tree should be worshipped on the seventh or thir-
<>teenth lunar day. The worshipper should go to the
<>tree, with those who recite mantras and spells. After
<>the worship is over the worshipper should say, “O,
<>Tree, which has originated by the power of Hari, you
<>come to my presence. On behalf of S4iva, and by his
<>order, I have to take you home.” Saying thus the
<>tree should be taken home and consecrated in the even-
<>ing in accordance with the rituals. In the house, the
<>Sun, S4iva and Agni should be evoked and worshipped
<>and then the root of the tree should be planted in the
<>I1s4a1na (N.E. point) corner of the house and when it
<>is planted, the spell or incantation of Va1madeva Mantra
<>or S4iromantra should be recited. In the same way
<>the trunk with the branches of the tree is planted on
<>the Northern part. The fallen leaves and flowers
<>should be placed on the eastern side. When the root
<>is planted the following prayer should be uttered:
<Poem>A1mantritosi deves4a
<>pra1tah2 ka1le maya1 prabho /
<>Kartavyastapaso la1bha1h2
<>Pu1rn2am3 sarvam3 tava1jn5aya1. //</Poem>
<>After that at dawn he should take bath and with obla-
<>tions of sweet-smelling flowers etc. should worship
<>S4iva. This is Damanaka1rohan2avidhi. (Agni Pura1n2a,
<>Chapter 80).
<HI>DAMAYANTI1 Daughter of Bhi1ma the King of Vidar-
<>bha. The most noble of all the Indian heroines, Dama-
<>yanti1 has secured a place in the literatures of almost
<>all the languages in the world. The famous hero Nala
<>is her huband.
<NI>1) {%The background of the story of Nala.%} During the time
<>of the forest life of the Pa1n2d2avas, Arjuna had gone to
<>Kaila1sa to do penance before S4iva for getting divine
<>weapons. The rest of the Pa1n2d2avas spent the time
<>till the arrival of Arjuna, in sorrow and sadness. They
<>spent miserable days one by one. At that time the her-
<>mit Br2hadas4va visited them. They welcomed the
<>hermit. In the midst of their conversation Dharma-
<>putra told the hermit with tears that he was the most
<>unhappy man in the world. Hearing this the hermit
<>told them the story of Nala, to console the Pa1n2d2avas.
<NI>2) {%The birth of Damayanti1.%} The King Bhi1ma of Vidar-
<>bha was childless for a long time. While the King was
<>spending sorrowful days, as he had no children, a
<>hermit named Dama reached his palace. The King
<>welcomed the hermit. Seeing the sorrow of the King the
<>hermit blessed the King and the queen, and as a result
<>a daughter named Damayanti1 and as her brothers [Page196-b+ 64]
<>three sons Dama, Da1nta and Damana were born to
<>them.
<NI>3) {%The Svayam3vara (marriage) of Damayanti1.%} During
<>this period a son named Nala was born to Vi1rasena the
<>King of Nis2adha. The child was an expert in the game
<>of dice and he would speak only truth. He grew up
<>and became a youth.
<NI>Once the swans which came to the palace of Dama-
<>yanti1 from Nala, began to praise Nala. They sang
<>about the glory of Nala. Nala also knew about the
<>beauty and the other good qualities of Damayanti1.
<>Thus they fell in love with each other. Nala also started
<>for Kun2d2inapuri1, the capital city of Vidarbha, to marry
<>Damayanti1. The devas (gods) Indra, Agni, Varun2a
<>and Yama, having heard of the beauty of Damayanti1
<>from Na1rada, had gone Kun2d2inapuri1, to take part
<>in the marriage. On the way they met Nala. They
<>were aware of the fact that Damayanti1 was in love
<>with Nala. So they called Nala and sent him to Dama-
<>yanti1 with a message that Damayanti1 should accept
<>one of the four gods as her husband. By the boon of
<>invisibility, given by the Gods, Nala entered the room
<>of Damayanti1 unseen by others and gave the message
<>to Damayanti1. But Damayanti1 did not agree to it.
<>The Gods also entered the nuptial hall and took their
<>seats in the shape of four Nalas. Damayanti1 entered
<>the hall with the nuptial garland in her hands. She
<>saw five Nalas including the real Nala, and prayed to
<>the gods to show her the real Nala her lover. So the
<>devas assumed their real forms and Damayanti1 put
<>the garland on the neck of the real Nala and accepted
<>him as her husband. Being much pleased with the be-
<>haviour and character of both Nala and Damayanti1,
<>the four gods blessed them. Agni promised to be near
<>Nala whenever he thought of him. Yama said that
<>Nala would always be righteous. Varun2a said that
<>Nala would obtain water at the thought of it. Indra
<>blessed him saying that he would get moks2a immedia-
<>tely after the completion of a ya1ga. The gods remained
<>till the end of the marriage ceremony and then they
<>departed. Nala and Damayanti1 lived in Kun2d2inapuri1.
<NI>4) {%The hatred between Kali and Dva1para.%} Hearing about
<>the Svayamvara of Damayanti1 Kali and Dva1para
<>started for Kun2d2inapuri1. On the way they met Indra
<>and the other gods, who were returning from the marri-
<>age of Damayanti1. They told Kali and Dva1para that
<>Damayanti1 had accepted Nala as her husband. Kali
<>and Dva1para got angry at this and returned saying
<>that they would destroy the kingdom of Nala. After
<>that Kali waited for a chance to enter the body of
<>Nala. After twelve years, once Nala, after passing
<>urine, without washing his legs performed his evening
<>worship. At that moment Kali entered the body of
<>Nala. With that Nala became bereft of all senses of
<>righteousness and duty, and challenged his younger
<>brother Pus2kara to a game of dice. Pus2kara engaged
<>Nala in the game of dice. Kali stood in the shape of
<>an ox as helper of Nala. Nala lost his kingdom in the
<>game. Seeing that her husband was being defeated
<>continuously, Damayanti1 sent for the charioteer Va1rs2-
<>n2eya and sent her son Indrasena and daughter Indra-
<>sena1 to Kun2d2inapuri1. After leaving the children at Kun2d2i-
<>napuri1 Va1rs2n2eya engaged himself in a travel. After
<>visiting several countries he reached Ayodhya1 and [Page197-a+ 65]
<>became the charioteer of King R2tuparn2a.
<NI>5) {%Nala and Damayanti1 to the forest.%} Pus2kara got the
<>kingdom, wealth and everything that his elder brother,
<>Nala, had possessed. The miserable Nala left every-
<>thing he had and clad in only one cloth got out of the
<>palace. His wife Damayanti1 followed him. They stayed
<>outside the city for three days. Pus2kara made a pro-
<>clamation that if anybody rendered any help to Nala
<>he would be ruthlessly put to death. After that Nala stay-
<>ed there for three more days, with only water for food
<>and drink. Then he went to the forest. His wife followed
<>him. They were hungry. Nala saw some golden birds.
<>Nala wanted to catch them for food. So he took his
<>only cloth and spread it on the ground. The birds
<>took that cloth and flew away. Those birds were the
<>dices used in the game. They were influenced by
<>Kali. The miserable and hungerstricken Nala asked
<>his wife Damayanti1 to go with the caravan traders
<>who were going to Avanti and save herself. But she
<>did not consent to go away from him.
<NI>They spent that night in an inn. They both fell asleep
<>because of weakness. After a time Nala woke up.
<>He thought that his wife would somehow or other
<>get to some country closeby and save herself, if he
<>left her there. So he decided to take half of her cloth.
<>How to do it without her knowledge? Thinking so he
<>walked round the inn. He saw a good sword. He
<>took the sword, and cut half of the cloth of Damayanti1
<>and leaving her in the inn he went away.
<NI>In a short while she woke up and missing her husband
<>she cried aloud and ran here and there in search of
<>him. Then she went into the forest crying. A big snake
<>caught hold of her and began to swallow her. She cried
<>aloud. Hearing the cry, a forester came there. He
<>cut the snake open and saved Damayanti1. But when
<>he saw her he became overpowered by libido and
<>tried to make her his wife. She got angry and cursed
<>him and he instantly fell down dead. After this she
<>began to wander in the forest aimlessly, often seeing
<>wild animals and fearing them. At last she got on a
<>rock and sat there and began to wail thinking of Nala.
<NI>6) {%Damayanti1 in the kingdom of Cedi.%} While she was
<>sitting on the rock wailing, a caravan of traders came
<>by that way. They had been attacked by a herd of
<>elephants and were running away from them. They
<>saw Damayanti1. She told them her story. They brought
<>her to the kingdom of Cedi. She wandered through
<>the country for a while and finally reached the palace
<>of the King Suba1hu. Thinking her to be a mad wo-
<>man the street boys were following her. The queen
<>saw the woman surrounded by street boys and felt
<>pity for her. She sent her maid and brought her to
<>the palace. Without revealing that she was the queen
<>of King Nala, she told everything else to the queen.
<>The queen consoled her and said that she would get her
<>husband back. She invited Damayanti1 to stay in the
<>palace till she got her husband back. Damayanti1
<>replied as follows: “Mother, I shall stay here. But
<>there are certain conditions. I won't eat remains and
<>offals of victuals, I won't make others to wash my legs.
<>I won't talk with men who are not related to me. If
<>anybody hankers after me you must order him to be
<>killed. Bra1hman2as may come and see me only in con-
<>nection with the search for my husband. This is my
<>vow.” The queen agreed to her conditions and made [Page197-b+ 65]
<>her the attendant of her daughter Sunanda1.
<NI>7) {%Nala in Ayodhya1.%} Nala filled with sorrow was walk-
<>ing along the forest when he saw a wild fire. From the
<>midst of the fire somebody was calling Nala by name
<>and crying. When Nala approached the fire, he saw
<>the great and famous serpent Karkot2aka lying entwined
<>in the flames. Karkot2aka the King of the Na1gas had
<>once deceived Na1rada, who then cursed him that he
<>would become stationary in a place like an immovable
<>thing till Nala came and rescued him. From that day
<>onwards, Karkot2aka was remaining in that forest with-
<>out moving. Then the forest caught fire. Karkot2aka
<>being unable to move had to remain in the fire. Nala
<>immediately saved Karkot2aka from the fire and with
<>that he was absolved from the curse of Na1rada. Kar-
<>kot2aka asked Nala to walk forward, counting the steps.
<>When Nala counted ten, Karkot2aka bit on the leg of
<>Nala and he became ugly. Then the serpent said to
<>the flurried Nala, “Don't fear. I have made you ugly
<>so that others may not recognize you. Now you are
<>ugly because of my poison. But my poison has affected
<>Kali who is in your body. Because he had harmed
<>you who are blameless, he has to suffer for it. So long
<>as this poison is in you, nobody will harm you. You
<>must go to R2tuparn2a the King of Ayodhya1 and tell
<>him that you are Ba1huka a charioteer. You must give
<>R2tuparn2a the spell ‘As4vahr2daya mantra’ and get
<>from him the spell ‘Aks2ahr2dayamantra’. After that
<>you will have reunion with your wife and children.
<>Here are two cloths for you. Whenever you want
<>to get your original form you need only wear them.”
<>Saying these words Karkot2aka gave Nala two divine
<>garments and then disappeared.
<NI>Nala started for Ayodhya1. He reached the palace
<>on the tenth day. There he was employed as the horse
<>keeper of R2tuparn2a on a pay of hundred pieces of gold.
<>Thus he stayed there under the name Ba1huka. Va1rs2n2eya
<>and Ji1vala who were the horse-keepers of R2tuparn2a
<>upto that time, were placed under Ba1huka.
<NI>Ba1huka used to recite a poem every evening when he
<>returned from his work.
<Poem>Weary and worn of hunger and thirst,
<>That poor woman, where might she be?
<>Serving whom will she be now.
<>Thinking of the unlucky fool?</Poem>
<NI>Hearing this song of lamentation everyday, once his
<>assistant Ji1vala asked Ba1huka, whom he was reciting
<>the poem about. Ba1huka replied: “Once there was
<>a man who was a fool. He got a good wife. For some
<>reason they were separated. The fool is still wandering
<>about weary and sad in search of his wife.”
<NI>8) {%Damayanti1 in Kun2d2inapura.%} At this time Dama-
<>yanti1 was living in the palace of the King of Cedi,
<>as the maid of princess Sunanda1. King Bhi1ma became
<>very sorry not knowing where Nala and Damayanti1
<>had gone. He sent men in all directions to search for
<>them. The King proclaimed that those who found
<>them would be awarded thousand cows, lands assigned
<>to Brahmins, and villages. He proclaimed that so
<>many cows would be given even to those who could
<>give any information about them. Hearing this Bra1h-
<>man2as went in all directions and Sudeva, one of them,
<>reached the Kingdom of Cedi. He recognized Dama-
<>yanti1 who was like fire inside the cover of smoke. When
<>Damayanti1 was alone Sudeva approached her and [Page198-a+ 65]
<>said that he had come from Vidarbha and that he was
<>a friend of Damayanti1's brother. Damayanti1 recog-
<>nized Sudeva and she cried aloud. Sunanda1 who saw
<>this scene, told her mother about it. The queen sent
<>for Sudeva and asked him what the matter was. He
<>revealed everything about Damayanti1, to the queen.
<>When they heard the story all the women in the harem
<>cried. Then with tears the queen said, “Hear this,
<>Damayanti1, your mother and myself are the two daugh-
<>ters of Sudama the King of Das4a1rn2a. Father gave
<>your mother to Bhi1ma the King of Vidarbha and me
<>to Vi1raba1hu the King of Cedi. I have seen you when
<>you were an infant.”
<NI>When the King of Cedi knew everything he sent Dama-
<>yanti1 in a palanquin to Vidarbha.
<NI>9) {%The search for Nala.%} Damayanti1 told her father
<>that she didn't want to live any longer unless Nala
<>was brought to her. Bhi1ma had been trying hard to
<>find out Nala. One day Parn2a1da one of the numerous
<>Brahmins who were engaged in the search for Nala,
<>came to Bhi1ma and said, “while I was wandering from
<>place to place, I happened to reach the palace of R2tu-
<>parn2a the King of Ayodhya1. There I made enquiries
<>about Nala the husband of Damayanti1, in the pre-
<>sence of the King. But nobody gave me any answer.
<>When I returned Ba1huka the charioteer of R2tuparn2a
<>followed me. An ugly man with short hands, an ex-
<>pert in driving horses, and an excellent cook, he asked
<>me several questions about Damayanti1.”
<NI>When Damayanti1 heard this she had horripilation.
<>She secretly went to her mother and compelled her
<>to send Sudeva the Bra1hman2a to Ayodhya1. Her mother
<>consented. She called Sudeva, in the presence of her
<>mother and told him to go to Ayodhya1 and tell R2tuparn2a
<>that the second svayam3vara (marriage) of Damayanti1
<>would take place before sunrise next day and that
<>he should come earlier. Sudeva instantly went to
<>Ayodhya1.
<NI>R2tuparn2a, hearing the words of Sudeva, wanted
<>Ba1huka to take him in the chariot to Vidarbha within
<>the period of a day time for the second svayam3vara
<>of Damayanti1. Nala consented with a breaking heart.
<>Immediately they started. Va1rs2n2eya also got into
<>the chariot. The chariot flew through the sky with
<>tremendous speed, to Vidarbha. On the way the upper
<>garment of the King fell on the ground. He ordered
<>the chariot to be stopped for taking his upper garment.
<>Nala said that within that winking time the chariot
<>had travelled a yojana (league) and gloried in his
<>power of driving horses. Then they saw in the forest
<>a Tanni tree (Terminalia bellerica) with fruits. Seeing
<>the Tanni tree the King said, “O Ba1huka, if you are
<>an expert in horse-driving I am an expert in reckoning.
<>I will tell you how many leaves and nuts there are
<>in that Tanni Tree. There are five crores of leaves in
<>both the branches together and two thousand and
<>ninetyfive nuts. They stopped the chariot and checked
<>the tree and found the reckoning of the King correct.
<>The King had the knowledge of the art called ‘Aks2ahr2-
<>daya’. It was with this art that the king calculated
<>the number of leaves in the tree, at one look. Ba1huka
<>drove the chariot with the speed of wind because he had
<>known the art called ‘As4vahr2daya’. Then and there,
<>Ba1huka taught the King the art of ‘As4vahr2daya’ and
<>the King taught Ba1huka the art of ‘Aks2ahr2daya’. The [Page198-b+ 65]
<>moment Nala learned Aks2ahr2daya Kali vomitted
<>the poison of Karkot2aka and got out of the body of
<>Nala. Long ago, the mother of Indrasena, a god, had
<>cursed Kali and drove him out. Since then Kali had
<>been living in the body of Nala. As soon as he came
<>out Kali begged Nala for pardon. Nala curbed his
<>anger. But Kali was afraid of Nala. So he made the
<>Tanni tree his abode. Because of that Tanni became
<>detestable.
<NI>R2tuparn2a, Va1rs2n2eya and Ba1huka reached Kun2d2ina-
<>pura in the evening. When the sound of the chariot
<>of Nala reached the ears of Damayanti1 her heart bumped
<>with joy. The chariot was stopped and R2tuparn2a
<>entered the palace. The King Bhi1ma welcomed
<>R2tuparn2a heartily. As there was no sign of any prepara-
<>tion for the svayam3vara (marriage) R2tuparn2a under-
<>stood that some sort of trick had been played on him.
<NI>10) {%Test of Nala.%} Damayanti1 sent her maid Kes4ini1
<>to Ba1huka to watch him. Kes4ini1 had a secret talk
<>with Ba1huka. Though Nala did not reveal himself,
<>he cried when she talked about Damayanti1. Kes4ini1
<>returned to Damayanti1 and told her what she had
<>heard and seen. Damayanti1 became more and more
<>convinced that Ba1huka was Nala himself So she sent
<>Kes4ini1 again to Nala. She discovered the following
<>facts about Ba1huka, after a keen observation, and
<>made a report of them.
<NI>(1) Ba1huka does not stop to pass through even a small
<>door. The upper sill lifts by itself for him to pass
<>through.
<NI>(2) He gets enough room for him to pass through even
<>a big crowd.
<NI>(3) Empty pots are filled with water at a look from him.
<NI>(4) When he stretches out a grass to the sun, it catches
<>fire.
<NI>(5) Fire does not burn him even if he touches it.
<NI>(6) When he crushes a flower, it blooms more beauti-
<>ful and fragrant than before.
<NI>When Kes4ini1 said all these facts to Damayanti1, she
<>became fully convinced that Ba1huka was none other
<>than Nala himself. Damayanti1 sent Kes4ini1 again to
<>Ba1huka to get some meat cooked by him. Damayanti1
<>tested the taste of the meat brought by Kes4ini1 and
<>she knew that the food was cooked by Nala. She
<>sent Kes4ini1 again to Ba1huka with her children. See-
<>ing Indrasena and Indrasena1 coming to him, Ba1huka
<>ran to them, gathered them and embraced them and
<>cried aloud. Then he told Kes4ini1 that he had done
<>so because the children were just like his two child-
<>ren.
<NI>11) {%Reunion.%} After having tested Ba1huka so far Dama-
<>yanti1 sent Kes4ini1 to her mother to tell her everything,
<>and to say that she was convinced of the fact that
<>Ba1huka was none other than Nala and that the diffe-
<>rence was only in shape and to request that she may
<>be permitted to see Ba1huka in person. The queen
<>informed the King of all these facts. With the permis-
<>sion of her parents Ba1huka was brought to the room
<>of Damayanti1. The moment Ba1huka saw Damayanti1
<>he began to shed tears. Damayanti1 also was filled
<>with emotion. But she told him thus:--
<NI>“Ba1huka, have you seen a man who had left his sleep-
<>ing wife in the forest? Who else, but Nala the famous
<>and the righteous would discard his blameless and
<>loving wife who had been sleeping, with fatigue and [Page199-a+ 65]
<>hunger in a lonely place? Have I done anything
<>wrong to him in my younger days so that he should
<>leave me in the forest when I was sleeping? Leaving
<>aside real gods I married him. I worshipped, him. I had
<>children from his blood and now he has abandoned
<>me. In the presence of Gods and with fire as witness,
<>holding my hands he had taken a vow that he would
<>support and protect me. Where has that vow gone?”
<>At these words Ba1huka shed tears for a long time.
<>Then he told her all that had happened to him since
<>their separation. Damayanti1 requested Nala, not to
<>have any misconception regarding the idea of a second
<>svayam3vara; she said to him that she committed no
<>wrong and that her fidelity and loyalty had undergone
<>no change. At that time Va1yu (the wind-god) in an
<>etherial voice said “Damayanti1 has committed no
<>sin”. Immediately Nala put on the divine clothes
<>given by Karkot2aka and regained his original form.
<>Damayanti1 embraced Nala and cried aloud. The king
<>her father, mother and the people of the city all ran
<>to them.
<NI>Next day R2tuparn2a returned. After a few days Nala
<>collected an army consisting of three hundred soldiers,
<>sixteen elephants, fifty horses and a white chariot only,
<>from Vidarbha and went to the kingdom of Nis2adha.
<>Reaching there Nala challenged Pus2kara for a game of
<>dice. Pus2kara refused the challenge. Nala took his
<>sword to cut Pus2kara into two. Finally Pus2kara agreed
<>to play. At the first cast of the dice the life and the
<>Kingdom of Pus2kara fell into the hands of Nala. But
<>Nala did not kill Pus2kara; instead, he embraced him.
<>Nala became King. Damayanti1 and the children reached
<>Nis2adha. Nala ruled the country with more happiness
<>and prosperity than before. (Maha1bha1rata, Vana
<>Parva, Chapters 52 to 79).

<HI>DAM3BHODBHAVA. An ancient emperor. He was a
<>mighty warrior prince. He brought the whole world
<>under control. At last there was nobody left to fight
<>with. So he walked about challenging everybody he met
<>with. But none dared to fight with him. Then Brahma1

<>So the emperor went with his mighty army to Gandha-
<>ma1dana and informed Nara and Na1ra1yan2a of his desire

<>that they had no power to fight. But Dam3bhodbhava was
<>not satisfied. Finally Nara and Na1ra1yan2a took some
<>is2i1ka1 grass and began to fight the emperor with it.
<>Though the army of the emperor was mighty and vast
<>it could not stand against the grass in the hands of the [Page199-b+ 65]
<>hermits. Finally the emperor admitted failure and
<>bowed before the hermits. They advised him not to be
<>arrogant in future and to lead a pure life. Hearing the
<>exhortations of the hermits the emperor became a
<>devoted man. He returned to his palace and led a life

<HI>DA1NAM. Gift. In ancient India a spiritual significance
<>was attached to da1nam. (offering of a free gift). One
<>who gave water was said to achieve contentment; one

<>one who gave a horse would attain the world of the
<>As4vini1devas; one who gave bulls would get prosperity;
<>one who gave cows would reach su1ryaloka; according to
<>Manusmr2ti.

<HI>DAND2A VI (DAN2D2AKA). A thief who had made the
<>world tremble in the Dva1para yuga. This wicked Dan2d2-
<>aka used to steal the wealth of the Bra1hman2as, kill
<>those who trusted him, speak lies, rape the women of
<>others, drink liquor, eat the flesh of cows, mingle with
<>wicked people and do various other sinful deeds daily.
<>Dan2d2a once went to a Vais2n2avite temple to commit
<>robbery. When he reached the steps of the temple he
<>wiped his legs which were wet, on the ground and a
<>small depression that was there, was levelled by the [Page200-b+ 65]
<>drawing of his legs. He broke the lock with an iron rod
<>and entered the temple. Inside the temple he saw Kr2s2n2a,
<>clad in yellow robes lying on a beautiful cot, with his
<>spouse Ra1dha1. The thief instantly bowed before the
<>spouse of Ra1dha1. By this act his sins were washed away.
<>Still impelled by his evil nature, he took the silk garment
<>of Kr2s2n2a, spread it on the ground, collected as many
<>things as he could take, placed them in the garment and
<>making them into a bundle, got out of the temple
<>with shaky feet. The bundle fell on the ground with a
<>loud noise. Hearing the noise the neighbours woke up
<>and looked out. The thief began to run and was bitten
<>by a poisonous snake and fell down dead. The men of
<>Yama (God of death) tied the spirit of the thief with a
<>rope and took him before Yama. Citragupta looked
<>into his accounts and informed Yama that he had
<>committed all the sins. Yama asked if there was any
<>good deed to his account. Then Citragupta said “When
<>this great sinner was going to commit theft in a Vais2n2a-
<>vite temple, at the steps of the temple, he levelled a pit
<>by the mud on his leg, and that single good deed on
<>his part has wiped out all his sins.” Hearing this Yama
<>gave him a golden seat and showed hospitality and then
<>bowed before him and said “By the soil on your legs you
<>have purified my abode today. I am grateful to you.
<>Now you may go to the blissful world of Vis2n2u where
<>there are no deaths or births or sorrow”. Hearing this
<>Dan2d2a entered Vaikun2t2ha. (Padma Pura1n2a, Chapter 1).

<HI>DAN2D2AKETU. A warrior who fought on the side

<HI>DAN2D2ANI1TI. (The laws of chastisement). Dan2d2a-
<>ni1ti is the law of punishment given to the subjects
<>by Kings of ancient India. According to the criminal
<>laws of ancient India, fining a man upto two hundred
<>and fifty pan2as (a coin) was called Prathama S4a1hasa
<>dan2d2a (first degree of punishment) and fining up to
<>five hundred pan2as was called Madhyama Sa1hasa [Page201-b+ 65]
<>dan2d2a (medium punishment) and fining upto thou-
<>sand pan2as was called Uttama Sa1hasa dan2d2a (highest
<>degree of punishment). If a man complains to
<>the King that his property is stolen, when actually
<>it is not stolen, the man will be fined the amount
<>that is said to have been stolen. There was another
<>law, that the owner of the stolen property and
<>the thief would have to pay double the amount
<>they admit, if the amount shown in the complaint
<>differed from the actual amount stolen. For appear-
<>ing as false witness, the three classes of non-Brahmins
<>were punished with fine and if he was a Brahmin he
<>would have to be cast out of the society. The King
<>ordered that if one spent the property entrusted with
<>him for keeping, the owner of the property would
<>have to be paid double the amount. If one says that
<>he has given the property for keeping when actually
<>it is not, the person will have to be punished as if he
<>were a thief. If a man spends an amount without
<>knowing that it was the property of others, the man
<>will have to be acquitted of the charge.
<NI>If an artisan has received money in advance for a parti-
<>cular product and fails to give the thing at the stipu-
<>lated time the King will fine the artisan one svarn2a.
<>If a man received money in advance on a promise to
<>do a particular work and if he fails to do the work he
<>will be fined one Kr2s2n2ala of gold. (One Kr2s2n2ala
<>weighs three yavagrains). If a man conceals his diseases
<>and marries a damsel, she will be considered as a spins-
<>ter and the man will be fined two hundred pan2as.
<>If a cowherd agrees to look after the cows of another
<>receiving pay and food and refuses to return the cow
<>to the owner the King will fine him hundred pan2as.
<>If a Ks2atriya scoffed a Bra1hman2a the fine imposed
<>would be hundred pan2as. If it is a Vais4ya the fine will
<>be two hundred pan2as. If it is a S4u1dra he will be put
<>to death. If a Brahmin scoffs a Ks2atriya the fine is
<>fifty pan2as. If a S4u1dra scoffs a Vais4ya the fine is twenty-
<>five pan2as. If a Brahmin scoffs a S4u1dra the fine is
<>only twelve pan2as. If a S4u1dra scoffs a Ks2atriya his
<>tongue should be cut off. If a S4u1dra advises a Brahmin,
<>he may inflict any punishment on the S4u1dra.
<NI>If a S4u1dra wronged a Brahmin, the S4u1dra might be
<>punished by cutting off the organ which was employed
<>in the crime. If one spat at a Brahmin, the two lips of
<>the culprit might be cut off. If a man passed urine on a
<>Brahmin the culprit would have his penis cut off. If
<>a S4u1dra sits on the seats of the upper classes his but-
<>tock will have to be cut off. If one slays cow, elephant,
<>camel or horse half of his limbs will have to be cut
<>off. Cutting across a road, changing of boundaries,
<>making the water of pools and ponds impure etc. will
<>have to be punished with a fine of two suvarn2as. (Agni
<>Pura1n2a, Chapter 227).

<HI>DAS4ARATHA. (Nemi). A famous king of the Iks2va1ku

<NI>1) {%Genealogy.%} Descended from Vis2n2u in the following order.
<>Brahma1--Mari1ci--Kas4yapa--Vivasva1n--Vaivasvata
<>Manu--Iks2va1ku--Vikuks2i--S4as4a1da--Kakutstha--Ane-
<>nas--Pr2thula1s4va--Prasenajit--Yuvana1s4va--Ma1ndha1ta1--
<>Purukutsa--Trasadasyu--Anaran2ya--Haryas4va--Vasu-
<>manas--Sudhanva1--Traiyya1run2a--Satyavrata (Tris4an3ku)
<>Haris4candra--Rohita1s4va--Ha1ri1ta--Cun5cu--Sudeva--
<>Bharuka--Ba1huka--Sagara--Asaman5jas--Am3s4uma1n--
<>Bhagi1ratha--Sr2tana1bha--Sindhudvi1pa--Ayuta1yus--
<>R2tuparn2a--Sarvaka1ma--Suda1s--Mitrasaha--(Kalma1-
<>s2apa1da)--As4maka--Mu1laka--Khat2van3ga (Dili1pa;
<>Di1rgha ba1hu)--Raghu--Aja--Daas4ratha.
<NI>2) {%Birth.%} Das4aratha was the son of Aja, of the family
<>of Iks2va1ku, born of his wife Indumati1.
<F>1) It is stated in Maha1bha1rata, Vana Parva, Chapter 274, Stanza 6 that ‘Ilabila1, was the name of the mother of Das4aratha.</F>
<NI>3) {%Das4aratha's hunt.%} Once during the early part of his
<>life Das4aratha was walking through a forest engaged in
<>hunting. He reached the bank of the river Sarayu1.
<>It was evening and the forest was thick. He walked in
<>search of wild animals. The night was advancing and
<>darkness getting thicker. Then he heard a sound from
<>the river as if an elephant was drinking water. Thinking
<>it to be an elephant Das4aratha sent an arrow in the
<>direction from which the sound came. Instantly he heard
<>a man crying with pain. The king was disappointed.
<>He ran to the spot and saw a hermit boy lying in a pool
<>of blood beating his limbs on the ground and crying.
<>The waterpot he had been dipping in the water lay
<>close by. In answer to the questions of the King, the [Page204-a+ 65]
<>hermit boy whose name was S4ravan2a,
<F>1. In Agnipura1n2a Chapter 6, it is stated that the name of this hermit boy was Yajn5adatta.</F> said, “Oh
<>King! What wrong have I done? My parents are
<>sitting thirsty and blind with age, in the hermitage close-
<>by. I, their only son, was dipping the pot to take water
<>to them, when you sent the arrow at me. So, please take
<>some water to them in this pot and console them.”
<NI>Hearing this, with tears the King drew out the fatal
<>arrow from the body of the boy and with that the boy
<>S4ravan2a said good bye to the world. Das4aratha took
<>water in the pot and went in search of the hermitage in
<>the darkness. With difficulty he found out the hermitage
<>and as he drew near, the aged parents of the boy heard
<>his footsteps and called him eagerly. The King, with
<>tearful eyes told them what had happened. There was
<>loud wailing and crying in the hermitage. According to
<>their wish the King took them to where their son lay.
<>Then the King made a fire and placed the dead body
<>of the boy in it. The aged and blind parents cursed
<>Das4aratha, “You also will die of loss of children”.
<>Then they also entered the fire and were burnt along
<>with their son's dead body. (Va1lmi1ki Ra1ma1yan2a, Ayo-
<>dhya1 Ka1n2d2a, Sarga 63).
<NI>4) {%Marriage.%} Das4aratha had three wives, named
<>Kaus4alya1, Kaikeyi1 and Sumitra1.
<NI>5) {%Kaikeyi1 given a boon.%} There was a great battle in the
<>world of the gods between the asuras and the gods.
<>According to the request of the devas, Das4aratha went to
<>the world of the devas to help them. Kaikeyi1 also went
<>with Das4aratha. In a severe fight with S4ambara, an
<>asura, Das4aratha fell down unconscious. Kaikeyi1 took
<>him away from the battle-field. When he recovered the
<>King got into the chariot and fought more fiercely than
<>before. This time the wheel-bolt of one of the wheels of
<>the chariot of Das4aratha slipped away. Without inform-
<>ing her husband of this danger, Kaikeyi1 inserted her
<>finger into the bolt-hole and prevented the wheel from
<>sliding away. The King won the battle. After the battle,
<>when the King came to know of the services rendered
<>by Kaikeyi1, he promised her two boons. Kaikeyi1 told
<>the King that she would ask for them later, when she
<>needed them. Then they returned to Ayodhya1. (Va1lmi1ki
<>Ra1ma1yan2a, Sarga 9, Kam3pa Ra1ma1yan2a, Ba1la Ka1n2d2a).
<NI>6) {%Administration of Das4aratha.%} The capital of Das4a-
<>ratha's kingdom of Kosala, was Ayodhya1. This city was
<>situated on the bank of river Sarayu1. Das4aratha was as
<>famous in Ayodhya1 as Indra was in the realm of the
<>gods. There were palaces in Ayodhya1 for the kings who
<>brought tribute to stay. In short, as mentioned in
<>Va1lmi1ki Ra1ma1yan2a, Ba1la Ka1n2d2a, Sarga 5, Ayodhya1 was
<>the heaven on earth.
<NI>7) {%Ministers.%} Das4aratha had eight ministers, Sr2s2t2i,
<>Jayanta, Vijaya, Siddha1rtha, Ra1s2t2ravardhana, As4oka,
<>Dharmapa1la, and Sumantra. (Agni Pura1n2a, Chapter 6).
<NI>8) {%The name Das4aratha.%} The real name of Das4aratha
<>was Nemi. Once the unattackable and invincible asura
<>S4am3bara conquered the throne of Indra. At the request
<>of Brahma1 and the gods, this King reached heaven and
<>destroyed the armies of the asuras very easily. S4am3bara
<>got angry, assumed ten shapes and attacked the King
<>from ten points at the same time. The king confronted
<>the ten S4am3baras at ten points, at the same time, and
<>killed all of them at the same moment. Because he
<>faced his chariot to ten points at the same time and
<>fought with enemies on those ten points Brahma1 appre- [Page204-b+ 64]
<>ciated his valiant fighting and charioteeering and gave
<>him the name ‘Das4aratha’ (one who is capable of driving
<>the chariot to ten points at the same time). Thus his
<>original name was forgotten and he came to be known
<>only by the name given him later. (Kam3pa Ra1ma1yan2a,
<>Yuddha Ka1n2d2a).
<NI>9) {%Birth of Sons.%} Kausalya1 was the first wife of Das4a-
<>ratha. She was the daughter of the King of Uttara
<>Kosala. A daughter named S4a1nta1 was born to Das4aratha
<>by Kausalya1. After this, no sons or daughters were born
<>to Das4aratha for a long time.
<NI>At this juncture Lomapa1da, the king of An3ga who was the
<>class-mate and a great friend of Das4aratha, came to
<>Ayodhya1 on a friendly visit. He also had no children.
<>So he entreated Das4aratha to give S4a1nta1 to him as a
<>foster-daughter. Thus he took away S4a1nta1 to An3ga.
<>Lomapa1da gave S4a1nta1 in marriage to R2s2yas2r2n3ga a
<>hermit. (See under R2s2yas4r2n3ga).
<NI>As Kausalya1 was childless Das4aratha brought as his
<>wife Kaikeyi1 the daughter of the King of Kekaya and
<>sister of Yudha1jit. Still no children were born to them.
<>He was much disappointed. At last he married again and
<>brought Sumitra1 the princess of Ka1s4i. Of these three,
<>Kausalya1 was the chief wife.
<NI>Though he had three wives, Das4aratha still remained
<>childless. The King and his queens spent their days in
<>sorrow for a long time. The King remembered the curse
<>he had incurred when he was young. The curse was
<>that as they had died with sorrow at the death of their
<>son S4ravan2a, the same thing would happen to me.
<>So he believed that sons would be born to him.
<NI>He performed several devotional acts to get children.
<>Finally he gave up all kingly pleasures and began to
<>lead an ascetic life. He built a temple for his own use
<>and consecrated the idol of Maha1vis2n2u in it. Then entrust-
<>ing the administration of the Kingdom to his ministers
<>he and his wives engaged themselves in daily devotion
<>and meditation in the temple. Then the King decided
<>to perform the sacrifice of Putraka1mes2t2i (sacrifice for
<>getting children) by the hermit R2s2yas4r2n3ga under the
<>guidance of Vasis2t2ha. The King informed R2s2yas4r2n3ga
<>of his decision. R2s2yas4r2n3ga could not refuse the King's
<>request as the king was his father-in-law. Moreover
<>Lomapa1da and S4a1nta1 also requested the hermit to com-
<>ply with the desire of Das4aratha. So R2s2yas4r2n3ga came to
<>Ayodhya1 and the sacrifice of Putraka1mes2t2i was begun.
<>The air vibrated with the recitation of mantras (spells and
<>incantations) and the Veda Su1ktas. The hermit uttered
<>the divine spell of Putraka1mes2t2i and offered oblations
<>in the sacrificial fire. Then a wonderful and luminous
<>figure came out of the fire with a pot containing a
<>pudding of ambrosia, and placed the pot before R2s2ya-
<>s2r2n3ga and then disappeared in the sacrificial fire. When
<>the wonderful figure disappeared, R2s2yas4r2n3ga took the
<>pot of pudding and gave it to Das4aratha with prayer and
<>incantations. Das4aratha received the golden pot and in
<>accordance with the instruction of the hermit, divided
<>the pudding between his first wife Kaus4alya1 and second
<>wife Kaikeyi1, both of whom gave half of their share to
<>Sumitra1. Thus the three wives ate the divine pudding
<>and by and by all of them became pregnant. Kaus4alya1
<>and Kaikeyi1 gave birth to a son each and Sumitra1
<>gave birth to two sons. The son of Kaus4alya1 was called
<>Ra1ma, the son of Kaikeyi1 was named Bharata and the [Page205-a+ 65]
<>sons of Sumitra1 were called Laks2man2a and S4atrughna.
<>(Kam3pa Ra1ma1yan2a, Ba1la Ka1n2d2a).
<NI>10) {%The boon of S4ani (Saturn).%} Astronomers are of
<>opinion that famine will occur in the world for the
<>period of twelve years, when the planet S4ani (Saturn)
<>comes into the orbit of Rohin2i1 (a star). But now the
<>planet Saturn does not come into the orbit of the star
<>Rohin2i1. There is a story in the Padma Pura1n2a, which
<>states that this happened so because of a boon S4ani had
<>given to Das4aratha.
<NI>During the regime of Das4aratha the planet S4ani approa-
<>ched the orbit of Rohin2i1. Experts in astronomy said that
<>the earth would be ruined by famine etc. if S4ani cleft
<>the star Rohin2i1. Hearing this Das4aratha took his bow
<>and arrows, got into his chariot, and started for the
<>firmament. He travelled for one lac and a quarter of
<>yojanas (leagues) and reached the hind part of Rohin2i1,
<>which is beyond the sun. Adorned with golden gar-
<>ments and a golden crown studded with jewels, seated
<>on a golden chariot decorated with precious stones, and a
<>lofty flagpost and yoked with horses of the colour of
<>swan, the king shone in the sky as a second sun. He drew
<>the bow-string right upto his ear and placed in it the
<>arrow of destruction. When the devas and the asuras
<>saw the arrow of destruction they began to tremble with
<>fear. S4ani came to Das4aratha and fell before him and
<>said that he would grant any boon to the King, and
<>requested him to recall the arrow of destruction. Das4a-
<>ratha told S4ani that he only wanted S4ani not to come to
<>the orbit of Rohin2i1. S4ani agreed. From that day onwards
<>S4ani has never entered the orbit of Rohin2i1. (Padma
<>Pura1n2a, Uttara Ka1nd2a, Chapter 34).
<NI>11) {%Death.%} Ra1ma and Laks2man2a with Si1ta1 went to
<>live in the forest. Sumantra who accompanied them
<>up to the river Ganges, returned to the palace. By
<>then Das4aratha had fallen down filled with grief. He
<>had never recovered from that state of unconsciousness.
<>At the time of his death Bharata and S4atrughna had
<>been away in the kingdom of Kekaya and Ra1ma and
<>Laks2man2a in the forest. Thus the curse he incurred
<>in his younger days from the aged hermit, the father of
<>S4ravan2a, was fully realized.
<NI>12) {%Das4aratha appears again.%} Ra1van2a was killed in the
<>battle. Si1ta1 was tested in fire and found sinless. Ra1ma
<>accepted her, seeing that her conjugal fidelity was not
<>marred. At this time S4iva appeared before Ra1ma in a
<>divine aeroplane. Das4aratha was seated in that plane
<>clad in pure garments. He took Ra1ma and Laks2man2a
<>into his lap and embraced them. He blessed them and
<>Si1ta1 who was standing with folded hands. Then Das4a-
<>ratha disappeared. (Va1lmi1ki Ra1ma1yan2a, Yuddha Ka1n2d2a,
<>Sarga 122).

<HI>DASYU. The ancient dwellers of North India. What
<>we see in R2gveda is mostly a history of the A1ryans from
<>the period of their exodus from the plateau Kumbha
<>till they reached the banks of the Yamuna1. The plateau
<>of Kum3bha is Kabul. The Dasyus were the first people
<>the Aryans had to confront with after passing the Indus.
<>R2gveda bears testimony to the fact that the civilization
<>of the Dasyus was far advanced than that of the A1ryans.
<>S4am3bara, King of the Dasyus, was the ruler of hundred
<>cities. All the cities were fortified with strong walls and
<>fortresses, which are described as ‘as4vamayi1’, ‘a1yasi1’,
<>‘s4atabhuji1’ etc. The greatest enemies of the A1ryans were
<>the ‘Pan2is’ of these cities. They were a particular class
<>of people of these cities. In the ‘Nirukta of Ya1ska’ it is
<>mentioned that pan2is were traders. Names of many of the
<>Kings of the Dasyus occur in the R2gveda. Dhuni,
<>Cumuri, Pipru, Varcas, S4am3bara and such others are the
<>most valiant and mighty among them. The most impor-
<>tant of the several tribes of the Dasyus were the S4imyus,
<>the Ki1kat2as, S4igrus and the Yaks2us. They are mentioned
<>as the Ana1sas in the R2gveda. (Ana1sas--without nose).
<>Perhaps their nose was flat; more over they are stated as
<>having dark complexion. So it may be assumed that the
<>Dasyus were Dravidians. They talked a primitive
<>language, and they despised sacrificial religion. They did
<>not worship Gods like Indra and others. They possibly
<>worshipped the Phallus, S4iva, Devi and the like.

<HI>DATTA1TREYA. (DATTA). A hermit famous in the
<>pura1n2as.
<NI>1) {%Birth.%} Anasu1ya1, the wife of hermit Atri gave birth
<>to Datta1treya. But Datta1treya was the incarnation of
<>Maha1vis2n2u.
<NI>There is a story in Brahma1n2d2a Pura1n2a how Maha1-
<>vis2n2u came to incarnate as Datta1treya.
<NI>Once there was a hermit called An2ima1n2d2avya (Ma1n2d2a-
<>vya). While the hermit was engaged in silent medi-
<>tation, some robbers passed by that way. The King's
<>men who were chasing the robbers, came to the hermit
<>and asked him about the robbers. The hermit did not
<>break the silence. The King's men, thinking the hermit
<>to be the thief bound his hands and legs and took him to
<>the palace. The King ordered Ma1n2d2avya to be killed by
<>piercing his body with a trident. Accordingly a trident
<>was posted on a hill far away and Ma1n2d2avya was seated [Page206-b+ 64]
<>on the tip of it. Ma1n2d2avya lay there in agony.
<NI>It was at this time that S4i1lavati1, famous for her conjugal
<>fidelity, went to the house of a harlot, carrying her
<>husband Ugras4ravas on her shoulder. When they passed
<>by that way Ugras4ravas scolded An2ima1n2d2avya; getting
<>angry at this An2ima1n2d2avya cursed Ugras4ravas that he
<>would get his head broken and die before the sun-rise.
<>Si1lavati1 became very sorry when she heard the curse
<>and she also cursed. “Let the sun not rise tomorrow”.
<>The sun did not rise next day. Everything in the world
<>was in chaos. The devas were flurried. They went to
<>Brahma1. Brahma1 took them to S4iva. They could not
<>find a solution. So all of them approached Maha1vis2n2u.
<>The Trimu1rtis (three gods) told the devas that the
<>problem would be solved, and the devas returned.
<>Brahma1, Vis2n2u and Mahes4vara went to S4i1lavati1. Before
<>seeing S4i1lavati1, they went to Anasu1ya1 the wife of Atri
<>and sought her help to persuade S4i1lavati1 to recall her
<>curse. Thus Anasu1ya1 and the Trimu1rtis approached
<>S4i1lavati1 and spoke compassionate and consolatory words.
<>At last S4i1lavati1 recalled the curse. The Trimu1rtis con-
<>vinced S4i1lavati1, that Ugras4ravas would not die. The
<>pleased Trimu1rtis asked Anasu1ya1 to ask for any boon. She
<>replied that she did not want any boon except that the
<>Trimu1rtis should take birth as her sons. Accordingly
<>Maha1vis2n2u took birth as Datta1treya, S4iva as Durva1sas
<>and Brahma1 as Candra, in the womb of Anasu1ya1. This
<>is how Datta1treya was born.
<F>1. Datta1treya is known by the name ‘Datta’ also in the Pura1n2as.</F> Datta1treya did penance
<>from his childhood and became a hermit. (Brahma1n2d2a
<>Pura1n2a, Chapters 39 to 44).
<NI>2) {%Ka1rtavi1rya1rjuna and Datta1treya.%} When Ka1rtavi1rya1r-
<>juna became King Datta1treya had become very famous.
<>Ka1rtavi1rya1rjuna wanted to obtain supernatural powers.
<>So he called the hermit Garga and asked for his advice.
<>Garga advised him that Datta1treya was the incarnation
<>of Vis2n2u and that he would grant his wishes. So Ka1rta-
<>vi1rya1rjuna and his wife came to the river Narmada1,
<>and taking bath in the river, began to worship Datta1-
<>treya, who was doing penance closeby. Datta1treya
<>was pleased and went to Ka1rtavi1rya1rjuna and asked him
<>what his desire was. He requested for thousand hands
<>and to be a youth for ever, and such other things.
<>Datta1treya granted his wishes. After that Ka1rtavi1rya1r-
<>juna would very often go to the hermit Datta1treya for
<>his advice. (Brahma Pura1n2a, Chapter 44).
<NI>3) {%Cursing Ra1van2a.%} Once Ra1van2a went to the hermit-
<>age of Datta1treya. The hermit had placed a waterpot
<>purified by reciting spells and incantations. Ra1van2a
<>stole that waterpot. When the hermit knew this, he
<>cursed Ra1van2a saying, “Since the water, which was
<>evoked by spells and incantations, has fallen on your
<>head, Monkeys will pollute your head by treading on
<>it.” (Va1lmi1ki Ra1ma1yan2a, Yuddha Ka1n2d2a).
<NI>4) {%Exhortation to the Sa1dhyas.%} Mention is made in Maha1-
<>bha1rata, Udyoga Parva, Chapter 36, Stanza 4, that the
<>hermit Datta1treya gave exhortations to the Sa1dhyas (the
<>sons of Dharmadeva by his wife Sa1dhya1).
<NI>5) A boon for the birth of Nahus2a. See Nahus2a.
<HI>DATTOLI. A son born to hermit Pulastya by his wife

<HI>DA1YAKRAMA. (INHERITANCE). Da1yakrama is [Page207-a+ 63]
<>the system by which the wealth of parents is received by
<>the heirs. The system of inheritance as described by Manu
<>is as follows:--
<NI>1) After the death of the parents, all the children should
<>divide their wealth among themselves. When either the
<>father or the mother is alive, the sons should not take
<>their wealth.
<NI>2) If the wealth is not divided, the eldest brother should
<>keep the whole of it in his custody and maintain his
<>brothers and sisters like a father.
<NI>3) Of the entire wealth, the eldest brother may take one-
<>twentieth part, the second brother, one-fortieth part
<>and the third brother, one-eghtieth part and the balance
<>is to be divided equally among all of them.
<NI>4) If there are more than three sons, the eldest and the
<>youngest may take one-twentieth and one-eightieth (of
<>the entire wealth) respectively, and each of the other
<>brothers may take one-fortieth. The rest of the pro-
<>perty must be divided equally among all of them.
<NI>5) After dividing like this, if there are unmarried sisters,
<>each of the brothers should give one-fourth of his share
<>for their marriage.
<NI>6) The ornaments which the mother had received by
<>way of her dowry etc. may be taken after her death by
<>her unmarried daughters.
<NI>7) If, after his marriage, one of the brothers dies, the
<>brother who keeps his movable and immovable proper-
<>ties, must beget children by the brother's wife and hand
<>over the above properties to her issue. (See under
<>Manusmr2ti).
<HI>D2AYA1NA1KS2ETRA. A famous temple of Goddess Diana
<>in Epheseus. In Greek mythology Diana (Daya1na1) is
<>the goddess of hunting. She is also identified with the
<>Moon Goddess. Being a virgin goddess, she came to be
<>worshipped by virgins who regarded her as their patron.
<>Daya1na1 (Diana) is one of the beautiful daughters of the
<>supreme God, Zeus. Her idol was worshipped in many
<>places in Greece. There was a famous temple of this god-
<>dess in the town of Epheseus. This temple was destroyed
<>in the 3rd Century B.C. by enemies.
<HI>DEVA(S). Gods or deities.
<NI>1) {%General information.%} From time immemorial belief in
<>Devas has existed in every country and all religions have
<>lauded their superior nature and power. It was the
<>Hindus and the Buddhists who first applied the term ‘Deva’
<>to certain very subtle beings recognised in all religions.
<>The Pa1rsi religion (Zaratus2t2ra) has spoken about seven
<>Devara1jans and their attendants. ‘Ames2 pentas’ is the
<>actual term used for the Devara1ja. The Christian reli-
<>gion refers to nine sects of Devas like Sra1fi, Kherubi,
<>Dominion, Angel etc. I1slam refers to four chief Devas
<>viz. Gabriel, the presiding Deva over revelations, Mik-
<>hayel of protection, Asriyal of death and Israfin of resur-
<>ruction. An important section of the R2gveda is Su1ktas
<>about Deva1s like Indra, Mitra, Varun2a, Agni etc. In
<>Yajn5as etc. Devas occupy a very important place. The
<>Kenopanis2ad states that the conceited and haughty Devas
<>were taught a lesson by God. The subject matter of the
<>Kat2hopanis2ad is the advice given by Yama to Naciketas.
<>The Mun2d2akopanis2ad says that the Devas, man and
<>birds originated from God.
<F>1. Tasma1cca deva1h2 bahudha1 samprasu1ta1h2 sa1dhya1h2 manus2ya1h2 pas4avo vaya1m3sah2.</F> There are references about
<>Deva worship in the Smr2tis. The Pura1n2as and Upapu- [Page207-b+ 62]
<>ra1n2as contain scientific discussions, about the origin,
<>work or functions, classes, etc. of Devas. The Mantra-
<>s4a1stra and Tantras4a1stra deal also about the various sects
<>and class of Devas, their nature, and the worship to be
<>offered to them etc.
<NI>2) {%Christian Devas, Satan and devils.%} Christian religious
<>books commonly use the term Ma1la1khas for Devas.
<>Greek mythology contains a story about the Ma1la1khas,
<>who did not obey the orders of Jehovah and how He
<>cursed them, turned them into devils and flung them into
<>hell. And, in hell they organised a revolutionary party
<>to fight Jehovah. Many leaders talked on the subject of
<>how to take vengeance on Jehovah. It was nearabout
<>this period that Jehovah created with the dust on earth
<>Adam as his beloved son and settled him in the garden of
<>Aden. God created from the ribs of Adam the woman
<>called Eve, and they lived in Eden quite happily.
<NI>Satan and other leaders in hell decided that the greatest
<>revenge that could be taken upon Jehovah was to create
<>troubles for his dear son, Adam in Eden. Accordingly
<>Satan went to Eden and made Adam and Eve eat the
<>forbidden fruit with the result that Jehovah cursed and
<>turned them into human beings and turned them out of
<>Eden. Milton in his reputed poem Paradise Lost has told
<>the above story in inimitable language.
<NI>3) {%Indian Devas.%} Most of the Indian Devas are the sons
<>of Kas4yapa Praja1pati by Aditi, daughter of Daks2a Praja1-
<>pati. The total number of Devas is 33 crores. The Devas
<>are divided into many classes or sections like A1dityas,
<>Vis4vadevatas, Vasus, Tus2itas, A1bha1svaras, Anilas, Maha1-
<>ra1jikas, Sa1dhyas, Rudras, Vidya1dharas, Pitr2devas etc.
<>There are further divisions like Apsaras, Viru1pa1ks2as,
<>Bhadras, Gandharvas, Kum3bha1n2d2as, Ra1ks2asas, Na1gas,
<>As4vini1s, Kinnaras, Kim3purus2as, Pis4a1cas, Guhyakas,
<>Siddhas, Ca1ran2as, Matr2s, Ku1s4ma1n2d2as, Bhu1tas, Veta1las,
<>etc. Yet another division is into Lokapa1las, Lipikas etc.
<>The Devas are classified with special reference to
<>bhu1tagan2as like Pr2thvi1 etc. and are, therefore, referred
<>to as Bhu1devata1s, Agnidevata1s, Va1yudevata1s, etc. The
<>presiding spirit of Bhu1devata1s is Kubera, that of the
<>Jaladevata1s is Varun2a, that of the Agnidevata1s, Va1yu-
<>bhagava1n and that of the A1ka1s4adevatas, Indra. And,
<>under them there are various sets of Devas in charge of
<>different departments. Sa1dhyas, Vasus, A1dityas, Apsaras
<>etc. are A1ka1s4adevas, Maruttas and Gandharvas,
<>Va1yudevas. Yaks2as and Yaks2i1s are servants of
<>Kubera. Viru1pa1ks2a, Bhadra etc. are Bhu1devas.
<>Viru1pa1ks2a supports the earth according to the Va1lmi1ki
<>Pura1n2a. Those who protect nidhis (treasures) may also
<>be considered Bhu1midevas.
<NI>There are 33 bosses or presiding spirits for the 33 crores
<>of Devas. Dva1das4a1dityas (12) Eka1das4arudras (11)
<>As2t2avasus (8) and As4vini1devas (2) are the 33 chiefs of
<>Devas. Indra is the chief of all of them.
<NI>4) {%The term Deva.%} The word Deva means light and
<>also play. Devas shine forth, they are also playful.
<>Thus the name is quite apt for them. (See Gra1ma-
<>devata1).

<HI>DEVADA1SA. A rich Vais4ya extolled much in the Katha1-

<NI>After roaming about for some time the helpless Devada1sa

<>perty for a small price.”
<NI>No sooner did Devada1sa hear the above than he
<>hurried back to his house and dug up the treasure
<>and thus became wealthy again. The paramour of his
<>wife went to him a few days later and purchased the
<>property at a heavy price. But, when he dug up the
<>place no treasure was found. Then he wanted to sell
<>back the property to Devada1sa himself, but he refused
<>to purchase it. The case was taken before the King when
<>Devada1sa detailed the whole story. And, as ordered by
<>the King, Devada1sa disfigured his wife and wedded
<>another wife. (Katha1saritsa1gara, La1va1n2akalam3bakam,
<>Taran3ga 5).
<HI>DEVADATTA I. A famous brahmin boy whose story is
<>described in the Katha1saritsa1gara.
<NI>Devadatta was the son of the Brahmin, Haridatta of
<>Kambukapura. Though as a boy Devadatta learnt all
<>the arts and sciences when he grew up to be a youth he
<>became a very wayward fellow. Dice-play became his
<>main job. One day in a game of dice he lost even his
<>clothes, and being afraid of his father, he left the place
<>without returning home.
<NI>Devadatta roamed about, and during one such trip
<>he saw a deserted temple, and a muni called Ja1lapa1da
<>engaged in reciting mantras. Devadatta prostrated be-
<>fore the muni and told him all the details about himself.
<>The muni accepted Devadatta as his s4is2ya and promised
<>to secure vidya1dharahood for him.
<NI>Next night Ja1lapa1da led Devadatta to a burning ghat, [Page208-b+ 64]
<>nearby the temple. After conducting Pu1ja1 in the shade
<>of a fig tree with offerings of Pa1yasa and Vais4va-
<>devabali the muni told Devadatta thus: “You should
<>come here daily and perform pu1ja1 like this, and pray
<>at the close of it, ‘Oh! Vidyutprabha1! please accept my
<>pu1ja1.’
<NI>Devadatta acted according to the above advice of the
<>muni, and one day, at the close of his pu1ja1 the tree broke
<>open into two and a beautiful female who appeared from
<>it took him with her saying that her mistress wanted him.
<>He was thus taken to a house studded with gems wherein
<>he saw a noble lady seated on a cot. She caught Deva-
<>datta by his hands and after seating him alongside said
<>to him: “I am Vidyutprabha1, daughter of the Yaks2a
<>king Ratnavars2a. I am a virgin girl. Muni Ja1lapa1da
<>has worshipped me so much that I have decided to
<>fulfil his desire. I am in love with you and you will
<>please wed me.”
<NI>Accordingly Devadatta married Vidyutprabha1 and lived
<>there with her. In due course she conceived, and Deva-
<>datta met Ja1lapa1da and told him about the whole
<>affair, and the muni, to achieve his personal object
<>asked Devadatta to cut open the abdomen of Vidyut-
<>prabha1 and take to him the child found therein. Deva-
<>datta did not at all relish the idea. Yet reluctant to
<>disobey his preceptor he returned to his wife. She permit-
<>ted him to carry out the directions of the muni, yet his
<>mind did not approve of the action. Then Vidyutprabha1
<>herself cut open her abdomen, took the child out of it
<>and placing it before her husband told him thus:--
<>“He who eats the child will become a Vidya1dhara.
<>Take it. I was a Vidya1dhari1 turned into a Yaks2i1 by a
<>curse, and to cut open my abdomen and take out the
<>child like this was the redemption promised me from the
<>curse, and now I go to the Vidya1dhara world; we shall
<>meet there.” And she disappeared.
<NI>In great sorrow Devadatta brought the child to Ja1la-
<>pa1da who, after sending him out on the pretext of his
<>(Ja1lapa1da's) performing Bhairavapu1ja, ate up the child.
<>Devadatta returned and when he knew of the action of
<>his preceptor he was overcome with rage. But the latter
<>rose up to the skies assuming the form of a Vidya1dhara.
<>Devadatta made up his mind to anyhow take revenge
<>upon Ja1lapa1da, and he also went to Vidya1dharaloka with
<>the help of a Veta1la whom he befriended by offering
<>human flesh. And, there he saw Ja1lapa1da drunk with
<>pride at his Vidya1dharahood sitting on a throne of
<>gems in a mansion. Meanwhile Vidyutprabha1 who was
<>now once more a Vidya1dhari1 had turned down the
<>advances made by the erstwhile Ja1lapa1da for her love.
<>But, at the sight of Devadatta her face flushed with love
<>for him while, in fear, the sword fell down from the
<>hands of Ja1lapa1da who now fainted. Devadatta pre-
<>vented the Veta1la from killing Ja1lapa1da and he (Veta1la)
<>at the instance of Devadatta took Ja1lapa1da to the burn-
<>ing ghat and cremated him there.
<NI>Ka1tya1yani1devi1 at this time appeared before Devadatta
<>and pleased with his courage appointed him as chief of
<>the Vidya1dharas. He wedded Vidyutprabha1 and lived
<>happily. (Katha1saritsa1gara, Caturda1rika1lam3bakam3,
<>Taran3ga 3).
<HI>DEVADATTA II. A king of ancient India, son of King
<>Jayadatta. Jayadatta wanted to marry his son Devadatta
<>to the daughter of a Vais4ya in Pa1t2ali1putra. Though it [Page209-a+ 65]
<>was a far cry from Pa1t2ali1putra to Jayadatta's court the
<>Vais4ya married his daughter to the prince as he attached
<>much importance to such an alliance with the King.
<>From the dowry given by the Vais4ya to his daughter
<>the resources of her father appeared to be very meagre
<>to Devadatta.
<NI>After some time Devadatta's wife returned to her house
<>with the permission of her husband. During her absence
<>Jayadatta expired, enemies attacked his kingdom and
<>Devadatta with his mother ran away from his kingdom.
<>Sometime after that he started for his wife's house. But,
<>feeling that it would not be proper for him to personally
<>tell his father-in-law about what had happened to him
<>he waited outside an inn near his wife's house, and he
<>had not waited there for long when he saw a woman
<>descending by a cord from the other door of the house.
<>He felt deeply mortified to detect that it was his wife
<>who was thus descending. And, when she, not recognis-
<>ing Devadatta in his dirty clothes, asked him who he
<>was, he answered a ‘traveller’. Taking no notice of this
<>‘traveller’ she got into the inn, and Devadatta followed
<>her. Inside the inn she was met by a man who beat her
<>for being late to go to him; but, in spite of the beating
<>she pleased him with coy words. Then and there Deva-
<>datta divorced her in his mind, but remained there
<>observing their love-making. In the course of their love-
<>play an ear-ring studded with costly gems rolled down
<>the floor from her ears, but she did not notice it. She
<>parted from her paramour before dawn. Devadatta
<>went to Ka1nyakubja with the ear-ring, and there he
<>pledged it for one lakh sovereigns with which money he
<>collected an army and conquered back his lost kingdom.
<>Then he redeemed the pledge and sent the ear-ring to
<>his father-in-law. The whole incident became public.
<>When she realised the fact that the stranger whom she
<>had met at the inn was her own husband, Devadatta's
<>wife felt so deeply hurt and humiliated that her heart was
<>broken and she died. (Katha1saritsa1gara, Narava1hana-
<>datta Janana, Taran3ga 1).
