<HI>DEVAPRATIS2T2HA1. The sacred rite of installation of
<>Devas (idols) in temples for which definite rules have
<>been laid down by great a1ca1ryas. According to the
<>rules Va1sudevamu1rti is to be installed in the centre of
<>the pan5ca1yatanas. (the S4ri1 kovil--the sanctum sancto-
<>rium and the four paths used for the procession of the
<>deity, around it). The mu1rtis Va1mana, Narasim3ha,
<>Hayagri1va and Vara1ha are to be installed in the zones [Page211-b+ 66]
<>(corners) dominated by Agni, Nirr2ti, Va1yu and I1s4a1na
<>respectively. Na1ra1yan2amu1rti should be installed at the
<>centre of the pra1sa1da. Ambika1 should be installed in
<>Agni's corner, A1ditya in Nirr2ti's corner, Brahma1 in Va1yu's
<>corner and S4ivalin3ga in the I1s4a1na corner. Lin3ga in the
<>form of Rudra should also be installed in the I1s4a1na
<>corner; or it may be installed in all the nine corners or
<>zones. In that case Va1sudevamu1rti should be installed at
<>the centre. All the Devas bearing the name Ra1ma
<>must be installed to the east of the Pra1sa1da. Lokapa1la-
<>kas like Indra should be installed at the eight places
<>starting with the east. In Pan5ca1yatana pratis2t2ha1 Puru-
<>s2ottama should be installed at the centre. Maha1laks2mi1
<>and Vais4ravan2a are to be installed to the east of the
<>Pra1sa1da, and the Ma1tr2s in the south. Subrahman2ya,
<>Gan2apati, I1s4a1na and the planets (grahas2) like Sun
<>should be installed in the west. Das4a1vata1ramu1rtis (ten
<>incarnations of Vis2n2u) should be installed in the North.
<>Can2d2ika1 should be installed in Agni's zone, Sarasvati1
<>in Va1yu's zone, Ambi1ka1 in Nirr2ti's zone and S4ri1 Bhaga-
<>vati1 in I1s4a1na zone. In temples with thirteen A1layas
<>Vis2n2u should be installed at the centre. Mu1rtis like
<>Kes4ava should be in the east.
<NI>Devapratima1s (idols) are of seven varieties like those
<>made of earth, of wood, of metals, of gems, of sandal
<>wood, of stone and of flowers. Of the above, idols made
<>of earth, sandalwood and of flowers are meant only for
<>temporary use, but they also grant all the desires of the
<>devotees.
<NI>Stone for idols should be got from mountains. Pa1n2d2ura
<>(white), Arun2a (red), Pi1ta (yellow) and Kr2s2n2a (black)-
<>rocks having any one of these four colours may be
<>selected. If no such stone is available any other stone
<>may be used, but by doing homa and reciting Narasim3ha
<>mantra the attributes of any of the specified varn2as
<>should be transferred to it. It is best to have white
<>markings on the stone selected for the idol. There are
<>the three genders, masculine, feminine and neuter with
<>reference to stones. Stone which produces the sound of
<>bell metal on being hit and gives out sparks of fire on
<>being cut is of the masculine gender. Stone which
<>possesses these attributes to a lesser degree is of the
<>feminine gender. Stone with no shape is of neuter gen-
<>der. Beside these three varieties there are the sagarbha
<>(pregnant) stones also, viz. stones with round markings
<>on them, and they should not be used for making idols.
<>Once it is decided to make an idol, Vanayajn5a should
<>be performed first. To worship the forest from which
<>the stone for the idol was to be obtained is vanayajn5a.
<>A specified place in the forest should be dug up, the
<>pit treated with cow-dung and a small platform raised
<>and Vis2n2u worshipped thereon. After sacrificial offerings
<>etc. instruments for the construction of the idol like
<>t2an3ka (chisel) should be worshipped. To sprinkle on
<>the stone s4a1litoya (water in which paddy grains are
<>put) purified with the chanting of astramantra is the
<>next step. After that bhu1tabali (offerings to the spirits
<>of the stone) should be performed upon which the
<>bhu1tas residing inside the stone will quit. Then the
<>a1ca1rya should go to sleep repeating the svapna mantra.
<F>1. {%Svapnamantra:%}
<Poem>Om3 namah2 sakalaloka1ya Vis2n2ave prabhavis2n2ave /
<>Vis4va1ya vis4varu1pa1ya svapna1dhipataye namah2 //
<>A1caks2va devadeves4a prasuptosmi tava1ntikam /
<>Svapne sarva1ni ka1rya1n2i hr2dista1ni tu ya1ni me //
<>Om Om hum phat2 vis2n2ave sva1ha1.</Poem></F> [Page212-a+ 65]
<>If he dreams good dreams everything is quite right;
<>if bad dreams are dreamt, the next morning narasim3ha-
<>homa should be performed, the stone given offerings
<>with chanting of astramantra. The edge of the instru-
<>ments used for sculpturing the idol should be firstly
<>smeared with ghee, honey etc. The architect should be
<>one who has gained mastery over the senses. He should
<>first cut the stone into a square, and then it should be
<>placed on a chariot, covered with cloth and taken to
<>his house. Then after performing a pu1ja1 the work on
<>the idol should be started. (Agni Pura1n2a, Chapter 43).

<HI>DEVAS4ARMA1 II. A Brahmin, an erudite scholar in the
<>Vedas. His story was once told to Pa1rvati1 by S4iva as
<>follows:--
<NI>Devas4arman, the very erudite brahmin scholar in the
<>Vedas came to be much respected by the people of the
<>four varn2as (Castes) because of his constant and unfail-
<>ing performance of the various religious duties and
<>rites like the Agnihotra etc. He had sons, relations and
<>cows in abundance. But, he did not observe the s4ukla-
<>pan5cami1 in Pros2t2hapada (Bha1drapada) which is one of [Page212-b+ 65]
<>the dva1das4aca1ndra ma1sas (the 12 solar months).
<>That day was his father's death anniversary (S4ra1ddha
<>day) for which he would invite Brahmins on the pre-
<>vious evening. And, in the morning he would get cook-
<>ed rice by his wife in 18 different ways for the gratifica-
<>tion of the departed soul. Then will he feed the
<>Bra1hmins. On one such day he duly treated a Bra1hmin
<>versed in the Vedas. In the evening when he
<>was washing his feet with water brought by his wife
<>he heard the following conversation between a dog
<>and an ox.
<NI>{%Dog :%}--Please attend to my words about what my
<>daughter-in-law did. One day, as fate would have it,
<>I went to my son's house, where a snake was drinking
<>milk, and I drank the whole of the milk which was left
<>over by it. My daughter-in-law saw me drinking it and
<>I was absolutely crest-fallen. I don't feel any yearning
<>even for food.
<NI>{%Ox :%}--Now dog, please attend to my sorrow and grief.
<>Today my son feasted the Brahmins, but he did not even
<>think of me. Nobody gave me even a blade of grass or
<>a drop of water. I have not tasted anything today.
<>Moreover I am a captive also. All this must be the result
<>of some sin committed by me in my past life; no doubt
<>about it.”
<NI>The very learned and intelligent brahmin inferred from
<>the above talk that the dog and ox were his mother and
<>his father respectively. He thought to himself: they have
<>been born as animals in my house. What remedy shall
<>I find out therefor?
<NI>On account of such painful thoughts the Brahmin could
<>not sleep in the night. Early in the morning he went
<>to sage Vasis2t2ha and unburdened his heart to him and
<>requested him to find a solution to the low and mean
<>birth which had become his parents’ fate. After medita-
<>ting for some time the great sage told the brahmin as
<>follows:--
<NI>“Look here, the ox was, in its previous birth, a great
<>brahmin in Kun2d2inanagara. He did not observe the
<>S4ukla-pan5cami1 in the month of Pros2t2hapada, the day
<>being the death anniversary of his father. His wife had
<>menstruation on the same day, but she herself feasted
<>the Brahmins. A woman is impure during the four days
<>of menstruation. Because of having feasted the Brah-
<>mins on the first day of her monthly period the Brah-
<>min's wife was born as a dog. As for the husband, he
<>was born an ox as he not only did not observe the
<>S4ukla-pan5cami1 day, but also shared the sin of his wife.
<>Further questioned by the brahmin as to what he should
<>do for the salvation of his parents, Vasis2t2ha advised him
<>to observe the R2s2i pan5cami1 in the month of Pros2t2hapada
<>(Bha1drapada). He did so and his parents blessed him
<>and attained salvation. (Padma Pura1n2a, Chapter 78).
<HI>DEVAS4ARMA1 III. (See Mitrabheda).
<HI>DEVAS4ARMA1 IV. The hero of a story told by Vis2n2u-
<>s4arman.
<NI>Vis2n2us4arman, the intelligent preceptor, told five stories
<>based on five tactics to educate the five dull sons of
<>emperor Sudars4ana. The fifth tactics is called asam3prek-
<>s2yaka1ritva. He told two important stories to illustrate
<>the dangers, which would happen to those who act in
<>haste and in anger, without patiently attempting to
<>find out the truth. Devas4arman is the hero of one of
<>the stories.
<NI>{%Story one.%} Once upon a time there lived in Gaud2ades4a [Page213-a+ 65]
<>a brahmin called Devas4arman with his wife Yajn5asena1.
<>When his wife got pregnant Devas4arman told her that a
<>good and lucky son would be born to them to which she
<>replied as follows:--“On no account build castles in
<>the air and brood over things. I shall tell you a story
<>about a danger which happened to one who built
<>castles in the air. A brahmaca1ri1 was carrying home in
<>a pot on his head some rice flour. On the way he
<>thought thus: “I will purchase a she-goat with the
<>money I get out of the sale of this rice powder, and
<>it will deliver two kids every year. I will sell all the
<>kids and with the sale proceeds purchase a cow and
<>when it multiplies I will have the wherewithals for cul-
<>tivation and then I will do cultivation in a good field
<>and produce much paddy. And when I have thus money
<>in hand I will renovate my house and marry. We will
<>then have a good son whom I will name Somas4arman,
<>and if my wife, without caring for the boy, goes to milk
<>the cow I will go to the cow shed and give her a good
<>beating. “When his imagination reached this point the
<>pot on his head received, without his knowing, a blow
<>with the stick in his hand and it fell down on the ground
<>and was broken to pieces.”
<NI>Five or six days after telling the above story to her
<>husband Yajn5asena1 gave birth to a child. One day she
<>went to the river to take her bath leaving the child
<>to the care of her husband. Soon after a messenger came
<>from the palace to invite Devas4arman for food, the day
<>being Ama1vasi1 when Brahmins were fed well and also
<>given daks2in2a1, (presents of money). He could not wait
<>till his wife arrived; the child became a problem to
<>him.
<NI>The brahmin had a beloved Mongoose. After entrusting
<>the child to the care of it the Brahmin went to the
<>palace. Within a short time a serpent was seen crawling
<>towards the child and the Mongoose attacked it and
<>bit it into pieces. Naturally the Mongoose got itself
<>smeared all over with the blood of the serpent, and the
<>brahmin on returning home finding the Mongoose bath-
<>ed in blood thought it might have eaten the child
<>and in a rage he killed the poor Mongoose. But, when
<>he entered the room and found pieces of the killed ser-
<>pent strayed all over there truth and light dawned on
<>him. Yajn5asena1, who had returned after bath by now
<>also found fault with him.
<NI>{%The second story.%} Once there was a Brahmin in very indi-
<>gent circumstances. He was an orphan from his very
<>childhood. One day while he was asleep quite weary
<>and tired as he had no food that day some one
<>appeared and told him in a dream that three sannya1sins
<>would come to his house that noon and that if beaten to
<>death they would turn into three pot-fulls of treasure
<>with which he could live comfortably well.
<NI>His dream came true, and he locked up the treasure in
<>his room after sending away a barber who had witnessed
<>the incident with a piece of gold. The barber returned
<>home cherishing in his mind the false belief that sann-
<>ya1sins beaten to death would turn into pot-fulls of
<>treasure. And, one day some sannya1sins came to his
<>house and he began beating them and they ran out
<>crying aloud. Their cries attracted the attention of the
<>servants of the King. The barber's limbs were cut off
<>and he was killed on S4u1la (a three-pronged weapon).

<HI>DEVAS4I1LA1. See Gaya1ti1rtha.
<HI>DEVASMITA1. A Gem of a woman who was much devoted
<>to her husband. The story of Devasmita1 has been so
<>often quoted in ancient Indian literature. She was the
<>daughter of a Vais4ya called Dharmagupta and was
<>married to Guhasena, son of a Vais4ya called Dhana-
<>datta, who belonged to Ta1mraliptinagara.
<NI>After the death of his father Guhasena decided to go to
<>Kat2a1hadvi1pa for trade, and Devasmita1, a very suspici-
<>ous wife, objected to it. When his other relations began
<>pressing him to go to the dvi1pa for trade and his wife
<>not to go, Guhasena could not take a decision on the [Page214-a+ 65]
<>matter. He began a penance so that God might take
<>the decision in the matter. Devasmita1 also followed
<>him. Then God appeared to them in their dreams
<>and gave each of them a red lotus flower saying, “both
<>of you keep one flower each with you, and while both
<>of you are away from each other if either of you go
<>astray the lotus flower in the hands of the other
<>person will fade.” And, on awaking both of them had
<>a flower in their hands. Guhasena with his flower in
<>his hands started for Kat2a1hadvi1pa, and Devasmita1 sta-
<>yed at home with her eyes fixed on her lotus flower.
<>Guhasena reached the dvi1pa and traded in gems. Every-
<>body was surprised to find in his hands a lotus flower
<>which never faded. Four Vais4ya youngsters wanted to
<>understand the secret of it and one day they invited
<>Guhasena to their house and treated him to liquor very
<>liberally. When he got absolutely drunk they question-
<>ed him about the secret of the lotus flower, and he,
<>though in indistinct words, explained it to them. When
<>the secret was thus out all the four youngsters decided
<>to seduce his wife. They extracted from him the infor-
<>mation that he was not returning in the near future,
<>and they then came to Ta1mralipti.
<NI>At that time there lived in Ta1mralipti a bogus Sannya1-
<>sini1 (a female anchorite) called Yogakaran2d2ika1. The four
<>youths from Kat2a1hadvi1pa visited her, and she under-
<>took to fulfil their desire. She asked them to halt at her
<>house and then went to Devasmita1 with some refreshments.
<>Devasmita1's bitch barked very violently at the bogus an-
<>chorite when she told the former thus:--“Child! don't
<>you see the bitch barking at my very sight. The bitch is
<>crying thinking of her previous birth at my sight. Her-
<>self (the bitch) and I were, in our last birth, the wives
<>of a Brahmin. Since our husband was a courtier at the
<>palace often he was away from home and in his absence
<>I used to satisfy the cravings of sex by associating freely
<>with other males. I had even at that time realised the
<>truth that there was no duty higher than satisfying the
<>senses, and so I am now reborn with memories of the
<>past life. As for this bitch in her past life as co-wife with
<>me of the Brahmin, she sinned against her sense-organs
<>by upholding, in ignorance, her chastity, as a punish-
<>ment for which she was born as dog in this life. Yet, she
<>remembers her past.
<NI>Though Devasmita1 saw through the false and trea-
<>cherous heart of the bogus anchorite, without expres-
<>sing it she talked in all respect with her. Believing that
<>she had succeeded in her mission she told Devasmita1
<>about the four youths who had come from Kat2a1ha-
<>dvi1pa, and Devasmita1 welcomed the idea very gladly.
<NI>Devasmita1 got some liquor and mixed poison in it.
<>When it was night one of the Vais4ya youths came, and
<>after rendering him unconscious by administering the
<>poisoned liquor to him Devasmita1 stripped him of all
<>his clothes and ornaments, got him scratched on the
<>brows by the bitch and threw him into a cow dung
<>heap. Before it was dawn next day he returned to the
<>anchorite's house, took a bath and dressed himself up in
<>decent clothings. His other three companions also visi-
<>ted Devasmita1 one after the other in the next three
<>days, and they too underwent the same experience as the
<>first one. Lastly the bogus sannya1sini1 also was got down
<>and Devasmita1 cut her limbs and sent her away. And,
<>after this Devasmita1 put on the garb of a merchant and
<>went to Kat2a1hadvi1pa and identified her husband from [Page214-b+ 65]
<>among a thousand merchants. Guhasena too felt his
<>own doubts about her identity. But, without revealing
<>her identity she saw the king and told him that she had
<>certain matters to submit before the praja1paris2at (popu-
<>lar assembly). The King accordingly convened a session

<NI>2) {%Curse by Kaca.%} Kaca, son of Br2haspati became
<>S4ukra1ca1rya's disciple to learn Mr2tasan5ji1vani1 vidya1
<>(the science to ward off death for ever). Devaya1ni1

<NI>3) {%Devaya1ni1 in a neglected well.%} Once Vr2s2aparvan was the
<>king of the asuras, and S4ukra1ca1rya lived in his palace.
<>S4armis2t2ha1, the daughter of the king and Devaya1ni1, the
<>daughter of S4ukra1ca1rya became thick friends. While
<>one day these two friends with their companions were
<>engaged in water sports Indra came that way. Very
<>much interested in seeing the deva damsels thus engaged
<>in water sports Indra assumed the form of air and blew
<>off the dresses of the damsels from the shore while they
<>were playing in the river. The damsels hurriedly got
<>out of water, ran after the fleeing garments and put
<>on whatever came to their hands. In the confusion what
<>S4armis2t2ha1 got and wore was Devaya1ni1's clothes. Devaya1ni1
<>ran after S4armis2t2ha1 and caught hold of the clothes, but
<>the latter did not yield them to her. Both of them
<>indulged in hot words against each other, S4armis2t2ha1
<>calling S4ukra1ca1rya a beggar living in her father's
<>palace for his food. Not only that, she pushed Devaya1ni1
<>into a neglected well and went her way.
<NI>At that time prince Yaya1ti, son of Nahus2a, was hunting
<>in that forest. Feeling very thirsty he came to the well
<>for a drink and therein saw Devaya1ni1 in tears. He
<>brought her out from the well and leaving her there
<>went on his way.
<NI>S4ukra1ca1rya and his wife actually got anxious and upset
<>that their daughter had not returned even after the
<>lapse of so much time and deputed their servant
<>Gurn2ika1 to seek for Devaya1ni1. She found her out in
<>the forest. But, Devaya1ni1 refused to return home as
<>she did not want to continue as the daughter of one
<>who lived on the charity of S4armis2t2ha1 and her father.
<>Gurn2ika1 carried back this news to S4ukra1ca1rya, who then
<>went himself to the forest and met his daughter. He
<>felt deeply wounded to hear about the insults heaped
<>on his daughter by S4armis2t2ha1, and sent word to
<>Vr2s2aparvan that they were not returning to the palace.
<>Vr2s2aparvan realised that if S4ukra1ca1rya quarrelled
<>with him the whole asura world also would become
<>divided. He, therefore, tried his best to pacify the
<>a1ca1rya and his daughter. At last Devaya1ni1 agreed to re-
<>turn to the palace in case S4armis2t2ha1 went to her with one
<>thousand of her maids to serve as her (Devaya1ni1's) maids.
<>Vr2s2aparvan acted accordingly and Devaya1ni1 was
<>satisfied and returned to the palace with her father.
<>(A1di Parva, Chapter 78 onwards.)
<NI>4) {%Wedding of Devaya1ni1.%} Some time afterwards when
<>Devaya1ni1 was sporting in the forest with S4armis2t2ha1 and
<>others, King Yaya1ti happened to pass that way. They
<>recognised each other and Devaya1ni1 requested Yaya1ti
<>to marry her as she had mentally chosen him as her
<>husband on the very same day on which he had saved
<>her from the well.
<NI>Yaya1ti realised only too well that it was really danger-
<>ous to marry the daughter of S4ukra1ca1rya in the forest
<>secretly and he, therefore, rejected Devaya1ni1's request.
<>But, she got down her father there and with his per-
<>mission Yaya1ti married her. He also got much wealth
<>by way of dowry. S4armis2t2ha1 and her maids accompanied
<>Devaya1ni1 but Sukra1ca1rya warned Yaya1ti that on no
<>account should he touch S4armis2t2ha1.
<NI>With his wife and her attendants Yaya1ti returned to
<>his palace; but he put S4armis2t2ha1 and her maids in [Page216-a+ 70]
<>a place newly built for the purpose. Devaya1ni1 delivered
<>a child in due course and it was named Yadu. S4armis2-
<>t2ha1 one day met Yaya1ti in the forest and requested
<>for a child by him. Though at first he was reluctant to
<>oblige remembering only too well the injunction of
<>S4ukra1ca1rya, he at last succumbed to the eloquence of
<>S4armis2t2ha1. She got pregnant by him and the child
<>she delivered was called Druhyu.
<NI>Devaya1ni1 was alarmed at the birth of a child to
<>S4armis2t2ha, who was unmarried, and questioned her
<>about it. S4armis2t2ha1's answer that a brahmin stranger,
<>who came that way was the father of her child satisfied
<>Devaya1ni1.
<NI>Ere long another son, Turvasu, was born to Devaya1ni1,
<>and S4armis2t2ha1 too delivered two children called
<>Anudruhyu and Pu1ru. Thus the mistress (Devaya1ni1)
<>had two sons and her servant, S4armis2t2ha1 three sons.
<NI>While Yaya1ti and Devaya1ni1 were one day strolling in
<>the garden the sons of S4armis2t2ha1 came there. She
<>noticed the close resemblance of those boys to her
<>husband (Yaya1ti) and it kindled her wrath. Though
<>Yaya1ti did not, because of the presence of Devaya1ni1,
<>notice the children, when she asked them who their
<>father was, they answered “Yaya1ti”, whereupon in
<>great rage she ran up to her father. Yaya1ti too followed
<>her. Devaya1ni1 told her father about the infidelity of
<>Yaya1ti and he (S4ukra) cursed that Yaya1ti be afflicted
<>with old age. When Yaya1ti asked for redumption from
<>the curse S4ukra1ca1rya told him that he could exchange
<>his old age for the youth of somebody else.
<NI>Yaya1ti then called together all his five sons and asked
<>them whether anyone of them was prepared to exchange
<>his (son's) youth for his (Yaya1ti's) old age for one
<>thousand years. The three elder sons refused to agree
<>to the proposal, but the fourth son Pu1ru gladly exchan-
<>ged his youth with his father's old age. After spending
<>his life in youthful enjoyment for one thousand
<>years Yaya1ti returned his youth to Pu1ru, and Pu1ru
<>assumed Kingship of the country. (A1di Parva, Chapters
<>81-83).
<NI>5) {%Synonyms for Devaya1ni1.%} Aus4anasi1, S4ukratanaya1 and
<>Bha1rgavi1.
<HI>DEVI1. (Maha1ma1ya1). Though Devi1 is without a beginn-
<>ing it is told how she became first visible to living beings.
<NI>1) {%Beginning:%} While Maha1vis2n2u was resting on a fig
<>leaf in the assumed form of a child he began thinking
<>as to who he was, who created him and how he should
<>act. And then a celestial voice announced as follows:
<NI>Sarvam3 khalvidameva1ham
<NI>Na1nyadasti sana1tanam.
<NI>(All that is, I am. There is nothing eternal but me.).
<>This declaration astonished Vis2n2u, but he could not
<>understand who made it. While he was meditating
<>upon it, Devi1 appeared to him in the following manner.
<>Devi1
<F>1) <Poem>Ratir bhu1tistatha1 buddhirmati ki1rtih2 smrtirdhr2tih2 /
<>Sraddha1, medha1, svadha1, sva1ha1 Ks2udha1, nidra1, daya1, gatih2 /
<>Tus2t2ih2 pus2t2ih2 ks2ama1 lajja1 jr2mbha1 tandra1 ca s4aktayah2 /
<>Sam3sthita1h2 sarvatah2 pa1rs4ve Maha1devya1h2 pr2thak pr2thak. //</Poem>
<>(Devi1 Bha1gavata, Prathama, Skandha).</F> held in her four sacred hands weapons like the
<>conch, the disc, the club and the lotus flower and was
<>clad in divine clothes and wore divine ornaments and
<>was attended upon by powers
<F>1) <Poem>Ratir bhu1tistatha1 buddhirmati ki1rtih2 smrtirdhr2tih2 /
<>Sraddha1, medha1, svadha1, sva1ha1 Ks2udha1, nidra1, daya1, gatih2 /
<>Tus2t2ih2 pus2t2ih2 ks2ama1 lajja1 jr2mbha1 tandra1 ca s4aktayah2 /
<>Sam3sthita1h2 sarvatah2 pa1rs4ve Maha1devya1h2 pr2thak pr2thak. //</Poem>
<>(Devi1 Bha1gavata, Prathama, Skandha).</F> like Rati (the erotic),
<>Bhu1ti (riches and prosperity), Buddhi (intelligence),
<>Mati (intellect), Ki1rti (reputation), Dhr2ti (understanding [Page216-b+ 61]
<>capacity), Smr2ti (ability to remember), S4raddha1 (atten-
<>tion, concentration), Medha1 (intellect), Svadha1, Sva1ha1,
<>Ks2udha1 (thirst), Nidra1 (sleep), Daya1 (compassion), Gati,
<>(momentum), Tus2t2i (pleasure, happiness) Pus2t2i (growth,
<>progress), Ks2ama1 (patience, forberance), Lajja1 (sense
<>of shame), Jr2mbha1 and Tandra1 (laziness).
<NI>And, the Devi1 spoke to Maha1vis2n2u as follows:--After
<>all, what is there to be wondered at. Whenever the
<>world faced the phenomenon of Sr2s2t2isthitilaya (crea-
<>tion, existence and absorption into the primordial force)
<>you too have come up due to the power of Maha1s4akti.
<>As for Para1s4akti (the ultimate, universal force) it is
<>above and beyond all attributes. We, all of us, possess
<>attributes. From the na1bhi (navel) of yourself in whom
<>sattvagun2a (gentle attributes) predominates will be
<>born Brahma1 in whom rajogun2a (active attributes)
<>will predominate, and from the centre of his brows
<>will be born Rudra with tamogun2a (black, destructive
<>attributes). Brahma1, by the force of his tapas will earn
<>powers of creation and will with his rajogun2a create
<>the world blood-red in colour, and you will be the
<>sustainer and guardian of that world. The very same
<>world will be annihilated by Rudra at the end of the
<>Kalpa era. You please do know that I am the sa1ttvic
<>force which depend on you for the purpose of creation.
<>All creations of worlds have been made according to
<>the suggestions of Devi1.
<NI>2) {%Origin of Devi1.%} Once King Janamejaya questioned
<>Vya1sa about the birth of Devi1 to which he replied as
<>follows:--
<NI>Even Brahma1vis2n2umahes4varas (Brahma1, Vis2n2u and
<>S4iva--the Hindu trinity) are not capable of thinking
<>about the origin of Devi1. Then, what about me? Yet
<>let me make an attempt. All the forces--everything--
<>which appear in various names and forms are in fact
<>Devi1 herself, her different manifestations. Just as a
<>master actor appears on the stage in different roles for
<>the delectation of the audience the formless Devi1, on
<>behalf of the Devas, assumes many forms and imposes
<>upon herself attributes though in fact she is without any
<>attribute whatsoever. Thus, depending on her various
<>forms, actions etc. she is addressed by different names.
<>(Devi1 Bha1gavata, Pan5cama Skandha).
<NI>3) {%Five forms of Devi1.%} When primordial nature (Mu1la-
<>prakr2ti) incarnated or manifested in the form of Vis2n2u
<>the desire for creation raised its head in him. And, at
<>once the Devi1--female--aspects in Vis2n2u manifested
<>in five forms like Durga1, Laks2mi1, Sarasvati1, Sa1vitri1 and
<>Ra1dha1. These five forms are designated as the
<>Pan5cadevi1s. Brief notes on the five Devi1s are given
<>below.
<NI>(1) {%Durga1devi1.%} Mother of Gan2es4a bhagava1n. (God with
<>the elephant's head). She is the consort of S4iva.
<>Na1ra1yan2i1, the Vis2n2uma1ya1, is none else than Durga1.
<>She is also perfect Brahma, the one and the only one
<>with no second to it. She is worshipped by Devas like
<>Brahma1 and by sages and mahars2is. She is the basis,
<>the root and root cause of everything. She is eternal,
<>upholder of dharma, truth itself and the giver and
<>promoter of happiness, reputation, welfare, salvation
<>etc. as also of sorrow, grief, pain etc. She takes great [Page217-a+ 64]
<>preasure in removing the worries and sorrows of devo-
<>tees who seek refuge in her. She is all-power, achieve-
<>ments and assets personified. All imaginable permuta-
<>tions and combinations of every attribute comprise in
<>her and she it is who activates the universal soul.
<NI>2) {%Laks2mi1.%} This Devi1 is the most sublime form of the
<>universal soul, nay, the universal soul itself. She is all
<>wealth and riches personified, and she is the very seat of
<>beauty, compassion, welfare, peace, all goodness etc.
<>Evil traits like anger, avarice, haughtiness etc. are miles
<>away from her. She is all sympathy and kindness
<>towards her devotees. In Vaikun2t2ha, as Maha1laks2mi1 she
<>is ever engaged in the service of her husband, Vis2n2u.
<>In heaven she is the very glory of it. In houses she
<>appears as the welfare and prosperity of the inmates.
<>She is in fact beauty itself and is the essence of beauty
<>everywhere. She is all-merciful, and worshipped by
<>all.
<NI>(3) {%Sarasvati1.%} She is very base and fountain head of and
<>the presiding deity of the word, intellect, knowledge,
<>the arts etc. She also is of the form of the universal
<>soul and liberally grants intellect, poetical skill, reason
<>and logic, understanding capacity etc. to her devotees.
<>The finest music with all its beauties originates from
<>her. Yes, she is the goddess of all learning, fine arts and
<>every branch of knowledge. Like the ice piece, the
<>Jasmine flower etc. she is beautiful in her white cover.
<>She recited the holy names of S4ri1 Kr2s2n2a. She is of the
<>form of tapas and blesses those who perform it. In fact,
<>she it is who grants all gifts and powers of understand-
<>ing. She is the goddess of language and learning (Va1n2i1-
<>ma1ta1) and without her human beings would lose their
<>capacity to speak.
<NI>(4) {%Sa1vitri1devi1.%} She is mother of the four varn2as (castes,
<>classes) Veda1n3gas, the ta1ntric science etc. She is of
<>the form of Japa (chanting of holy names and mantras),
<>tapas, the effulgence of Brahman, the very essence of
<>truth and existence and supreme bliss. She is eternal
<>and grants salvation. She forms the very basis and pith
<>and core of the glowing presence of the universal soul.
<>This world is pure, and has been purified by the touch
<>of the sacred feet of this Devi1.
<NI>(5) {%Ra1dha1devi1.%} Ra1dhika1devi1, fifth of the pan5caprakr2tis is
<>the presiding deity over the five pra1n2as and She is of
<>the form of those pra1n2as. She is exceptionally beautiful
<>endowed with all fortunes and happiness, sublime and
<>serious, and the consort of S4ri1 Kr2s2n2a and as eternal
<>and equally endowed with divine attributes as the lord.
<>She is formless also, above and beyond attributes,
<>unattached and detached, and not visible even to the
<>great Devas and munis by the naked eye. Her clothes
<>even Agni would not burn. She was born, in Va1ra1ha
<>Kalpa as the daughter of Vr2s2abha1nu, and because her
<>sacred feet trod over this land, Bha1rata (India) became
<>holy. Once Brahma1 did tapas for 60,000 years to have
<>a glimpse of the Devi1's lotus feet, but could not. People
<>were able to see her only after she, in unbounded com-
<>passion for the world, incarnated herself in Vr2nda1vana
<>(See Prakr2ti).
<NI>4) {%Am3s4aru1pa(s)--partial manifestations of Devi1.%} The
<>above discussion was concerned with the five forms of the
<>Devi1 in her full and complete forms. Now, there are
<>six other Devi1s, who are partial manifestations or born
<>from certain parts of the Devi1. [Page217-b+ 64]
<NI>(1) {%Gan3ga1devi1.%} This Devi1 who flows in the form of
<>water and washes off men's sins takes her origin from
<>the body of Maha1vis2n2u. (There is another story which
<>traces Gan3ga1's origin to the An2d2a (egg or seed) of
<>Brahma1). (See Gan3ga1).
<NI>(2) {%Tulasi1.%} She is the lover and also the servant at
<>the feet of Vis2n2u. She also washes off men's sins and
<>promotes their welfare. (See Tulasi1).
<NI>(3) {%Manasa1devi1%} Manasa1devi1, who was born as a dau-
<>ghter of Kas4yapa is another partial incarnation of
<>Maha1ma1ya1. She is a great tapasvini1, a favourite dis-
<>ciple of S4an3kara, unique in learning and erudition,
<>sister of Ananta (the king of Serpents), the presiding
<>deity of Mantras, wife of Jaratka1ru muni and mother
<>of A1sti1ka muni. (See Manasa1devi1).
<NI>(4) {%Devasena1devi1.%} Since this Devi1 has been born out of a
<>sixth part of Maha1ma1ya1 she is called S2as2t2hi1devi1 also. It
<>is this Devi1, who gives children to living beings and also
<>protects them. For one year from the birth of a child
<>this Devi1 should be worshipped. Those who cannot
<>afford it should worship Devi1 on the 6th or 21st day
<>after a child is born. (See Devasena1).
<NI>(5) {%Man3galacan2d2ika1.%} She is born from the face of
<>Mu1laprakr2ti. Anybody who propitiates and pleases
<>her will be the recipient of all good things like sons and
<>grandsons, wealth, reputation, welfare etc. (See Man3-
<>galacan2d2ika1).
<NI>(6) {%Bhu1midevi1.%} She is the basis for all things, soil for
<>the origin of the vegetable kingdom, the treasure
<>house of all gems and the very incarnation of compas-
<>sion and sympathy. (See Bhu1midevi1).
<NI>5) {%Am3s4akala1devi1(s).%} Another class of devi1s who are
<>also partial manifestations of the Supreme Devi1. They
<>are born out of the parts of Maha1devi1. The following
<>are the Am3s4akala1devi1s.
<NI>(1) {%Sva1ha1devi1.%} Consort of Agni Bhagava1n (Fire God).
<>Sva1ha1 is worshipped in all the worlds. If havis (obla-
<>tion) is offered without repeating her name the Devas
<>will not accept it.
<NI>(2) {%Daks2in2a1devi1.%} Wife of Yajn5adeva, this Devi1 is wor-
<>shipped by all. Without this Devi1 all Karmans (actions)
<>in the world will become futile.
<NI>(3) {%Di1ks2a1devi1.%} Wife of Yajn5adeva, this Devi1 is worshipp-
<>ed by all.
<Poem>Di1yate vimalam3 jn5a1nam
<>Ks2i1yate karmava1sana1 /
<>Tena di1ks2eti sa1 prokta1.</Poem>
<>The Devi1 grants pure knowledge.
<NI>(4) {%Svadha1devi1.%} Wife of the Pitr2s, worshipped by manes
<>and men. Offerings made to the Pitr2s without honour-
<>ing this Devi1 will prove to be futile.
<NI>(5) {%Svastidevi1.%} Consort of the Va1yu. When offering Daks2in2a1
<>and making divya-da1na (divine gifts) if ‘Svasti’ (may
<>good happen) is not uttered the gifts will be of no use.
<NI>(6) {%Pus2t2idevi1.%} Wife of Gan2apati. If this Devi1 ceased to
<>exist men and women would become feeble, because
<>she is the source of all strength.
<NI>(7) {%Tus2t2idevi1.%} Consort of Ananta worshipped by every-
<>body. If the Devi1 ceased to exist there would be no
<>happiness in the world.
<NI>(8) {%Sampatti devi1.%} Wife of I1s4a1na. Worshipped by Devas
<>and men. If the Devi1 ceased to exist the whole world
<>would become poor and indigent.
<NI>(9) {%Dhr2ti.%} Consort of Kapila. Everybody worships her [Page218-a+ 64]
<>all the time. If the Devi1 ceased to exist the whole world
<>would become timid and cowardly.
<NI>(10) {%Sati1devi1.%} Wife of Satya. Well-wisher of all, this
<>Devi1 is worshipped by muktas. (people released from
<>worldly attachments). If the Devi1 ceased to exist there
<>would be no friendship and amity between people.
<NI>(11) {%Daya1devi1.%} Wife of Moha. If the Devi1 ceased to
<>exist the world would become hellish and a fierce battle
<>field.
<NI>(12) {%Pratis2t2ha1devi1.%} Wife of Pun2ya. In the absence of
<>this Devi1, who grants good and happy results to people's
<>actions the whole world would become as though it
<>were dead.
<NI>(13) {%Siddhadevi1.%}
<NI>(14) {%Ki1rtidevi1.%} Both the Devi1s, Siddha1 and Ki1rti are
<>wives of Sukarma1. If they ceased to exist the whole
<>world would be bereft of reputation and become lifeless
<>like a dead body.
<NI>(15) {%Kriya1devi1.%} Wife of Udyoga. If she ceased to exist
<>the whole world would become inactive and cease to
<>function.
<NI>(16) {%Mithya1d2evi1.%} Wife of Adharma. Wayward and
<>characterless people worship this Devi1. If the Devi1
<>ceased to exist the whole world as evolved by Brahma1
<>would cease to exist. This Devi1 was not seen anywhere
<>in the world during Kr2tayuga. She began to appear
<>in a subtle form here and there during Treta1yuga. In
<>dva1parayuga she attained more growth and then her
<>limbs and organs became doubly strong. In Kaliyuga
<>she developed to her full stature and growth and goes
<>about everywhere with her brother, Cheat (Kapat2a).
<NI>(17) {%S4a1ntidevi1.%} (see below).
<NI>(18) {%Lajja1devi1.%} Both the Devi1s, S4a1nti and Lajja1 are good-
<>natured wives. If they ceased to exist the world would
<>become dull and sleepy.
<NI>(19-20-21) {%Buddhidevi1, Medha1devi1%} and {%Dhr2tidevi1.%} These
<>three Devi1s are wives of Jn5a1na. If they ceased to exist
<>the world would become steeped in ignorance and
<>foolishness.
<NI>(22) {%Mu1rti.%} Wife of Dharma. She is very beautiful and
<>effulgent. In her absence the universal soul would be-
<>come devoid of vitality, helpless and meaningless.
<NI>(23) {%S4ri1devi1.%} Wife of Ma1li1. Her absence will make the
<>world lifeless.
<NI>(24) {%Nidra1devi1.%} Wife of Ka1la1gni. The Devi1, a sage
<>who has attained realisation, affects everybody in the
<>world during night and makes them lose consciousness
<>and plunges them in sleep. In the absence of this Devi1
<>the world will become a lunatic asylum.
<NI>(25-26-27) {%Ra1tri1, Sandhya1%} and {%Divasa1.%} These three are the
<>wives of Time (Ka1la). In their absence nobody would
<>have any sense of time and none would be able to cal-
<>culate and fix time.
<NI>(28-29) {%Vis4appu%} and {%Da1ham.%} Vis4appu = hunger.
<>Da1ham = thirst. These two Devi1s are the wives of
<>greed (Lobha). They go about the world affecting
<>people and thus making them worried and miserable.
<NI>(30-31) {%Prabha1devi1%} and {%Da1hika1 devi1%} are the wives of
<>Tejas (Vitality). Without them I1s4vara will find it im-
<>possible to continue the function of creation.
<NI>(32-33) {%Mr2tyu%} and {%Jara1%} are the consorts of Prakr2s2t2a-
<>jvara and daughters of Ka1la. And, if they cease to exist,
<>Brahma1's creation would also cease. (For Brahma1's
<>creation individual souls with the will for action are [Page218-b+ 65]
<>necessary. He cannot create a soul of his own. So
<>death is a pre-condition of creation, birth. If there is
<>no death there is no birth also. There is a school of
<>thought which maintains that if there is no death but
<>only birth there will not be space on earth for the living,
<>and therefore birth will stop if there is no death.).
<NI>(34-35) {%Tandra1%} and {%Pri1ti%} are the daughters of Nidra1 and
<>consorts of Sukha (pleasure, happiness). These Devi1s go
<>around the world on the orders of Brahma1.
<NI>(36-37) {%S4raddha1%} and {%Bhakti%} are the consorts of Vaira1gya
<>(aversion to wordly comforts, renunciation) and they
<>give salvation to the souls of the people in the world.
<NI>Aditi, mother of the Devas, Diti, mother of the Asuras,
<>Surabhi, mother of cows, Kadru1, mother of serpents
<>and Vinata1, mother of Garud2as are also involved in the
<>process of creation, and they are born out of parts of
<>Devi1.
<NI>6) {%Idol of Devi1.%} There are special injunctions for mak-
<>ing idols of the Devi1 for installation in temples. Can2d2i-
<>ka1devi1 should have twenty hands. The hands on the
<>right side should hold S4u1la, sword, disc, cord,
<>d2amaru, s4akti etc. and the hands on the left side should
<>hold na1gapa1s4a, flag, club, mirror etc. (There are also
<>idols with slight differences from the above).
<NI>Laks2mi1devi1 would hold in her right hand the lotus
<>flower and a Ku1vala fruit in the left. Sarasvati1devi1 will
<>have in her hands books, aks2ama1la1 and vi1n2a1. Gan3ga1devi1
<>rides on a makaramatsya (fish) holding in her hands
<>a pot and lotus flowers. Yamuna1devi1 rides the turtle
<>with a pot in hand and she is blue in colour.
<NI>Now, about the saptama1tr2s. Tumburu, white in colour,
<>and mounted on an ox rests on a s4u1la with vi1n2a1 in hand
<>before the Ma1tr2s. Among the Ma1tr2s Bra1hmi1 has four
<>faces, wears the aks2ama1la1 and holds the aks2apa1tra etc.
<>in her left hand. She is seated on a swan. S4a1n3kari1
<>(Ma1hes4vari1) is white in colour. She has in her right
<>hand bow and arrows, and the disc and bow in her left
<>hand. The ox is her vehicle. Kauma1ri1 is seated on the
<>peacock. She has two hands in one of which is held
<>S4akti (Vel). Laks2mi1 has in her right hand the conch
<>and disc, and the club and the lotus flower in her left
<>hand. Va1ra1hi1 rides the buffalo with staff, sword, club
<>and conch in her hands. Indra1n2i1 is seated on the club,
<>holding diamond in her hands. She has a thousand
<>eyes. Ca1mun2d2i1 has three eyes and is without flesh in the
<>body. Her hairs are raised above. She holds in her left
<>hand elephant's skin and s4u1la in the right hand. At
<>times she is seated on a corpse also.
<NI>Now, about the idols of the eight Am3ba1s. Rudra Carc-
<>cika1 holds a skull, S4u1la and cord in her hands. She
<>wears elephant's skin, her legs slightly held up. She
<>becomes Rudraca1mun2d2a1 when she assumes eight hands,
<>and holds the skull and the d2amaru. And in dancing
<>pose she is called Nat2es4vari1, and with four faces Maha1l-
<>aks2mi1. She is called Siddhaca1mun2d2a1 when she assumes
<>the form with ten hands and three eyes, and when she
<>eats men, horses and buffaloes. In this form she holds
<>in her right hand the sword, d2amaru etc. and in her
<>left hand the Tris4u1la, bell etc. Since the Devi1 is sarva-
<>siddhiprada1yika1 (giver of all divine attainments or
<>assets) she is Siddhayoges4vari1 also. There is also an-
<>other Devi1 in this very form who holds the pa1s4a and
<>Am3kus4a in her hand and who is slightly red in colour.
<>She is called Bhairavi1. When Bhairavi1 assumes the
<>form with twelve hands she is called Ru1pavidya1. All the [Page219-a+ 64]
<>above eight Devi1s were born in burial grounds and are
<>Raudramu1rtis. They are known as the As2t2a1mba1s.

<HI>DEVI1PI1T2HA. The dead body of Sati1devi1 crumbled into
<>small pieces and fell in different places in Bha1rata.
<>Each place where a piece of the dead body fell, is known
<>by the name Devi1pi1t2ha. There is a reason why the
<>dead body crumbled into pieces.
<NI>Daks2a performed a sacrifice to which S4iva was not invit-
<>ed and Sati1devi1 came to the sacrifice, and committed
<>suicide by jumping into the fire. S4iva who became furi-
<>ous, killed Daks2a and carrying the dead body of his
<>wife on his shoulder, walked the length and breadth of
<>Bha1rata like a mad man. It seemed that he was not
<>going to recover from this mania, and the devas (gods)
<>were worried at this. To rescue S4iva from this mental
<>disposition, Maha1vis2n2u, unseen by others, followed
<>S4iva with a bow and arrow. Whenever it was conve-
<>nient Maha1vis2n2u sent an arrow at the body of Sati1devi1,
<>on the shoulder of S4iva. By the hitting of the arrows
<>the body was crumbled into pieces and fell here and
<>there. Thus within a few days the body of Sati1devi1
<>came to an end and S4iva walked to Kaila1sa. The
<>pieces of the dead body fell in 108 places, and thus 108
<>Devi1pi1t2has came into existence. The names of the
<>places and the names by which the Devi1 is known in
<>such places are given below.

<HI>DEVI1STHA1NA. A holy place, dedicated to S4a1kam3bhari1
<>Devi1. It is stated in Maha1bha1rata (Vana Parva, Chapter
<>84, Stanza 13) that one who stays in this holy place
<>and eats greens (cira or kira) for three days will get
<>the fruits of eating greens for twelve years.
<HI>DEVI1TI1RTHA(M). The common name of three holy
<>centres (S4an3khini1, Madhuvat2i1, Mr2gadhu1ma1) in Kuruk-
<>s2etra. Those who bathe in S4ankhini1 will become beauti-
<>ful in appearance, while those who bathe in Madhu-
<>vat2i1 and Mr2gadhu1ma1 will get the benefits of perform-
<>ing a thousand goda1nas (gifts of cows).
<HI>DHA. This letter means dha1ran2a, S4obhana, Dha1ta1, dhat-
<>tu1ra (umma plant). (Chapter 348, Agni Pura1n2a).
<HI>DHA1MA1. A hermit who protected Gan3ga1-Maha1dva1ra.
<>(Maha1bha1rata, Udyoga Parva, Chapter 111, Stanza
<>17).
<HI>DHAMADHAMA. A follower of Subrahman2ya. (S4loka
<>27, Chapter 46, S4alya Parva).
<HI>DHANAKA. A king of the Yaya1ti dynasty. (Navama
<>Skandha, Bha1gavata).
<HI>DHANAPA1LA. A Vais4ya who lived in the city of Ayo-
<>dhya1. He built in Ayodhya1 a temple for the Sun-god
<>and appointed, for a year, paying his wages in advance,
<>a scholar well versed in Pura1n2as to read aloud the
<>Pura1n2as in the temple. After six months Dhanapa1la
<>died and as a result of the goodness accrued to him by
<>his worthy deeds the Sun-god came to him with his
<>chariot and took him to his place and seating him on
<>his seat paid respects to him. Later he was taken to
<>Brahmaloka. (Bhavis2ya Pura1n2a, Brahma Ka1n2d2a,
<>Chapter 94).
<HI>DHANAS4ARMA1. A brahmin belonging to Madhyades4a.
<>Once when Dhanas4arma1 went to the forest to get
<>darbha he confronted three fierce evil spirits and gett-
<>ing frightened ran away from them. But the spirits
<>followed him and the Brahmin then cried and said
<>“Oh, please do not harm me. Maha1vis2n2u will bless
<>you.”
<NI>The moment they heard the name of Vis2n2u there came
<>a change in them. They reiterated their story to
<>Dhanas4arma1. Their names were Vais4a1kha, Suda1sa and
<>Advaita. Because of the ingratitude of Vais4a1kha he
<>got a nick-name ‘Kr2taghna’ (ungrateful man) also. All
<>the three were human beings in their previous birth.
<>Not only that, Advaita was a king called Harivi1ra.
<>All the three became evil spirits because of their sins.
<NI>From the conversation Dhanas4arma1 understood that
<>Vais4a1kha was his father and they then knew each other. [Page220-b+ 64]
<>The son then anxiously enquired what he should do to
<>absolve his father of his sins and save him from hell.
<>Vais4a1kha then told him observe the vrata, Vais4a1kha-
<>pu1rn2ima1. He did that and all the spirits went to
<>heaven. (Chapter 94, Padma Pura1n2a).
<HI>DHANAVARDHANA. A virtuous Vais4ya who lived in
<>the island of Pus2kara in the Kr2tayuga. Once Dhana-
<>vardhana after having performed Vais4vadeva sat for
<>his food. At that time he heard a cry from outside
<>“Annam3 dehi (give me food)”. Dhanavardhana imme-
<>diately came out to see who the visitor was but seeing
<>none went in and started taking his food. Because he
<>ate his food once left aside, his body was torn to
<>hundred pieces. (Bhavis2ya, Brahma Ka1n2d2a).
<HI>DHANAVATI1. See Su1ryaprabha1.
<HI>DHANAN5JAYA I. A famous serpent. This serpent was
<>born to Kas4yapapraja1pati of his wife Kadru1. (S4loka 5,
<>Chapter 35, A1di Parva). This serpent served as a rope to
<>bind the horses to the chariot of S4iva during the time
<>of burning to death the Tripuras. (S4loka 29, Chapter
<>34, Karn2a Parva). This serpent sits in the court of
<>Varun2a and worships him. (S4loka 9, Chapter 9,
<>Sabha1 parva).

<HI>DHANAN5JAYA IV. A Sanskrit critic who lived during
<>the period between 11th and 12th century A.D. He was
<>a member of the court of King Mun5ja. His important
<>work is ‘Das4aru1paka’. This work contains three
<>hundred s4lokas divided in four separate divisions. After
<>the death of Mun5ja, Dhanika, brother of Dhanan5jaya,
<>wrote a commentary on this work. The treatise is
<>named ‘Das4aru1pa1valoka’. There are several quotations in
<>this from the Sanskrit dramas ‘Ven2i1sam3ha1ra’ and ‘Ratna1-
<>vali1’. He has in this work discussed Drama and Poetry
<>in general giving prominence to the emotional side of it.
<>The critical work, Das4aru1pa1valoka, contains quotations
<>from a book ‘Ka1vyanirn2aya’ by Dhanika himself. But
<>the work ‘Ka1vyanirn2aya’ has not yet been made avail-
<>able. Even in composing poetry Dhanan5jaya was well
<>versed. He is the author of the well-known epic ‘Ra1ghava-
<>pa1n2d2avi1ya’. It is known as ‘Dvisandha1na Ka1vya’ also.
<>This is based on the lives of Pa1n2d2avas and S4ri1 Ra1ma.
<NI>Dhanan5jaya bears the name of S4rutaki1rti also. It is
<>stated by scholars that Ra1ghavapa1n2d2avi1ya was written
<>during the period 1123 to 1140 A.D. (History of classi-
<>cal Sanskrit Literature).
<HI>DHANAN5JAYA V. There was once a brahmin ascetic
<>called Dhanan5jaya in the gotra of Vasis2t2ha. This hermit
<>had hundred wives and got hundred sons, one by each
<>of his wives. The son born of his wife S4a1bha1ka (S4ala1ka)
<>was named Karun2a. The father divided his assets
<>equally among his sons and yet they quarrelled with
<>each other.
<NI>Once Karun2amuni went to the shores of Bhava-
<>na1s4ini1 along with a few other munis to worship
<>Narasim3ha. There a brahmin had brought a lovely
<>lime as an offering to the deity. Karun2a took the lime
<>from him and smelt it. At this the brahmin got angry
<>and cursed him. “Sinner, may you live as a fly for a
<>hundred years. Then Maha1tma1 Dadhi1ca will give you
<>back your original form.” Karun2a instantly became a [Page221-a+ 65]
<>fly and he pleaded to his wife thus “Beloved, I have
<>become a fly. Please do protect me.” Karun2a started
<>flying hither and thither and his cruel brothers made
<>the fly fall in oil and killed it. S4ucismita1, wife of
<>Karun2a, started weeping laying the dead fly in her lap.
<>Arundhati1devi1 passing that way saw her and consoled
<>her thus: ‘S4ucismita1, stop lamenting. I shall bring it
<>to life this instant by sacred ashes.’ So saying the Devi1
<>took some ashes from the fire-pit and reciting the
<>powerful Mr2tyun5jaya mantra sprinkled it on the dead
<>body of the fly. S4ucismita1 fanned the fly. The potency
<>of the ashes brought the fly back to life.
<NI>After a hundred years one of his relatives killed it
<>again. S4ucismita1 grief-stricken, took the dead fly to the
<>Mahars2i, Dadhi1ca. The sage told her thus: “It was
<>bhasma (sacred ashes) that gave life three times to
<>Jamadagni, Kas4yapa, the devas and myself. I will,
<>therefore, give life to thy husband by bhasma itself.”
<>Dadhi1ca took some ashes and meditating on Mahes4vara
<>recited a mantra and sprinkled it on the dead fly and
<>brought it back to life. By the touch of Dadhi1ca, the
<>fly, husband of S4ucismita1, became Karun2a again and
<>both of them went back to their hermitage. (Chapter
<>101, Padma Pura1n2a).
<HI>DHANAN5JAYA VI. A brahmin devotee of Maha1vis2n2u
<>who lived in the Treta1 yuga. Once, in winter, when
<>for want of proper clothing he could bear the biting
<>cold no longer, he broke some twigs of the As4vattha
<>tree (poplar leafed fig tree) and made fire to warm
<>him. When Dhanan5jaya broke the twigs it gave reflec-
<>tive pain on the body of Maha1vis2n2u. But Maha1vis2n2u
<>who was aware of the unflinching devotion of Dhanan5-
<>jaya to himself was not displeased but appeared before
<>Dhanan5jaya with wounds all over the body. The
<>Brahmin enquired how Vis2n2u got the wounds and on
<>knowing that it was the consequence of his breaking the
<>twigs of As4vattha Dhanan5jaya in utter grief started to
<>cut his own head off. Greatly pleased Vis2n2u stopped
<>him from his attempt and asked him to name a boon.
<>Very modestly he replied that he would be satisfied if
<>he was given the strength to continue as a devotee of
<>Vis2n2u. (Padma Pura1n2a, Kriya1 Ka1n2d2a).
<HI>DHANES4VARA. A Brahmin born in the city of Avanti.
<>Abandoning the duties enjoined by scriptures to a
<>Brahmin he strayed away from the Brahmin fold doing
<>all sorts of sins. He became a store-house of sins. He
<>was engaged in commerce and once while travelling he
<>reached the city of Ma1his2mati1. There he met many people
<>from several adjoining villages who had come there to ob-
<>serve the Ka1rttika Vrata. Later Dhanes4vara was bitten by
<>a cobra there and fell down fainting. Very soon he died.
<NI>When he died the messengers of Yama came
<>and took him bound to Ka1lapuri1. There, Citra-
<>gupta read out to Yama all the sins Dhanes4vara had
<>committed from boyhood till his death. Citragupta
<>added “Even from boyhood there is no record of any
<>good deed done by him. On the other hand the sins he
<>has committed could not be told in a year. He is an
<>incarnation of evil. He must be roasted in hell till the
<>end of the world.” On hearing this the body of Yama
<>blazed like flame with anger and calling aside an atten-
<>dant he ordered, “Hi, Pretapa1laka, take this sinner
<>away, beat him with spiked clubs and throw him into
<>Kumbhi1pa1ka”. Pretapa1laka instantly took him away
<>and breaking his head with a thorn-spiked club threw [Page221-b+ 64]
<>him into a copper vessel containing burning oil. But to
<>his great amazement the burning oil became cold as
<>soon as the body of Dhanes4vara came into contact with
<>it just as it happened once before in the case of Prahla1da.
<>The perplexed attendant ran to Yama and told him of
<>this strange incident. Yama was also surprised at this
<>novelty in hell and called back Dhanes4vara and started
<>enquiring about his life once again. Na1rada came there
<>at that time. Both of them then talked about Dhanes4vara.
<>Na1rada told Yama how the unanticipated thing so
<>happened because Dhanes4vara on the eve of his death
<>spent some time with people who were observing the
<>Ka1rttika Vrata washing away thus all the sins accumu-
<>lated so far in his life. Hearing that Yama took Dhanes4-
<>vara round hell and giving Dhanes4vara, the position of
<>a Yaks2a sent him to Kuberapuri1. (Chapter 115, Padma
<>Pura1n2a).

<HI>DHANURVEDA.
<NI>{%General information.%} A scientific treatise on the art of
<>warfare in ancient Bha1rata. Because of the undue im-
<>portance of the science the treatise is deemed and respect-
<>ed as a Veda. There have been innumerable books on the
<>subject to teach Dhanurveda to the Ks2atriya youths.
<>In the book ‘Prastha1nabheda’ by Madhusu1dana Saras-
<>vati1 he states that Dhanurveda is a branch of Yajurveda.
<>A Sanskrit book called Dhanurvedasam3hita1 is now
<>available. Some scholars are of opinion that not much
<>antiquity can be attributed to this work. Many books
<>relating to Dhanurveda have been lost to us. What
<>details are there in Agni Pura1n2a are given below:
<NI>2) {%Divisions of Dhanurveda.%} This is known as Catus2pa1da
<>(four-footed) also because of the fact that the four
<>main constituents of an army are the chariots, elephants,
<>horses and men. Dhanurveda has been divided into
<>five parts (1) Yantramukta, (2) Pa1n2imukta, (3) Mukta-
<>sandha1rita, (4) Amukta and (5) Ba1huyuddha. There is
<>another division according to Astra and S4astra. There
<>is yet another division of the science into two by some
<>scholars from a different viewpoint calling them Ma1ya1
<>and R2ju.
<NI>{%Yantramukta%} (Yantra = machine). This means a war-
<>fare where machines are used. Ks2epin2i1 (sling) bow and
<>arrow and other such machines are included in this.
<NI>{%Pa1n2imukta.%} (Pa1n2i = hand). This is where the hand is used
<>mainly. Stone, mace etc. are used in this.
<NI>{%Muktasandha1rita.%} This includes the use of weapons like
<>a spear.
<NI>{%Amukta.%} The use of a weapon like a sword which never
<>leaves your hand.
<NI>{%Ba1huyuddha.%} (Ba1hu = head). The fight with only bare
<>hands without the use of a weapon. One who wishes
<>to fight should make ready his own weapons. He should
<>be one who will never get tired even after exertion. A
<>war with bows and arrows is considered the best and [Page222-a+ 65]
<>most manly, that with a spear ranks next, a fight with a
<>sword is bad and a fight with hands, the most mean.
<NI>3) {%The Trainees.%} Only a brahmin is entitled to be a pre-
<>ceptor in Dhanurveda. The Ks2atriya and Vais4ya should
<>learn from him. The S4u1dra can fight of his own in
<>danger. He is not allowed to learn military science
<>from a preceptor. Those mixed-born are to help the
<>king during a war.
<NI>4) {%Various postures.%} There are nine different ways of
<>standing when you give a fight, classified according to
<>the different positions of one's foot, heel and knee.
<NI>{%Samapa1da.%} When you stand with your An3gus2t2ha, Nari-
<>ya1n2i (Gulpha), Pa1n2i and Pa1da closely joined together,
<>it is termed Samapa1da.
<NI>{%Vais4a1kha.%} Keep your legs twentyseven inches apart and
<>without bending your knees stand erect throwing your
<>weight on the fingers of the foot. This stand is called
<>Vais4a1kha.
<NI>{%Man2d2ala.%} Keep your knees thirtysix inches apart and
<>stand in the shape of a ham3sapan3kti and this position
<>is called Man2d2ala.
<NI>{%A1li1d2ha.%} Bend your right knee and thigh in the shape of a
<>plough and without any movement to that posture
<>draw your left leg fortyfive inches apart. This stand is
<>called A1li1d2ha.
<NI>{%Pratya1li1d2ha.%} If you bend your left knee and thigh and
<>stand like above it is called Pratya1li1d2ha.
<NI>{%Ja1ta.%} Place your right foot straight and place the left one
<>perpendicular to the right one with the heels and the
<>ankles of the feet five fingers apart. The whole length
<>of the posture should not exceed twelve fingers. This
<>stand is called Ja1ta.
<NI>{%Dan2d2aya1ta.%} Keep the right knee bent and the left leg
<>straight or vice versa. This posture is called Dan2d2a1yata.
<NI>{%Vikat2a.%} If for the above posture the distance between
<>the two legs is two palm-length it is Vikat2a.
<NI>{%Svaput2a.%} Keep the knees vigun2as and the feet Utta1yanas
<>for the posture of Svaput2a.
<NI>{%S4vastika.%} Keep your legs sixteen fingers apart and raise
<>your feet a little keeping the level of both the feet equal.
<>This is Svastika.
<NI>5) {%Use of arrows.%} Before a man is to make use of a bow
<>and arrow he should first take the posture of Svastika
<>and bow down. He should take the bow by the left hand,
<>take the arrow by the right hand. He then should adopt
<>a posture of either Vais4a1kha or Ja1ta and taking the bow
<>should sink into the earth one end of the bow and
<>draw the bow-string up to the other end and see whe-
<>ther there is sufficient length for the string. He should
<>take an arrow from his sheath and thrust the arrow-
<>head into the ground near the bow. He should
<>place his elbow on the top of the arrow with his forearm
<>bent and fist clenched. If the clenched fist touches the
<>top of the bow that bow and arrow is superior to any
<>other. He should tie the bow-string in a way that the
<>distance between the tied bow-string and the bow is
<>twelve fingers. He should place the bow in front of his
<>navel and the arrows in a quiver on his hip. Taking an
<>arrow he should raise the bow and fix the arrow at a
<>point on the bow-tring between his eye and ear. He
<>should take the arrow by his left fist and fix it so that the
<>fist touches the left nipple. The bow should not change
<>position horizontally, vertically, laterally, face down-
<>wards or upwards. Then taking an arrow from the sheath
<>by the thumb and forefinger fix it on the bow-string and [Page222-b+ 65]
<>stretch it well before sending the shot. The clue to
<>correct aim is this. Anything that one can see with one's
<>eyes but can be covered by his fist is within the arrow-
<>shot. When one arrow is sent another should be taken
<>immediately from the sheath and sent in quick succes-
<>sion. This is called Upaccheda.
<NI>6) {%Operations with a sword and a carma (shield).%} There
<>are thirtytwo different ways of putting into use a sword
<>or a ‘carma’ (shield). Bhra1nta, Udbhra1nta, A1viddha,
<>A1pluta, Vipluta, Sr2ta, Sam3pa1ta, Samudi1rn2a, S4vetapa1ta,
<>A1kula, Savyoddhu1ta, Savya1vadhu1ta, Daks2in2oddhu1ta, Dak-
<>s2in2avadhu1ta, Ana1laks2ita, Visphot2a, Kara1la, Indramaha1sa-
<>kha, Vikara1la, Nipa1ta, Vibhi1s2an2a, Bhaya1naka, Samagra-
<>pa1da, Arddhapa1da, Tr2ti1ya1m3s4apa1da, Pa1da, Ardhava1rija,
<>Va1rija, Pratya1li1d2ha, A1li1d2ha, Vara1ha and Lalita.
<NI>7) {%Operations with ropes.%} There are eleven different ways
<>of using a rope in a war. Para1vr2tta, Apara1vr2tta, Gr2hi1ta,
<>Laghugr2hi1ta, U1rdhvaks2ipta, Adhah2ks2ipta, Sandha1rita,
<>S4yenapa1ta, Gajapa1ta and Gra1hagra1hya. Adepts in this art
<>have stated that there are five acts in the rope-operation.
<>They are: R2ju, A1yata, Vis4a1la, Tiryak and Bhra1mita.
<NI>8) {%Mode of using the weapons.%} (1) Deeds with a wheel
<>are: Chedana, Bhedana, Pa1tana, Bhraman2a, S4ayana and
<>Vikartana.
<NI>(2) {%S4u1la (spear).%} Deeds with this are: A1sphot2ana, Ks2o-
<>lana, Bhedana, Tra1sana, A1ndolana and A1gha1ta.
<NI>(3) {%Tomara (iron club).%} Deeds with this are: Dr2s2t2igha1ta
<>Pa1rs4va1gha1ta, R2jupaks2a and Is2t2apra1pta.
<NI>(4) {%Gada (Mace).%} Deeds with this are: Gomu1tra, A1hata,
<>Prabhu1ta, Kamala1sana, Tata, U1rdhvaga1tra, Va1mana-
<>mita, Daks2in2amita, A1vr2tta, Para1vr2tta, Pa1doddhu1ta,
<>and Avapluta Ham3samarda.
<NI>(5) {%Paras4u (axe).%} Deeds with this are: Kara1la, Avagh-
<>a1ta, Dam3s4a, Upapluta, Ks2iptahasta, Sthita and S4u1nya,
<NI>(6) {%Mud2gara (hammer).%} Deeds with this are: Ta1d2ana.
<>Chedana, Cu1rn2ana, Plavana and Gha1tana.
<NI>(7) {%Bhindipa1la.%} Deeds with this are: Sam3s4ra1nta, Vis4ra1-
<>nta, Govisarga and Sudurdhara. Deeds with the Lagud2a
<>are the same as these.
<NI>(8) {%Vajra.%} Deeds with this are: Antya, Madhya, Pa1ra1vr2t-
<>ta, and Nides4a1nta. The deeds with Pat2t2is4a are the same
<>as there.
<NI>(9) {%Kr2pa1n2a (Churika1--small dagger).%} Deeds with these
<>are: Haran2a, Chedana, Gha1tana, Bhedana, Mr2m3ks2an2a,
<>Pa1tana and Sphot2ana.
<NI>(10) {%Ks2epin2i1 (Kavin2a--Sling).%} Deeds are Tra1sana, Raks2-
<>an2a, Gha1tana, Baloddharan2a and A1yata.
<NI>The feats of one who fights a bludgeon or cudgel are the
<>following:
<NI>Santya1ga, Avadam3s4a, Vara1hoddhu1taka, Hasta1vahasta,
<>A1li1na, Ekahasta, Avahasta, Dvihasta, Ba1hupa1s4a, Kat2ireci-
<>taka, Utgata, Urogha1ta, Lala1t2agha1ta, Bhujavidhamana,
<>Karoddhu1ta, Vima1na, Pa1da1hati, Vipa1dika Ga1trasam3s4le-
<>s2an2a, Sa1nta, Ga1traviparyaya, U1rdhapraha1ra, Gha1ta, Go-
<>mu1tra, Savya, Bhaks2in2a. Pa1raka, Ta1raka, Dan2d2a, A1kula,
<>Kabari1bandha, Tiryagbandha, Apa1ma1rga, Bhi1mavega,
<>Sudars4ana, Sim3ha1kra1nta, Gaja1kra1nta and Garbha1kra1nta.
<>(Agni Pura1n2a, Chapters 249-252).
<NI>9) {%Dhanurveda in Maha1bha1rata.%} Some references to Dhanur-
<>veda in Maha1ha1rata are given below:
<NI>(1) A sage named S4aradva1n was a noted preceptor in

<NI>(2) Dron2a1ca1rya learnt Dhanurveda from Paras4ura1ma [Page223-a+ 65]
<>and imparted it to many other disciples of his including
<>the Kauravas and Pa1n2d2avas. (Chapter 129, A1di Parva,
<>M.B.).
<NI>(3) Agnives4a, a sage, learnt Dhanurveda from sage
<>Agastya. (S4loka 9, Chapter 133, A1di Parva, M.B.).
<NI>(4) Dhanurveda has got ten an3gas and four caran2as.
<>(Chapters 6 and 41, S4alya Parva, M.B.).
<NI>(5) Four-footed Dhanurveda worshipped Subrahman2ya.
<>(S4loka 22, Chapter 44, S4alya Parva, M.B.).
<HI>DHANUS2A. An ancient sage. He was a priest in the ya1ga

<HI>DHANVANTARI I. A deva who was a preceptor in
<>A1yurveda.
<NI>1) {%Origin.%} The devas and asuras together churned the
<>milky ocean, Ks2i1ra1bdhi, to salvage Amr2ta (Nectar) from
<>it. After thousand years there arose from the ocean a
<>deva with a Kaman2d2alu (water-pot of ascetics) in one
<>hand and a dan2d2a in the other. That deva was Dhan-
<>vantari, (S4loka 31, Sarga 45, Ba1la Ka1n2d2a, Va1lmi1ki
<>Ra1ma1yan2a).
<NI>The birth of Dhanvantari from the ocean of Milk is
<>described in Chapter 29 of Harivam3s4a thus:
<NI>Prosperous-looking Dhanvantari rising above the water-
<>level of Ks2i1ra1bdhi stood worshipping Maha1vis2n2u. Vis2n2u
<>gave him the name of Abja. Dhanvantari is thus known
<>by the name of Abja also. Dhanvantari bowing to
<>Vis2n2u said “Prabho, I am your son. Allot to me
<>yajn5abha1ga”.
<NI>Vts2n2u replied thus: “Portions of yajn5a have already
<>been allotted. Because you were born after the devas
<>you cannot be considered as one among them. You will
<>be born again in the world for a second time and then you
<>will be a celebrity. In your second life even from while
<>in the womb you will have knowledge of An2ima1 and
<>Garima1. Therefore you will be born as a deva incar-
<>nate. You will write in eight divisions a book on
<>A1yurveda; your second life will be in Dva1para yuga.”
<>After having said so much Vis2n2u disappeared.
<NI>2) {%Rebirth of Dhanvantari.%} Suhotra, King of Ka1s4i, in
<>the second Dva1para yuga had two sons, S4ala and Gr2tsa-
<>mada. S4unaka was the son of Gr2tsamada. S4ala got a son,
<>A1rs2t2is2en2a. Ka1s4a was born to A1rs2t2is2en2a. To Ka1s4a was
<>born Di1rghatapas (Dhanva1). For a long time Dhanva1
<>did not have any children and so he went and did
<>penance to propitiate Abjadeva. Abjadeva (Dhanvan-
<>tari) was pleased and was born as a son to Dhanva1.
<>Dhanva1 named the boy as Dhanvantari and the latter
<>taught his disciples A1yurveda, by parts, eight in num-
<>ber. From Dhanvantari in order were born Ketuma1n--
<>Bhi1maratha--Divoda1sa. (Chapter 29, Harivam3s4a).
<NI>3) {%Dhanvantari and Pariks2it.%} There is a story in the
<>pura1n2as that when Taks2aka went and bit Pari1ks2it to
<>kill him, a Vis2aha1ri (one who cures those infected with
<>snake venom) rushed to save the king but was bribed
<>and sent back by Taks2aka. There are indications in
<>the Pura1n2as to show that the Vis2aha1ri under reference
<>was Dhanvantari though it is stated that Kas4yapa was
<>the person involved. (See under Taks2aka).
<NI>4) {%Dhanvantari and Manasa1devi1.%} In the Kr2s2n2ajanma [Page223-b+ 65]
<>Khan2d2a of Brahmavaivarta Pura1n2a there is a story
<>connecting Dhanvantari with Manasa1devi1, a serpent-
<>goddess.
<NI>Once Dhanvantari with his disciples was going to Kaila1sa.
<>On the way Taks2aka made a venom-spitting hiss. At
<>once one of the disciples of Dhanvantari boldly went
<>and plucked the diamond on the head of Taks2aka and
<>threw it to the ground. Hearing this Va1suki, King of
<>serpents, sent to Dhanvantari thousands of serpents
<>under the leadership of Dron2a, Pun2d2ari1ka and Dhanan5-
<>jaya. The poisonous breath of all these serpents joined
<>together made the disciples of Dhanvantari swoon.
<>Immediately Dhanvantari by a medicine made from
<>vanaspati made all his disciples recover and then sent
<>all the serpents to a swoon. When Va1suki heard this
<>he sent the serpent-maid, Manasa1devi1, a disciple of
<>S4iva, to face Dhanvantari. Manasa1devi1 and Gad2u1ra
<>were both disciples of S4iva. But Dhanvantari was a
<>follower of Gad2u1ra. Manasa1devi1 made all the disciples
<>of Dhanvantari swoon but the latter because of his great
<>proficiency is Vis2avidya1 soon brought back his disciples
<>to normal. When Manasa1devi1 found that it was impos-
<>sible to defeat Dhanvantari or his disciples by using
<>poison Manasa1devi1 took the tris4u1la given to her by S4iva
<>and aimed it at Dhanvantari. Seeing this S4iva and
<>Brahma1 appeared before them and pacifying them sent
<>them all their way.
<HI>DHANVANTARI II. (Amr2ta1ca1rya). An eminent medi-
<>cal scientist born in the ambas2t2ha caste. There is no
<>reference anywhere in the Pura1n2as regarding any
<>relationship between the two Dhanvantaris. There is
<>the following story about Amr2ta1ca1rya in Ambas2t2ha1ca1ra-
<>candrika1.
<NI>Once Ga1lava Mahars2i went to the forest to collect
<>darbha and firewood. He walked for long and felt
<>thristy and hungry. Then he saw a girl coming that
<>way with water and Ga1lava quenched his thirst taking
<>water from her. Pleased with her the Mahars2i blessed
<>her saying “May you get a good son.” The girl replied
<>that she was still unmarried. Ga1lava then made a figure
<>of a male with darbha and told her to get a child from
<>that figure. She was a Vais4ya girl named Vi1rabhadra1
<>and she got a beautiful child of that darbha male.
<>Because the boy was born to a Vais4ya of a brahmin
<>male he belonged to the Ambas2t2ha caste. The boy was
<>named Amr2ta1ca1rya.
<HI>DHANYAMA1LA1. The foster-mother of Atika1ya. (See

<HI>DHARA1. Wife of a Vasu named Dron2a (see under

<HI>DHARA I. He is the first Vasu born to Dharma of his

<HI>DHARA1PA1LA. A king of ancient times. He ruled over
<>a country named Vidis4a1. There is a holy place where
<>the rivers Vetasi1 and Vetravati1 meet. Dhara1pa1la cons-
<>tructed a temple at the confluence. There is a story also
<>for his doing so.
<NI>Once a S4iva-pa1rs2ada brought to S4iva a woman at a time
<>when Pa1rvati1 was away from the place. S4iva and the
<>woman were enjoying each other when Pa1rvati1 came
<>to the place. Getting angry she cursed the Pa1rs2ada and
<>made him into a fox. The fox came to the place where
<>the rivers Vetasi1 and Vetravati1 meet and did penance
<>there and attained Moks2a. Dhara1pa1la saw the divine
<>plane coming down to take the Pa1rs2ada away and he,
<>therefore, constructed a temple at the place where the
<>plane landed. He then arranged scholars well versed
<>in Vedas and s4a1stras to read Padma Pura1n2a there. He
<>himself read Padma Pura1n2a and attained moks2a.
<>(Chapter 29, Padma Pura1n2a).

<NI>2) {%Marriage and family life.%} The Bha1gavata states that
<>Dharma married the thirteen daughters of Daksapraja1-

<>Lamba1, Kukubh, Ja1mi, Vis4va1, Sa1dhya1, Marutvati1,
<>Vasu, Muhu1rta1 and San3kalpa1 and also a woman named
<>Sunr2ta1. According to Maha1bha1rata Dharma married
<>the following daughters of Daks2a: Ki1rti, Laks2mi1, Dhr2ti,
<>Medha1, Pus2t2i, S4raddha1, Kriya1, Buddhi and Lajja1.
<NI>From each of his wives there originated a family. The
<>son born to each is given below S4raddha1--S4ubha;
<>Maitri1--Prasa1da; Daya1--Abhaya; S4a1nti--Sukha; Tus2t2i--
<>Moda; Unnati--Darpa; Buddhi--Artha; Medha1--
<>Sukr2ti; Titiks2a1--S4ama; Hri1--Pras4raya. Mu1rti gave

<>Satyasena became famous by slaying many cruel and

<>Indrasena. Vidyotana became the father of stanayitnu.
<>Kukubh delivered San3kat2a and San3kat2a became the
<>father of Ki1kat2a and Durgadeva. Ja1mi got a son Svarga
<>and of him was born Nandi1. Vis4va1 gave birth to Vis4va- [Page224-b+ 60]
<>devas and Sa1dhya1 to Sa1dhyas. These Sa1dhyas are different
<>from those born of Brahma1. Sa1dhyas became the father of
<>Arthasiddhi. Marutvati1 gave birth to Marutvat and
<>Jayanta. Vasu gave birth to eight sons and they were

<NI>3) {%Prominent sons.%} Four sons of Dharma, Hari, Kr2s2n2a,

<NI>4) {%The daughter named Dharmavrata1.%} Dharmadeva got a
<>daughter named Dharmavrata1 of his wife Dharmavati1.
<>Dharmavrata1 was an ascetic and she was married to
<>Mari1ci, son of Brahma1. One day Mari1ci came back
<>from the forest after getting darbha and flowers extre-
<>mely tired and so after food lay down to rest. His wife
<>sitting by his side massaged his legs. The sage fell as-
<>leep. Then Brahma1 came there and Dharmavrata1
<>attended on him and worshipped him. Mari1ci, when he
<>woke up, finding Dharmavrata1 attending on another
<>man cursed her and made her into stone. Dharmavrata1
<>pleaded not guilty and standing inside a fire-pit did pen-
<>ance for ten thousand years. Brahma1 and other devas
<>appeared before her and consoled her by assuring that
<>though she would be a stone devas would take their
<>abode in it and that she would be worshipped by all.
<>From that day onwards that stone was known as Devas4ila1
<>and even now it is believed that Brahma1 and other
<>devas live therein. (Chapter 114, Agni Pura1n2a).

<NI>7) {%Dharma and Ka1la.%} There is a misunderstanding found
<>even in some Pura1n2as that Ka1la, the chief of Ka1lapuri1,
<>and Dharma are one and the same person. But if the
<>stories around each are examined it is easy to deduce
<>that they are two different devas. The father and
<>mother of Dharmadeva is Brahma1. The father of Ka1la
<>is Su1rya and mother Sam3jn5a1, daughter of Vis4vakarma1.
<>This itself is a sufficient evidence to show that the two
<>are different persons. Further, Ka1la or Yama is the
<>sixth descendant of Vis2n2u.
<NI>But scholars are misled to think that the two are iden-
<>tical. There is a reason for it.
<Poem>Dharmara1jah2 Pitr2patih2
<>Samavartti1 Paretara1t2 /
<>Kr2ta1nto Yamuna1bhra1ta1
<>S4amano Yamara1d2 Yamah2 //
<>Ka1lo Dan2d2adharah2 Sra1ddha-
<>Devo Vaivasvatontakah2. / (Amara).</Poem>
<NI>The above are the synonyms of Ka1la. Vya1sa has used as
<>synonyms for Dharmadeva in the Maha1bha1rata the
<>words Dharmara1ja, Vr2s2a and Yama. Now among the
<>synonyms for the two there are two words in common--
<>Dharmara1ja and Yama. This has led to this misunder-
<>standing. Because Ka1la weighs the evil and good in
<>man he got the name Dharmara1ja. Dharmadeva got
<>that name because he is the incarnation of Dharma.
<>The real name of Ka1la is Yama. Dharmadeva got the
<>name Yama because he possesses ‘Yama’ (control of
<>the self for moral conduct). Ka1la has no sons; Vidura
<>and Yudhis2t2hira are the sons of Dharmadeva.
